it to all fellow-sufferers. —Yours truly,

A GRAND LITERARY INSTITUTION.
Cole’s Book Arcade now runs from the centre
of the block in Bourke-street to the centre of the
block in Collins-street, Melb., a distance of G6oft.,
and contains the following 17 departments, mostly
literary and artistic: 1, New Books; 2, Second-
hand Books ; 3, Stationery ; 4, Music ; 5, Fancy
Goods ; 6, Toys ; 7, Perfumery; 8, Pictures ; 9,

Gallery ; 15, a Bargain Room ; 16, a Tea Room ;
17, a splendidly arranged new Wholesale Book
Department, containing half-a-million books. The
main walks of the establishment are half-a-mile
long.*

Dear Bulletin, — The risks of the soldier,
sailor, fireman, are taken in the public eye, and
their bravery is much advertised, but the average
man knows little of the courage displayed by
many a lonely prospector, without companionship,
borne up by no feverish excitement. Yet the man
“ on his own ” who tries his luck on the goldfields,
and sets out with swag and feed and water-bag to
face the unknown, has need of more than ordinary
pluck in his equipment. Along the Coolgardie-
Menzies track, every few miles, you come on a
little mound marking the spot where some poor
devil fell from exhaustion or died of thirst. On
one such grave is placed the pipe and pannikin of
the dead man—no doubt these were his two best
friends in his travels. On another is a New Tes-
tament, belonging to one Petersen, the fly-leaf of
which is covered with pious inscriptions in differ-
ent hands, such as : “Pray for him,” “May his
soul rest in peace,” “Send word along to his
friends,” “ God be merciful to him,” “ Send up a
prayer for him,” &c., &c., showing that the
traveller out here has a kind as well as stout
heart. I have known men to stop in their tramp
and cut down saplings to fence in the grave of an
unknown wanderer.— Watersag.

When Father Comes Home From
The Lodge.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When father comes home from the lodge,
O ! then you should hear mother dodge
In fifty directions,
Like plum-pudding sections
At sight of a famishing Hodge !
He’s amorous, clamorous —rather !
He bursts in the door with, “ Is Ma there ?
Hie 1 Love, it was prime !
We had sushjolly time ! ”
Says our rollicking, reckless old father.
“ There was Jones shang * Two Lovely Black
Eyes ! ’
(He’sh a good f’l’a, Jonesh, and no flies !)
And Mellor—he’sh Master-
Yelled, “ Chorush, and faster! ”
(Old charmer, we made the roof rise !)
Then Hockings holdsh on by the table
And standsh up—as much as he’sh able—
And bangs for a toast —
‘ The Ladiesh—our boast!
Hiphooraysh !’. . . Well—they talk about Babel!
“ And Mellor says, ‘ Brother J. Brown,
Chair callsh you reshpond—honor town ! ’ ”
“ What, you make a speech ! ”
“ Me, yes ! how you screech !
They all shpeak —why should I shtay down ?
So I saysh, ‘ I’ve heard brethren to-night
Talk of Nana, that beautiful sight;
But I didn’t go :
’Caush why ? Well, you know,
I’ve a Nana at home—she’sh all right! ”
You said that? You —you !” “Now, my
lovesh,
My darling, my angel, my dovesh 1
When I compliment her
She shouldn’t loseshtemper—
Besides, I know who’sh got boxsh glovesh !
And, Lyddy, though—here [—Brother Farmer
Used cater for Queen—’sif I’d harm her !
There’s one thing, I shwear,
That I couldn’t find there—
That’s a Irish from my lovely old charmer ! ”
And Jimmy and I hear it all,
With ears glued close up to the wall—
The talk and the scuffle
The bricks hardly muffle—
And wish we were grown up quite tall;
And yearn for the time to come—rather !
When we will burst in with, “ Is ma there ?
Hie ! Love, it wash prime !
We had sushjolly time ! ”
Like our merry, Masonic old father !
BRIGHAM BROWN, M.D.

CHANGE OF LIFE,
DIZZINESS AND SHORTNESS OF
BREATH CURED.
What Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills will do for
women at the change of life is shown in a case
reported in the Dewsbury (Eng.) Chronicle.
It is that of Mrs Benjamin Smith who lives
at Hanover-street, Dewsbury. The change
of life had left her weak and miserable;
with constant pains, dizziness, and shortness of
breath on the slightest exertion, together with
chronic rheumatism in the head and eyes. The
pain in her head was too acute for words ; she got
no sleep either by day or night, and her agony
was at times so great that it took two people to
hold her in bed. A skilful doctor was called in,
but did no good. “My cheeks would swell up as
big as saucers,” says Mrs. Smith ; “my eyes were
red as fire, and there was no getting rid of the
pains in the head. I also suffered from a sluggish
liver and a weak heart, and at times my eyesight
was so bad that objects before me seemed but dim
shadows, and there were none of my friends who
looked for my restoration to health.
_ “ I saw an account in a newspaper of a cure
similar to mine through the use of Dr. Williams’
Pink Pills, and my husband urged me to try them.
Before two bottles were used the pains had dis-
appeared as if by magic, and the continued use of
the pills for a little more than a month has left me
enjoying as good health as ever I had in Iny life,
I consider Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills a blessing* and
as I know there are thousands Of \Vdmeil who suffer
in silence from troubles having the same origin
as mine* I aha grateful enough for what they have
dorte for me to tell my story in the hope that some
Suffering sister may be similarly helped, and you
have full permission to publish what I have told
you, which statements can be verified bV any of
my neighbors.” This wonderful remedy is offered
to the public with a full confidence that there is
no disease arising from a watery Or impoverished
condition of the blood, Or shattered herVbhS forceS;
which it will not cure if the directions A,re faith-
fully followed And the treatment persisted in. Dr.
Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale People are not a
patent medicine, but a thoroughly scientific pre-
paration, the result of years of careful study on
the part of an eminent Edinburgh University
physician, and they were successfully used by him
m his everyday practice for years before being of-
fered for general sale. They positively cure Rheu-
matism, Sciatica, Lumbago, and Neuralgia, and
are a specific for all diseases of women, such as
anaemia, poor and watery blood, female irregulari-
ties, nervous headache and hysteria. They are
not a purgative medicine, but brace up and per-

OLDFIELD (Member of “Toe Dental Board of Victoria.'
Dear Bulletin, —So Dr. Cobb “stands up
for the crow,” does he? Well, Dr. Cobb can’t
know the crow from a crow—the crow Bill and
I know, anyway. Bill could tell you all about
crows, but as most likely he’s engaged just now
losing himself and his empty water-bag on some
parched-up plain; or is busy writing an ode to
me, his old mate Jim, whose death he’s probably
just heard reported again (that’d make it 29
times); I feel it my sacred and solemn duty
to let my spite out on Mr. Crow. In my
option there is no more vicious fiend on
Orod s beautiful earth than our own Austra-
lian crow. Eaglehawks and dingoes aren’t a
patch on him for cruelty, cunning, and destruc-
t* v ® neas - No, I haven’t seen crows pick eyes out
of half-dead people, but I know they’d do it if
they had half a chance. I’ve tried them, and you
can try them if you like, by lying still for a few
minutes under a tree. Mr. Crow soon spots you,
and perches on a branch above you. There, cun-
ningly turning his head from side to side, like a
Earrot in a cage listening to your “ pretty cocky,”
e watches you a while, and gradually comes
lower, and at last flies on to the ground some little
distance away. With his wings half-open and
nervously twitching, ready to fly in case
of danger, he comes closer and closer with a
sort of side-shuffle, watching you carefully all
the time with his fiendish white eyes. If you
keep quiet, he’ll have a peck at your cloth, or, if
still doubtful (they’re awful cowards), fly up to a
branch again and call for his mates. They’ll soon
arrive, and all have a try to find out, before
tackling you, whether you’re helpless enough to
stand pecking at. Some will drop small sticks on
you, and, if that doesn’t move you, dirt will
follow, and, if you don’t get up then—well,
I got up then. Last summer, I found a corpse
(victim of the heat-wave) out in the “ dry corner.”
The body was literally covered with crows,
though the poor fellow couldn’t have been dead
more than an hour or two. Yet both his eyes
were picked out and his brain pulled through the
sockets by the black fiends. His nose was also
gone, and his mouth a gory, torn-about gap, minus
the tongue. It was a ghastly sight, and, to
revenge the poor fellow, I started to pelt sticks
at the crows, who were perched on an adjacent
tree calmly awaiting my departure. They must
have been at that man before he was dead, judg-
ing from the work done. But crows don’t often
banquet On human eyes. Weak ewes, lambs, bogged
sheep, cattle, and horses are their general prey.
Has Dr. Cobb ever seen a swarm of his black pets
wait for a ewe lambing? It’s a pitiful sight to
see the often hopelessly-weak mother trying to
save her offspring. At lambing time paddocks
are full of blind ewes and lambs staggering about
followed by crows. Pastoralists lose thousands of
their sheep yearly by the beaks of these black
wretches. If a sheep or cow gets bogged or
caught in a wire-fence the black devils pounce
upon them and have their eyes out long before
they are dead; some live days after. Has
Dr. Cobb ever watched a crow take the
young ones out of a galah’s or other
bird’s nest, while the parents anxiously fluttered
around hopelessly attempting to save their brood ?
Every bird, every animal, and every man worth
the name in the bush, bitterly hates the crow, and
has a cut at him whenever possible. But stop—
there’s one good point about the crow. When lost
in the bush you can always depend upon finding
water by following his flight at midday or sun-
down ; or by making to where you hear his mono-
tonous “Go an’ die.” But, then, if the wretches
only knew, they’d most certainly lead you away
from water for the sake of your eyes.—6 xB.

NEW SOUTH WALES GOVERNMENT RAILWAYS.
CHRISTMAS & NEW YEAR HOLIDAYS
TO TIEUE
BLUB OR SNOWY MOUNTAINS.
Alpiije Ranjbtes. WoQderfOl - - -
- - - Seaside Pleasures. Oceao Breezes.
Boating 1 , Bathing, Fishing, Picnics, &c.
The pleasures of an Australian holiday have
become largely enhanced through the facilities
afforded by the railways to the dweller in the
crowded, dusty city or wide-spreading, arid
plains of the interior to escape to the cool, re-
freshing atmosphere of the mountain range, in
the one case, or the delicious breezes of the sea-
side in the other, where, forgetting the toil and
worry of business life, he obtains the change of
scene so necessary to the recuperation of his
physical and mental powers. To those engaged
in the routine of country labor there can be no
greater enjoyment than that afforded by a holi-
day trip to one of the beautiful seaside resorts
in which New South Wales is so rich ; and the
opportunity furnished by the summer holiday
season for enjoying this pleasure causes the
approach of Christmas and the New Year to be
hailed with delight by the sun-browned bush-
man, who possesses a wide choice of picturesque
localities in which to pass his holiday hours.
mm
:
■
sm
WEEPING ROCK, WENTWORTH FALLS, BLUE MOUNTAINS.
Then there are the Blue Mountains, a realm
of shady glens, umbrageous recesses, rippling
streams, silvery cascades, and al\ the myriad
charms with which Nature enchan ts the lovers
of the beautiful. Katoomba and Wentworth
Falls, the stupendous Valley of the Grose, and
the thousand and one natural marvels, have
been brought by the railway within the reach
of the holiday-maker. Again, there are the
Jenolan Caves, the great subterranean wonder-
world, which are easily reached from Katoomba,
Mount Victoria, or Tarana.
* * * *
North of the Metropolis is the Hawkesbury,
perhaps the most beautiful river in Australia,
with its extensive reaches, unsurpassed scenery,
and ever-changing views of lofty hill and luxu-
riant valley. Beyond this stream is the exten-
sive lake region of which Brisbane Water, the
Tuggerah Lakes, and Lake Macquarie are the
principal features. Here the lovers of boating
and fishing may enjoy themselves to their
The Southern tourist district, between Mittagong and Goulburn, is, in irany
places, a land of enchantment. Here are the celebrated Fitzroy and Meryla
halls, the scenic glories of Mittagong, Bundanoon and other favorite halting-
places, with even grander landscapes going beyond. From Goulburn enjoyable
trips r ay be made to the Wombeyan Oaves, or to Lake Bathurst and Lake
George, the latter being a notable sportsman’s resort
♦ * *' * ♦ * * *
From Cooma may be reached the Yarrangobilly Oaves, perhaps the most
wonderful, as they are the most extensive, of any in Australia; also the
Snowy Mountains, where, on the summit of Mount Kosciusko, and surrounded
by the eternal snows, the visitor may enjoy the cooling Alpine breezes during
the warmest season of the year, returning (if so desired) by way of Tumut and
Gundagai to the main southern line at Cootamundra, instead of via Cooma.
heart’s content, while in the surrounding country, there is an endless wealth
of ferns and hush flowers.
* * * * * * * *
Away on the South Coast is the magnificent National Park, a charming
epitome of all that is lovely and impressive in Australian scenery ; and a few
miles beyond the famous Illawarra district, the G-arden of New South Wales,
the manifold beauties of which have irresistibly impressed those who have
visited it. Here, too, are the Bulli and Macquarie Passes, Lake Illawarra,
Clifton, Wollongong, and other sea-side resorts; Kiama, with its great natural
phenomenon, the “Blow Hole”; Nowra, the metropolis of the luxuriant
Shoalhaven district ; Kangaroo Valley; the Cambewarra and other lofty moun-
tain ranges, and other delightfully picturesque localities.

The Australian public
—who are generally under
the impression that their
native tongue is not
understood on board
these magnificent steam-
ers will doubtless be
gratified to learn that
English Stewards are
carried on all steamers,
and that all officers are
acquainted with the
English language. Many
people look at the Over-
land Journey from Mar-
seilles to London with a
sentiment of dread. They
fancy that the trip by
train is tedious, that their
native tongue on a foreign
soil may not be spoken or
understood. All this is
a great mistake. The
overland journey from
Marseilles to London, far
from being long and tedi-
ous, may be accomplished
in 23 hours, as compared
with the six days’ sea trip
by Gibraltar and the Bay
of Biscay. Again, the
passenger who has no
special reason for hurry-
ing through the continent
may leisurely travel over
France during 14 days,
for which time his ticket
is available. He is thus
afforded an opportunity
of visiting Paris, about
which city any praise
would almost be out of
place. It may also be
advisable to add that in
all hotels of any standing
throughout the continent,
and especially in Paris,
English is spoken as. well
as French by all waiters-

ing by which the verse is in-

Special Notice.
The whole contents of this newspaper are copyright by
the publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
article, tale, sketch, poem, paragraph, illustration, or
any other part of the contents of this number, is hereby
expressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and, acknowledge in the
< Correspondence ” column all contributions submitted
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or, say, 3000 words, are specially acceptable),
Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings. All
communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand, that,
we are not responsible for its preservation or transmission.
To Amateurs in JilacJe and, White.
A nyone supplying to The Bulletin original humorous or
other topical drawings in line or wash, of sufficient merit
and interest and adapted for direct reproduction, or any
interesting photograph illustrative of a current event,
will be ■paid for same on acceptance. Any picture con-
taining a good idea, but unfitted for direct reproduc-
tion— i.e., any picture which has to be re-drawn—will be
paid for as a suggestion only. The conditions under
which such artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.

after the arrival of each mail, at the office of the British
Australasian Co., Limited, 31 Fleet Street, E.C. ; Cordon
and, Gotch, Ludgate Circus ; Street & Co., Cornhill.

SATURDAY, DECEMBER 12, 1896.
Song of the Artesian Water,
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Now the stock have started dying, for the Lord
has sent a drought ;
But we’re sick of prayers and Providence—we’re
going to do without;
With the derricks up above us and the solid earth
below,
We are waiting at the lever for the word to let
her go.
Sinking down, deeper down ;
Oh, we’ll sink it deeper down.
As the drill is plugging downward at a thousand
feet of level,
If the Lord won’t send us water, oh, we’ll get it
from the devil;
Yes, we’ll get it from the devil deeper down.
Now, our engine’s built in Glasgow by a very
canny Scot,
And he marked it twenty horse-power, but he
don’t know what is what.
When Canadian Bill is firing with the sun-dried
gidgee logs,
She can equal thirty horses and a score or so of
dogs.
Sinking down, deeper down,
Oh, we’re going deeper down.
If we fail to get the water then it’s ruin to the
squatter,
For the drought is on the station and the weather’s
growing hotter,
But we’re bound to get the water deeper down.
But the shaft has started caving and the sinking’s
very slow,
And the yellow rods are bending in the water
down below,
And the tubes are always jamming and they can’t
be made to shift
Till we nearly burst the ''Agine with a forty horse-
power lift.
Sinking down, deeper down,
Oh, we’re going deeper down.
Though the shaft is always caving, and the tubes
are always jamming,
Yet we’ll fight our way to water while the stub-
born drill is ramming—
While the stubborn drill is ramming deeper down.
But there’s no artesian water, though we’ve passed
three thousand feet.
And the contract price is growing and the boss is
nearly beat.
But it must be down beneath us, and it s down
we’ve got to go,
Though shes bumping on the solid rock four
thousand feet below.
Sinking down, deeper down ;
_Oh, we’re going deeper down,
And it’s time they heard us knocking on the roof
of Satan’s dwellin’;
But we’ll get artesian water if we cave the roof of
hell in—
Oh ! we’ll get artesian water deeper down.
But it’s hark ! the whistle’s blowing with a wild,
exultant blast,
And the boys are madly cheering, for they’ve
struck the flow at last,
And it’s rushing up the tubing from four thousand
feet below
Till it spouts above the casing in a million-gallon
flow.
And it’s down, deeper down—
Oh, it comes from deeper down ;
It is flowing, ever flowing, in a free, unstinted
measure
From the silent hidden places where the old earth
hides her treasure—
Where the old earth hides her treasure deeper
down.
And it’s clear away the timber, and it’s let the
water run :
How it glimmers in the shadow, how it flashes in
the sun !
By the silent belts of timber, by the miles of
blazing’ plain
It is bringing hope and comfort to the thirsty land
again.
Flowing down, further down ;
It is flowing further down
To the tortured thirsty cattle, bringing gladness
in its going;
Through the droughty days of summer it is flow-
ing, ever flowing—
It is flowing, ever flowing, further down.
THE BANJO.
" The Secret of the Sea,"
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
LONGFELLOW.
Ah ! What pleasant visions haunt me
As I gaze upon the sea ;
All the old romantic legends,
All my dreams come hack to me.
“Wouldst thou” —so the helmsman answered—
“ Learn the secret of the sea?
Only those who brave its dangers
Comprehend its mystery 1 ”
Beautiful words ! But do those who brave
its dangers comprehend the “ Secret of the Sea ”?
And, if so, how is it that the shipmen are, one and
all, if not voiceless, yet inarticulate ? It is remark-
able that the sea has not given to the English-
speaking world one great poet, or even a minor
poet of note. It cannot be that the imaginative
faculty is lacking in those “that go down to the
sea in ships,” for none are more fanciful than
sailormen—even now, when board and State
schools and steam and the “ mighty march of
modern progress ” generally have done so much to
exploit the mystery of which poet Longfellow
sings. The grizzled shellbacks of the old wooden
sailing-ship days —of the epoch prior to iron
vessels, double topsail yards, and floating steam
palaces—were often permeated with true poetic
feeling, however rude the expression thereof may
have been. “ How do you contrive to keep awake
on the look-out on fine nights ? ” was asked of a
blasphemous, barnacle-encrusted ancient mariner.
“ I tries to think things out —where the birds’
songs come from, and the flowers’ smell, and what
it is at back of the purple on the hills.” He
pointed to the sun rising in tropic splendor and
to the many-colored glories of the eastern heavens
—“ The sun a-shinin’ on God’s garden flowers ! ”
he explained, with comprehensive wave of tarry
hand.
Once, sailing with light, favoring breeze through
Sunda’s Straits—no lovelier place exists on earth—■
the setting sun, shedding crimson radiance on
land and sea, on the “ mountain wooded to the
peak,” and on the swelling canvas of the ship,
suddenly irradiated a canoe lightly drifting bow-
first on that summer sea to eastward. In it, face
to sky, a dark-skinned man stark and dead, and a
great bird, snow-white, sitting sphinx-like in the
stern gazing fixedly on the face of the dead—
“ Floating to glory and Christ’s archangel a-
steerin’,” was the comment, surely not all un-
imaginative, of the most gnarled old reprobate
aboard. A large number of the “chanties,” or
sailors’ songs, sung 30 years or more ago, were bar-
baric and coarse; others there were, however,
replete with poetry and imagination, full of sug-
gestions of the “ Secret of the Sea,” and chanted
to melodies strange, wild, plaintive, and fascinat-
ing—songs and airs to the originals of which
Ulysses arid “The World’s Desire” may have
listened.
* * * »
But the deep-sea men can no more help
being imaginative than they can the salt drop in
their blood that bids them wander till they die.
For they perforce must mark Ocean’s occult moods
and tenses and something understand of the mute
hints that sunrises and sunsets —no two ever alike
—give of eternity and that greater secret of exist-
ence in which the lesser secret of the sea is merged.
Something, too, they must gather of the mystery
of gales and calms; they near, as no landsman
hears, the voices of the night, the weird tongues
that whisper in the ratlings or shriek among the
shrouds and stays. They see, as not even the
desert Arabs see, the mighty marshalling array
and splendor of the stars. They know there is
no real but only an apparent sameness in the com-
ings and the goings of the sea. To the landsman
all seas are merely sea, all skies but sky. To the
initiate each ocean has its own distinctive features,
habits and customs-its own particular color,
movement, length, and rhythmical sweep of wave,
and its own distinctive skies to arch above and
meet it at horizon’s edge; so that an old salt—
could he be hypnotised and so carried to any sea
he had sailed, and then aroused—would surely,
within a little, recognise latitude and longitude.
For the skilled seamen, the trackers of the waters,
sees signs and tokens to others invisible—ay ! and
dream dreams no shoredweller dreams. And in
these faculties is something of the “ Secret of the
Sea.”
* * * »
That seafaring men are imaginative is
evidenced also by their legend and tradition. The
race that evolved the story of the “ Flying Dutch-
man ” had surely ideality. And, cn passant, it
may be remarked that the occasional appearance
in the region of the Cape of Good Hope of an old-
time vessel carrying a press of canvas, while the
vessels which encounter her are reefed down, is as
well authenticated as is the existence of the sea-
serpent, in which phenomenon the present pen-
man admits profound belief. Mention of the Fly-
ing Dutchman recalls an incident which came
under the writer’s personal cognizance and which,
as it never received the slightest elucidation, may
rightly be regarded as a secret of the sea :
In November, 1859, the barque Marion, bound
from London to Hongkong and three days out
from the Cape of Good Hope, was chased—no
other word describes it—by a large red light, pre-
cisely similar in color to, but about twice the size
of, a ship’s port sidelight, and as high out of the
water as the sidelight of a vessel of the calibre of
the Great Eastern. The light, straight ahead and
coming down with great speed, was first seen
about three bells in the second dog-watch—the
night somewhat hazy, wind moderate and fitful
moon. When the helm was ported the light sud-
denly disappeared, but presently came rushing up
on the port quarter, vanished, came up, first on
one, then on the other beam and, a few moments
later, was right ahead again. After nigh crazing
the ship’s company for an hour or over it came no
more. An experience so disconcerting naturally
stimulated all there was of imaginative faculty
whether before or abaft the mast, and much
horrific legend resulted—one, by an old foremast
hand, ran as follows: “I was A.B. aboard the
Bombay, and was goin’ forrnd, bein’ relieved from
the wheel just arter eight bells o’ the first night-
watch—dark, sloppy, greasy night it were,
drizzlin’, not much wind. Watch all stowed
away under lee of fo’castle. Just as I comes abaft
the forehatch I sees someone a-sittin’ on the wet
hatch. ‘ Hullo ! ’ I says, ‘ who’s that V It never
spoke—only, as I comes abreast of it, it turns its
head and looks me straight in the eyes. It were a
dead face, but all lit up with a pale light ! It
weren’t the face of anybody as belonged to that
hooker l All hands and the old man saw that face
one time or another durin’ the passage.”
A narration by an officer was of equally uncom-
fortable a nature: “ When a midshipman in one
of Green’s ships, then three months from port, he
was sent aloft one night to loose a main topgallant
sail. As he was loosing the bunt a man whom he
did not know crept towards him from the weather
yard-arm. The narrator jumped for the backstay
and slid to deck. Two men were sent aloft to
interview the man on the yard, who was plainly
visible from the deck —the idea being either that it
was a practical joke or that a man was walking in
his sleep. The two men slid down as rapidly as
the first, declaring the visitant did not belong to
the ship. The captain was called, all hands were
mustered and found present. Captain and chief
mate then went aloft and attempted to capture
the intruder who, with a loud scream, apparently
threw himself from the yard-arm into the sea;
but no one heard or saw him strike the water.”
* * * *
Perhaps the most imaginative of British-
speaking seamen are Welshmen. The writer, as
a boy, and for his sins, served under a Welsh chief
mate who, on night watches, would invite the
former on the poop, ostensibly for nautical instruc-
tion and to arouse in his young breast the ambition
to become a great sea-captain ; but, really, to
freeze his youthful marrow by the narration of
virulent nautical ghost-stories (and thus induce
preternatural wakefulness and watchfulness in the
junior) while he (the mate) subsequently snored
upon a hencoop. He was more tolerable even
snoring than as a romancist.
One night, in the North Atlantic, the mate
soothed himself to slumber by a ghostly narrative
more than usually ghastly. The man at the
wheel, encouraged by the quietude, probably
dozed; the look-out certainly slept; the writer
meditated spook generally and the Cambrian
variety thereof particularly—with his eyes closed.
Of a sudden there was a rushing as of mighty
wings. Shaving our taffrail, standing west under
a cloud of canvas—we were heading northward,
homeward bound—was a great American-built
ship, whence sprung in that lonely but moonlit
ocean Heaven knows ! “ Ballarat, ahoy ! ” —she
was close enough to read the name blazoned in
gold upon our stern—“ Ballarat, ahoy ! Are ye
all asleep, Britisher ? ” came the cheery hail borne
clear upon the breeze. There followed a rippling
laugh, a woman’s laugh, and the moonbeams
glanced and danced upon the fair hair of a girl
standing on the topgallant rail of the strange ship
and holding to the rigging, lightly swaying to and
fro. “ Ha ! ha ! ha ! Wake up ; Good-night,
Britisher ! God bless you ! GOOD-NIGHT !
Good-night ! Good-night! ” And the stranger
that had brought us benison, having a strong fair
wind and keeping away a couple of points, swept
onwards into the night and, the moon clouding
over, was lost to our ken for evermore. Good
night! Good night! And for us, who had
not heard voice from the outside, or looked on the
face of woman for months, was there naught of
poetry in such a meeting, nothing of the mystery
and the secret of the sea and the mystery of life,
in that girl’s sweet, sudden benediction, flung
wide and free on the waters to those she had
never seen before, and was, mayhap, to meet
nevermore on earth or sea ?
* * * *
Quaint, imaginative, and tinged with the
mystery of the sea were the views held by old-
time sailors regarding birds. The stormy petrel,
or “Mother Carey’s chicken,” was, for instance
looked upon as robin-redbreast of the waters, and
was safe from gunshot and decoy-line; for it carried
over the crested wave the souls of departed
shipmasters. Nor did sailors hold with killing
the cruel and greedy albatross, as it, they thought,
possessed occult power to wreck ships and drown
men. But the shore birds blown to sea they
would capture, feed, fondle, and set free within
sight of land ; for they carried the souls of little
children that lay beneath the green turf in quiet
churchyards. And here an incident might perhaps
furnish a theme for the singer of the sea that is to
come : The writer, going aloft leisurely at sunset
—it was off the Gulf of Guinea—chanced to look
down. On the far side of the slowly-moving ship
there floated, face-upwards, a little, white-robed
child gay with ’broidery and pink ribbons—gay,
but dead ; its tiny face round and with the curves
of health ; its arms outstretched as if in mute
appeal to its mother. God knows who mourned
it! God knows from whose tendei but heedless
embrace and breast it had slipped to oblivion on
the broader bosom of the sea ! It may, falling
from some ship’s deck, have sunk, and, after,
risen.
Something stirred in the clear water below. A
fathom or less beneath the child, convoying it,
fluttered, watching and waiting—appalling, black,
and spectral—a square acre or more of vast, flat,
quadrangular, beaked fish. Scarcely they seemed
to progress, only, now and again, one would
change position with another. And so the little
one, shadowed by its awesome attendants, went
drifting away on unknown currents to unknown
coasts ; maybe to lie at last in some pearl-lined
cavern of the sea, or to be washed on the shore
where is the Garden of the Hesperides, there to
rest until the sea gives up its Secret and its Dead.
* * * *
Not least among the secrets of the sea is
the fascination it exerts on those who have once
succumbed to its influence. The shipman, railing
ever at his mistress the sea, yet loves her in any
of her moods. Vowing to forsake her finally when
once his foot touches shore, he nevertheless re-
turns to her again and again, when she calls to
him from the far inland meadows, from the bush,
the mine, or from “ the vision splendid of the sun-
lit plains.” The real sailor, the born seaman, may
drift away from, but sooner or later almost inevit-
ably drifts back to, the unquiet bosom of his first
love. He may put half a continent between ocean
and himself, but some day there comes “so loud
a calling of the sea ” that the old chest is rum-
maged from the lumber-room and ocean gets her
own again. There was one, kinsman and once
shipmate of the writer, to whom the sea had been
unkind. He left it to find wealth, wife, and posi-
tion on dry land. The years, passed. There came
a summer season when—business anchoring him
to the grim city so that he could not seek the sea
for even a few hours—he spent every available
minute in the little steamers plying between
bridges, making believe very hard that yellow
Thames was cerulean Indian Ocean.
“ But,” said one, “you hate the sea? ”
“ Hate it! I love it! The temptation to go back
to it altogether—to its freedom, to the excitement
of wild weather, the charm of hot days under the
line, when the scented pitch bubbles from the
planks, and when one sinks to sleep on snowy
decks with coil of rope for pillow, and, fanned by
bellying mainsail, wakes to sec the sun come up,
splendid ; wakes strong, fresh, alive —I say the
temptation to cast everything aside and go back
is almost too much for me ! ”
And at last he went, leaving wife, wealth, and
duty, to double stormy capes and headlands, and
find a sailor’s death on the sea that will not be
denied.
* * * •
And he who writes ? Whenever there
comes to him the scent of sweetbriar his thoughts,
vaulting bygone years, fly to a certain day on
Eastern tropio sea—the most beautiful day he
shall ever see on earth. Then, in fancy, he sees
once more an ocean divine in color and purity;
sees the glittering flight of myriad flying-fish, the
glory of the nautilus, the sheen of albicore and
bonito darting under keel, and the many-tinted
ever-flitting sails of proas. He hears again the
music of splashing oars and sailors’ songs sung far
away, while from the hills, lying purple and dis-
tant yet lustrous under the effulgent sun, are
wafted the odors of briar, of rose, and of sweet-
smelling spices. And to him also the old longing
comes back. And then ? And then a voice,
tender and true, which says with Ovid, though
not in the Latin tongue:
“ Quid tibi cum pclago ? Terra contentus fuisses."
Sydney.
J. EVISON.
Bill and Jim Fall Out.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Bill and Jim are mates no longer—they would
scorn the name of mate —
Those two bushmen hate each other with a soul-
consuming hate;
Yet erstwhile they were as brothers should be
(tho’ they never will) :
Ne’er were mates to one another half so true as
Jim and Bill.
Bill was one of those who have to argue every day
or die —
Tho’, of course, he swore ’twas Jim who always
itched to argufy.
They would, on most abstract subjects, contradict
each other flat
And at times in lurid language—they were mates
in spite of that.
Bill believed the Bible story re the origin of him—
He was sober, he was steady, he was orthodox;
while Jim,
Who, we grieve to state, was always getting into
drunken scrapes,
Held that man degenerated from degenerated
apes.
Bill was British to the backbone, he was loyal
through and through ;
Jim declared that Blucher’s Prussians won the
fight at Waterloo,
And he hoped the color’d races would in time wipe
out the white —
And it rather strained their mateship, but it
didn’t burst it quite.
They battled round in Maoriland —they saw it
through and through—
And argued on the rata, what it was and how it
grew ;
Bill believed the vine grew downward, Jim de-
clared that it grew up —
Yet they always shared their fortunes to the final
bite and sup.
Night after night they argued how the kangaroo
was born,
And each one held the other’s stupid theories in
scorn.
Bill believed it was “ born inside,” Jim declared it
was born out —
Each as to his own opinions never had the slight-
est doubt.
They left the earth to argue and they went among
the stars.
Re conditions atmospheric, Bill believed “ the hair
of Mars
Was too thin for human bein’s to exist in mortal
states. ”
Jim declared it was too thick; if anything—yet
they were mates.
Bill, for Freetrade; Jim, Protection; argued as to
which was best
For the welfare of the workers—and their mate-
ship stood the test !
They argued over what they meant and didn’t
mean at all,
And what they said and didn’t —and were mates
in spite of all.
Till one night the two together tried to light a fire
in camp
When they had a leaky billy and the wood was
scarce and damp.
And —no matter —let the moral be distinctly
understood :
One alone should tend the fire, while the other
brings the wood.
HENRY LAWSON.
Many of the old familiar goldfield-ladies’
nick-names have re - appeared (in some cases
attached to their originals) on the Westralian
fields: “The Faded Lily,” “Cold and Wet.,/
“The Old Gray Mare,” “ The Red Flag,” “ The
Long-handled Shovel,” “ Old Fracture,” “ Feather
Legs,” “ The Silver Grill.” A Bulletin corres-
pondent deeply regrets to hear nothing, so far, of
“The Murrumbidgee Barge,” “The Speckled
Hen,” “Mrs. Coppernose,” “Gentle Annie,”
“Port-wine Liz,” “ The Sudden Jerk,” and “The
Bull Pup.” But, perhaps, after working their
way from old Tambaroora to Cape River and the
Palmer, they have gone to Heaven— via Wyalong.


O'Toole and M'Sharry.
(A LACHLAN IDYLL.)
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Fn the valley of the Lachlan, where the perfume
from the pines
Fills the glowing summer air like incense
spreading;
Where the silent flowing river like a bar of silver
shines
When the winter moon its pallid beams is
shedding ;
Fn a hut on a selection, near a still and silent pool,
Lived two mates, who used to shear and fence
and .carry ;
The one was known, both near and far, as Dandy
Dari O’Toole,
And the other as Cornelius M'Sharry.
And they’d share each other’s blankets, and each
other’s horses ride,
And go off together shearing in the summer ;
They would canter on from sunrise to the gloam-
ing, side by side,
While M'Sharry rode the Barb and Dan the
Drummer,
And the boys along the Lachlan recognised it as a
rule
From Kugowra to the plains of Wanandarry,
That if ever love was stronger than M‘Sharry’s
for O’Toole
’Twas tjhe love O’Toole extended to M'Sharry.
And their love might have continued and been
constant to the end,
And they might have still been affable and jolly,
But they halted at a shanty where the river takes
a bend,
And were waited on by Doolan’s daughter,
Polly.
Now, this pretty Polly Doolan was so natty, neat
and cool,
And so pleasant, that they both agreed to tarry,
For she winked her dexter eye-lid at susceptible
O’Toole,
While she slyly winked the other—at M'Sharry.
So they drank her health in bumpers till the rising
of the moon,
And. she had them both in bondage so com-
pletely .
That each time they talked of going she said,
“ Must you go so soon ? ”
And they couldn’t go. She smiled at them so
sweetly.
Dan O’Toole grew sentimental and M Sharry
played the fool,
Though they each had sworn an oath they d
never marry,
Yet the self-same dart from Cupid’s bow that
vanquished Dan O’Toole
Had gone through the heart of honest Con
M'Sharry.
Then M'Sharry thought, “If Dandy Dan got
drunk and went to bed
He (M'Sharry) could indulge his little folly,”
And Dan thought if M'Sharry once in drunken
sleep lay spread,
He Could have a little flirt with pretty Polly ;
So they kept the bottle going till they both were
pretty full,
And yet each rival seemed inclined to tarry ;
The precise amount of pain-killer it took to fill
O’Toole
Was required to close the optics of M'Sharry.
So the rivals lost their tempers, and they called
each other names,
And disturbed the Doolan children from their
pillows,
And then Doolan came and told them that he
wouldn’t have such games,
Thqy must go and fight it out beneath the
willows.
So they went beneath the willows, near a deep
and shady pool,
With as much ’aside as each of them could
carry,
And M'Sharry started thumping the proboscis of
O’Toole,
And O’Toole retaliated on M'Sharry.
And they fought till they were winded, and yet
neither had the best,
Though from each of them the blood was freely
flowing;
And they paused at last to breathe awhile and take
a moment’s rest,
But O’Toole’s two -yes with rage were fairly
glowing ;
Then without a moment’s warning he charged for-
ward like a bull,
And before poor Con had time to run or parry,
With a terrible momentum the big head of Dan
O’Toole
Went, bump ! into the stomach of M'Sharry.
And the force of the concussion laid M'Sharry out
quite still,
With his feet above his head among the bushes,
While O'Toole, with the momentum, cannoned
madly down the hill,
And fell plump in the lagoon among the rushes.
Like a weedy river-god he climbed the far side of
the pool,
And he did not for one single moment tarry,
For the curse of Cain was in the brain of Dandy
Dan O’Toole,
Who felt certain he had settled poor M'Sharry.
Now, while Dan O’Toole was stealing through the
still and silent night,
And his aching brain with pain-killer was
throbbing,
M'Sharry lay and listened, till his heart stood
still with fright,
And he eased his guilty soul with silent sobbing.
For he heard his boon-companion falling headlong
in the pool,
And he thought he was as dead as poor old
Harry,
And M'Sharry mourned the drowning of poor
Dandy Dan O’Toole,
While O’Toole was sadly weeping for M Sharry.
And the valley of the Lachlan never more will
know the men, .
That were once so loving, frolicsome, and frisky,
For O’Toole cleared out to Queensland and was
never seen again,
While M'Sharry started south and took to
whisky.
And M'Sharry, in his nightmare, often sees that
fatal pool,
And the pricks of guilty conscience tries to
parry ,
While away among the backblocks wanders Dandy
Dan O’Toole,
Always flying from the ghost of Con M Sharry.
THOMAS E. SPENCER.
The Last of His Tribe.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
It was years ago in new country on a Northern
Territory creek. We had struck the creek the
fight bJfore, and at daylight began following it
down. By noon things began to look rather
doubtful. The creek continued a dry, shallow
watercourse, bordered with polygonum ; on either
side limitless downs : there seemed no change m it,
nor likely to be any. The horses were getting to
look very tucked-up, and there was no chance ot
going back. However, soon after noon we flushed
I bronze-wing pigeon, and this gave us h°pe, for
bronze-wing pigeons drink regularly every evening
and gradually make their way into the watering-
places by short flights during the afternoon.
There were two of us and a black boy. Holden,
my mate, who had ridden up the downs a little
way, suddenly whistled to me, and when I rode to
join him he pointed ahead. , . , , , ,
“There’s either a lagoon or a big blue-bush
flat,” he said. . , j
Certainly, a mile or so away the creek broad-
ened out into what might as easily have been one
as the other. By means of field-glasses we made
it out to be water, and returned to the creek
much relieved. The wind was away from us an
the horses slouched listlessly on in fact, we had
been in full sight of the lagoon some time before
they awoke to the fact that there was a fine water-
hole right under their noses. Both horses and
cattle when thirsty go entirely by smell. Lhey
will walk past water should the wind be from
the wrong direction, while, if it blows from water
—or even damp earth —two or three miles away,
you can’t hold them.
It was still a good lagoon, although the poly-
gonum ring showed that, when full, it was a fine
sheet of water. There is no better, cleaner camp
than the short sward that grows on the sloping
bank of an inland lagoon as the water recedes.
Holden and I were soon enjoying a lazy smoke
and congratulating ourselves on our luck, whilst
our contented horses grazed around, when my
attention was drawn to an object moving on the
opposite bank. Holden’s attention was occupied
just then with a fine pair of wood-ducks, which he
reckoned would make a good supper; the black
boy, of course, had gone to sleep. Something
black was crawling along the water’s edge on the
opposite bank. A gidyea scrub ran down just
there, so that the figure was less conspicuous.
Without speaking to Holden, I got the glasses and
had a good look.
A nigger of some sort was feebly crawling
along the bank, every now and again thrust-
ing one arm deep in the water. I handed the
glasses to Holden and told him to look. He was
surprised. The idea of there being any blacks
about had never occurred to either of us, as for
miles and miles we had seen neither tracks nor
smoke. .
“It’s an old gin,” he said at last, getting
mussels out of the muddy bank. There’s some-
thing moving in the scrub behind —a native dog.
Let’s go round and interview her.”
I took a piece of damper, and Holden took the
shot-gun for thebenefit of the aforesaid ducks. It
was a long walk round, and when we came to the
spot we found there an old; attenuated skeleton of
a gin feebly groping for mussels, of which she had
succeeded in gathering about two-thirds of a dilly-
bag. She was almost blind, and was accompanied
by two gaunt dingoes, which bared their fangs at
us in that evil grin that makes every bushinan
hate a wild dog. The old gin did not recognise
us as whites with her rheumy eyes, but seemed
incensed at our presence, and apparently tried to
set on to us the dogs, which were only restrained
by their instinctive fear of a white. I offered her
the damper, but she struck it feebly out of my
hand, and the dogs fell on and fought for it.
We looked around for further tracks, but
could find none ; and, as we could do nothing more
then, we strolled back to camp, Holden on the
way bagging the ducks.
Next morning the old lady was at her mussel-
fishing again, and Holden proposed that we should
take round some of the duck and some fish we had
caught. We took Bob, the black-boy, with us,
thinking that perhaps she might recognise and
take the food from him.
It was a worse failure than before, as the dogs
went for Bob at once and nearly got themselves
killed for it. She refused the food, and the hungry
dogs got it again.
It was so good a camp in every respect that
we proposed staying there for some days for the
horses’ sake; but the gin was such an un-
sociable old lady that we let her alone, although
she was at her mussel-fishing every day with her
two jackal-like attendant's. Once, when the wind
was blowing across the lagoon, I thought I could
smell burning gidyea, but was not sure. Of course
the poor old wretch had a camp somewhere where
she cooked and ate her shellfish, but as she did not
want us, we did not call.
One strange thing we noticed. At one end of
the lagoon was a native camp some two years old
or more. It was not ‘very large, but, barring
the old gin, it was the latest indication of natives
we discovered. On the morning preceding our
departure we were eating breakfast when Bob ex-
claimed, “Look!—old gin dead!” He pointed
across, and with the glasses I too saw a black arm
poked stiffly out of the water.
When we got round we found that the old
creature had slipped in, and, although she had
hung on to a branch, she had been too feeble to re-
cover, and had been drowned. We got her out and
put her on the bank, just as the dogs came up,
with their everlasting grin.
Holden looked at me and took out his revolver.
He was one of the few good pistol-shots I have
ever met. There were two shots and the brutes
were dead. We both hated them. We covered
the old gin with boughs and returned to our
camp but not to our breakfast.
That night we were awakened by a noise like
sobbing from across the lagoon.
Bob woke up and heard it too. It came from
the direction of the dead gin, and he made one
frightened bound on to his legs; we had to kick
him half-way to the lagoon before we could make
him understand that we didn’t want him. Then
we sat and listened. In the deep silence of the
windless night a strange noise was certainly com-
ing from where the gin’s body lay. It was a croon-
ing, wailing noise, very eerie in that place and at
that time. “ Let’s take a couple of candles and go
round,’’ suggested Holden. Bob followed us ; he
was too frightened to stop alone.
We knew the way pretty well, and, when we got
near the place and the location of the strange
noise, we lit our candles and investigated. A
strange sight we saw. An old blackfellow with
white hair and beard, as shrunken as the dead
gin—and until you have seen an old black gin in
the starvation districts, you cannot conceive how
attenuated a human being can become lay
moaning near the bodies of the old woman and
the dogs. He was sightless, and we presently
found that he was crippled, and could only drag
himself along the ground. We gave him a drink
from the lagoon, but he was evidently dying, and
when we came back in the morning he was dead.
We buried him, the gin, and the two dogs m a
shallow grave, and then searched the place. Back
in the scrub we found a camp—such a camp ! A
poor break-wind of old boughs, a fire, still
smouldering, which the old man evidently tended
while the gin sought the only means of existence
it was possible for her to find, the wretched
Hl The mystery of the whole thing we never
fathomed—why no blacks had come near the lagoon
for years, and why the old pair had been left there
by themselves.
DELCOMYN.
Concerning a Steeplechase
Rider.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Of all the ways in which men get a living
there is none so hard and so precarious as that of
steeplechase-riding in Australia. It is bad enough
in England, where steeplechases only take place
in winter, when the ground is soft, and where the
horses are properly schooled before being raced,
and where the obstacles for the most part will
yield a little if struck and give a, horse a chance
to blunder over safely. In Australia the men have
to go at racing-speed on very hard ground, over
the most rigid and uncompromising obstacles—
ironbark rails clamped into solid posts with bands
of iron. No wonder they are always coming to
grief, and are always in and out of hospital in
splints and bandages. Sometimes one reads that
a horse has fallen and the rider has “escaped with
a severe shaking.” That “shaking,’ gentle reader,
would lay you or me up for weeks, with a doctor
to look after us and a crowd of sympathetic friends
calling to know how our poor back was. But the
steeplechase-rider has to be out and about again,
“ riding exercise ’’every morning, and “schooling
all sorts of cantankerous brutes over the fences.
These men take their lives in their hands and look
at grim death between their horses’ ears every tune
they race or “ school.”
The death-record among Australian cross-
country jockeys and horses is something awful .
it is a curious instance of how custom sanctifies all
things that such horse-and-man slaughter is ac-
cepted in such a calm, callous way. If any theatre
gave a show at which men and horses were habitu-
ally crippled and killed in full sight of the audience
the manager would be tried for manslaughter™ no
time. But the race-tracks use up their yearly
average of horses and men without attracting re-
mark. One would suppose that the risk being so
great the profits were enormous ; but they arenot
—quite otherwise, in fact. In “the game as
played on our racecourses there is just a bare liv-
ing for a good capable horseman while he lasts,
with the certainity of an ugly smash if he keeps at
it long enough.
* * * *
And they don’t need to keep at it very
long. After a few good “shakings” they begin
to “ take a nip or two ” to put heart into them
before they go out, and after awhile they have to
increase the dose. So that at last they cannot
ride at all without a regular cargo of alcohol on
board, and they are either “ half-muzzy ” or shaky
according as they have taken too much or too
little. And then they commence to fall—it is an
old axiom that as soon as a man begins to funk he
begins to fall. The reason is that a rider who has
lost his nerve is afraid of his horse making a mis-
take, and takes a pull or urges him onward just at
the critical instant—the one crucial moment when
the horse is rattling up to his fence and judging
his distance so as to make his spring. And the
little pull at his head or the little touch of the
spur takes his attention from the fence, with the
result that he makes his spring a foot too far off
or a foot too close in, and—smash ! And then the
loafers who hang about the big fences rush up to
see if the jockey is killed or stunned, and, if so,
they dispose of any jewellery he may have about
him —they have been known to almost tear off a
finger in their endeavors to secure the ring. And
the ambulance clatters up at a canter, the poor
rider is pushed in out of sight, and the ladies in
the stand say how unlucky they are—that brute
of a horse falling after they backed him. And a
wolfish-eyed man in the Leger-stand shouts to a
wolfish-eyed pal, “ Bill, I believe that jock was
killed when the chestnut fell,” and Bill replies
“Yes, damn him, I had five bob on him.” And
the rider, gasping like a crushed chicken, is carried
into the casualty-room and laid on a little stretcher,
while outside the window the bookmakers are
roaring “ Four to one bar one,” and the racing is
going on merrily as ever.
* * * *
Which remarks may serve to introduce
one of the fraternity who may be considered as
typical of all. He was a small, wiry, hard-
featured fellow, the son of a stockman on a big
cattle-station, and began life as a horse-breaker —
he was naturally a horseman, and able and willing
to ride anything that could carry him. Then he
left the station to go with cattle on the road, and,
having picked up a horse that showed pace,
amused himself by jumping him over fences.
Then he went to Wagga and entered the horse m
a steeplechase, rode him himself, won handsomely,
sold the horse at a good price to a Sydney buyer,
and went down to ride the horse in his Sydney
races. Did very well in Sydney, and got a name
as a fearless and clever rider, and was offered
several mounts on fine animals, so he pitched his
camp in Sydney and became a fully-enrolled mem-
ber of the worst profession in the world —that of
steeplechase-rider. I had known him in the old
days on the road, and when I met him on the
course one day I inquired how he liked the new
life.
“ Well, it’s a livin’,” he said, but it s no great
shakes. They don’t give steeplechase-riders a
chance in Sydney” (which is true enough).
“ There is very few races, and the big sweep-
stakes keep horses out of the game.” •
“ Do you get a fair share of the riding ? I
“Oh, yes; I get as much as anybody. But
there’s a lot of ’em got a notion I won’t take hold
of a horse when I’m told ( i.c ., pull him to prevent
him winning), but some of these days 111 take
hold of a horse when they don’t expect it.”
I smiled as I thought there was probably a
sorry day in store for some backer when the
jockey “ took hold ” unexpectedly.
“ Do you have to pull horses, then, to get em-
ployment ? ” „ , . , .
“ Oh, well, it’s this way, he said, rather apolo-
getically, “if an owner is badly treated by the
handicapper, and is just giving his horse a run to
get weight off, then it’s right enough to catch hold
a bit. But when a horse is favorite and the public
are backing him it isn’t right to take hold of him
then. I would not do it.” This was his whole
code of morals—not to pull a favorite : and he felt
himself very superior to the scoundrel who would
pull favorites or outsiders indiscriminately. >
“What do you get for riding? ” I asked him.
“ Well,” he said, looking about uneasily, “we’re
supposed to get a fiver for a losing mount and ten
pounds if we win, but such a lot of the steeple-
chase-owners are what I call ‘ battlers ’—men
who have no money and get along by owing every-
body money. They promise us all sorts of money
if we win but they don’t pay if we lose. I only
got two pounds for that last steeplechase.”
Two pounds ! I made a rapid mental calcula-
tion. He had ridden over eighteen fences for two
pounds—had chanced his life eighteen times at
less than half-a-crown a time.
“ Good Heavens,” I said, “that’s a poor game.
Wouldn’t you be better back on the station ?
“ Oh, I don’t know—sometimes we get laid a
bit to nothing, and do well out of a race. A/nd
then, you know, a steeplechase rider is somebody,
—not like an ordinary fellow that is just working.’
I realised that I was an “ordinary fellow who
was just working,” and felt small accordingly.
“I’m just off to weigh now,” he said —“I’m
riding Contractor, and he’ll run well, but he
always seems to fall at those logs. Still, I ought
to have luck to-day. I met a hearse as I was
coming out. I’ll get him over the fences, some-
how.”
“Do you think it lucky, then, to meet a
hearse ? ”
“Oh, yes,” he said, “if you meet it. You
mustn’t overtake it—that’s unlucky. So is a
cross-eyed man unlucky. Cross-eyed men ought
to be kept off race-courses ’’—and away he went
followed by a little knot of hungry-looking men
who were beseeching him fora “ tip ” for the race.
* * * *
When he reappeared he was clad in racing
rig, and we set off to see the horse saddled. We
found the owner in a great state of excitement.
It seemed he had no money, absolutely none what-
ever, but had borrowed enough to pay the sweep-
stakes and stood to make something if the horse
won and lose nothing if he lost, as he had nothing
to lose. My friend the rider insisted on being
paid two pounds before he would mount, and the
owner nearly had a fit in his efforts to persuade
him to ride on credit. At last a backer of the
horse came forward who agreed to pay £2 10s.,
win or lose, and the rider was to get £25 out of the
prize if he won. So up he got, and as he and the
others walked the big muscular horses round the
ring nodding gaily to friends in the crowd, I
thought of the gladiators going out to fight in the
arena with the cry of “Hail, Caesar, those about
to die salute thee ! ”
The story of the race is soon told. My friend
went to the front at the start and led nearly all
the way, and “ Contractor ! ” was on every one’s
lips as the big horse sailed along in front of his
field. He came at the log-fence full of running,
and it looked certain that he would get over. At
the last stride he seemed to falter, then plunged
right in to the fence, striking it with his chest
and turning right over it, landing on his unfor-
tunate rider. Evidently, meeting the hearse had
not brought him luck.
Man and horse lay still, and there was silence
in the stand, broken only by a few audible curses
from those who had backed the horse. A crowd
clustered round and hid horse and rider from
view, and I ran down to the casualty-room to
meet him when the ambulance came in. The gay
silks and colors were all mudspattered and blood-
stained as the limp form was carefully taken out
and laid on a stretcher while a doctor examined
the crushed ribs, the broken arm, and all the
havoc that the horse’s huge weight had made.
There was no hope from the first. My poor friend,
who had so often faced Death for two pounds,
lay very still awhile, gasping and quivering
slightly. Then he began to talk—wandering in
his mind. “ Where were the cattle ” —he had lost
the cattle—his mind evidently going back to the
old days on the road. Then he spoke quickly,
“Look out there —give me room!” and again
wandered off—“ Five-and-twenty pounds, Mary,
and a sure thing if he don’t fall at the logs.”
And “ Mary ” was sobbing beside the bed, cursing
the fence and the money that had fetched him to
grief. At last, in a tone of satisfaction, he said,
quite clear and loud: “I know how it was— there
couldn’t have been any dead man in that hearse 1 ”
And so, having solved the mystery to his own
satisfaction, he again drifted away into uncon-
sciousness—and woke somewhere on the other
side of the big fence that we can neither see
through nor over, but all have to face sooner or
later.
THE BANJO.
Lament.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Peace, your little child is dead !
Peace, I cannot weep with you 1
I have no more tears to shed,
I have mourned my baby, too—
I that ne’er was wooed or wed !
Love has looked within your eyes ;
Love has filled your hungry heart;
You have borne the babe, your prize,
You have blossomed, done your part—
Though the flower faded lies.
But to me was Love denied,
God had said it could not be :
Still my hungry hopes abide :
All the motherhood in me
Aches —and starves, unsatisfied.
How my soul has yearned for thee,
Sweet, sweet unborn child of mine !
How thy life would tenderly
Round thy mother’s life entwine —
Hope of hopes that may not be !
How thy hands would pluck my breast!
I have felt them o’er and o’er,
And thy soft, sweet skin caressed —
Baby mine I never bore !
Did I dream so ? Dreams are best!
You have nothing now to fear,
* Mother, you have fondled him,
Held his pretty face so near,
Laid your lips to each soft limb—
He is dead, but he was dear !
You have something you may mourn—
Some sweet memory to kiss ;
I am lonelier, more forlorn,
God has left me only this—
My sweet babe that was not bom.
Maoriland.
ARTHUR H. ADAMS.
Queensland is desperately trying to foster
a leather industry chiefly for the benefit of Too-
woomba. Toowoomba leather (it is alleged) is mostly
like tin in hot weather and like tripe in the web
season. Some time ago a gentleman travelling to
England took with him a portmanteau of the
aforesaid Toowoomba material. The officers, en-
dured the vile stench it emitted from the cabin as
. i ons as possible, until one of them propounded to
the owner the poser- did all Queensland stink like
that leather ?

“SHOUTING” TOR A CAMEL: A “BANJO” BALLAD ILLUSTRATED.


Matrimonial Love.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Poets have agreed to apotheosise courtship pre-
nuptial unfulfilled desire. The conventional
belief, the legendary idea, is that matrimony and
possession destroy the ideal and open the flood-
gates of dreary, drudging prose.
I cannot see it. .
Six years ago I married. I was not particularly
in love, though she was a sweet girl, passing fair
and tender. I cannot tell exactly why I married.
My reasons were manifold as my nature. I felt
lonely, .unappreciated. I yearned for somebody
to read my verses to, and gently smooth my fur-
rowed brow, and all that. “ I wanted some fool
to call me wise. ”
And then—the girl loved me !
Discreetly I pass over the honeymoon. When
my senses had regained their accustomed calm, I
felt some changes in the atmosphere surrounding
me. Contemplating as usual with earnest ad-
miration my own self (this is euphemistically
called the analytical habit), I remarked with
astonishment strange protuberances upon the
comer of that bright sun.
A great French astronomer discovered the
existence of an unknown planet through unac-
countable aberrations in the conduct of a neigh-
boring star. Even so, I for the first time, though
married for many months, became aware of the
existence of an outside influence powerful enough
to work silent but decisive changes in the whole
current of my thought.
The case interested me psychologically.
I could not have lived near a woman with ideas
of her. own, and would certainly not have been
influenced by her.
I had never expected my wife to stand mentally
on the same level with me. I knew she did not.
She could not reason (what woman can ?) and her
thought was not creative. Mine was, as my poems
prove .
And yet, though unable to follow me intel-
lectually, she influenced my ideas. There was in
her some natural gift, which I will call the instinct
of sympathy, that en-
abled her to accompany
me on my highest flights,
to feel with me in my
deepest and to her unin-
telligible sorrow.
Sh e seemed the connect-
ing-1 ink between nature
and the absurd position
of conceited manhood;
the root that drew life
out of Mother Earth to
feed the overgrown shoot
of artificially-forced civi-
lisation.
one: became mentally
indispensable to me.
I repaid the services
she unwittingly rendered
with such actions as I
thought would please her
most. I bought her yel-
low roses when she wore
a blue dress ; I killed her
pet dog in trying to cure
it of the results of over-
eating, at her loving
hands. And she adored
me.
Her adoration was
gratifying, and I began
to find beauty in these
little commonplaces of
connubial life. I des-
cended from my super-
lativeness, and consented
to interest myself in her
petty troubles in ex-
change for her help at
my latest poem.
She was my Inspira-
tion, though she was not
poetical. Others she
might’ have inspired to
political ambition, or
battle, or discovery.
Like beautiful music, a
food woman arouses the
test in man, independent
of her own inclinations.
The Best in me, unhap-
pily, was a capacity to
write poems —of the sort
which are never paid for.
Then she fell ill. It
did not seem serious at
first, but the disease
would not be vanquished.
For the first time in
my existence I felt the
sweetness of knowing a
being entirely dependent
on my person, my pre-
sence. I noticed, as I
had never noticed before,
the beauty of that pale,
thin face, as it lay framed
in a mass of dark hair—
the classic chiselling of
the bare arms folded
languidly on her bosom.
For hours I used to sit
beside her bed and drink
in that lovely picture, till
my soul was saturated
wi th her personality.
We spoke little, for she
was weak, and I was
happy. I was in love.
Not a love that desires,
that the poets sing about
and praise above all things
in heaven and earth. No,
my love had nothing
left to wish for. All
was mine.
But, ah! what bliss
could I not draw out of
a faint smile, a touch of
our hands, the confidence
of possession that lay in
her eyes !
Daily, steadily she was
sinking towards death.
I realised it slowly only,
by degrees. She knew it long before me, and
there was a sweet pity in her looks that often
puzzled me deeply.
Modern man, I had flattered myself, is a being
in whom civilisation has suffocated the wild,
vulgar barbarian passions. He appreciated shades,
degrees, not the glaring colors that delight the
savage and infuriate the bull.
It may be so. Perhaps lam not civilised.
But when the awful truth flashed upon me,
when suddenly I knew how I loved that woman
who was going to leave me, I verged on madness.
She grew very religious—l was an utter un-
bfeliever. But to please her I knelt at the bedside,
and prayed aloud, my voice addressing God, but
my soul the goddess I worshipped.
She seemed pained at the vehemence of my
grief, and so I tried to master myself for her sake.
I had never done so before. I think I even
managed to smile; and when I kissed her for the
last time, when her soft arms encircled my neck
and the vanishing breath touched my brow, 1
found strength to speak of a meeting hereafter.
And all the time I knew it would never be.
But then, you see, I loved. _ Not with your
selfish, prenuptial desire, but with the love that
she had taught me.
Queensland.
VON KOTZR.
A Bush Tangueray.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The coach stole round a path hewn out of
j; be grey sandstone, to the road that ran white and
bafe oyer djo summ it Q f a hill. The driver pulled
Away doW n in the smoke-laden hollow a,
P J , , , , f■ t
number of men gathered and sent up a faint
cheer. Then a shirt of many colors, supported by
yellow moles, rose solemnly from the box-seat and
Jnade some para bolic gestures in return. The
d™ touched hi ß leader tenderly on the flank
and the coach wound through lichen-covered
boulders into a dingy mulga background. Simul-
fcaneously the crowd below adjourned to the pub.
mottle-faced old “whaler” peeped in at the
door to remark, for the fifth time, that “ water
was bad, and the road too strnkin’ for anything,
one noticed him until, pressed by a great
thirst, he hazarded another cast of the die :

song without funny business . Ever eard, When
Molly marries the Ringer ? ’ I’d sing ‘ Billy, the
Boundry,’ only I’m gone in the ’igh notes through
sl in - in tbe wet without a bluey.”
1 .. . v
A derisive, withering reply sent him hobbling to
the kerb to examine further the grey ridges that
bounded an everlasting plain, and the question of
his life —the road. Conversation in the bar turned
upon Benjamin Stokes, the man who had just left
by coach for Sydney. Everybody admitted that
Ben was too reserved and sullen. In the first
place, his life had been spent beyond the enlighten-
ing influence of his fellow-townsmen, in long
night-watches with stamping herds and vicious
colts. “ And the result,” said Tackier, the school-
master, “ is a product as rough as Nature—his god.
Gentlemen,” continued Tackier, seizing a gin-and-
peppermint, “the man Stokes is a heathen idola-
tor.” And Mottle-face went lamely over the hill,
his tattered clothes flapping weirdly through a
vista of white dust.
* * * *
Ben’s trip was to last a month, and each
week of his absence was duly notched off on the
post outside the pub. W hen the notches grew to
ten, and he did not return, the circumstance was
referred to in the Deep Ckeek Dabbler.
Ben had never seen a train before ; his ideas of
city life had been drawn from the rough word-
pictures of bushmen. The cause of his prolonged
absence was explained in the first page of his new
pocket-book —
Stoping two teeth, one ginny. Millysent Lee—cab—
mattrymonal agency, 3£ 2s. 6d.
* * * *
One afternoon the coach dropped them at
the door of a hut near the creek. The driver shook
hands with Ben, winked at Ben’s wife, and flogged
his horses over the wooden bridge to the township.
A tabby cat brought out several blind kittens for
inspection, exhibiting a sinful pride which led to
painful consequences, for a few minutes later an
anxious mother mewed piteously near the tank,
while Benjamin did strange things with her blind
offspring in a bucket of water. Millicent threw
herself wearily on a biscuit-box and slowly took
out her hat-pins. The room was stuffy and
dark ; the tiny window and the little tin mirror
filled her with a profound astonishment. In a
corner was a narrow bed that met the require-
ments of a long, single man, and its presence
plainly indicated that the whole wedlock business
was unpremeditated. A sporting print on the
wall depicted “Jimmy the Biff” going sweet and
fresh after ten hard rounds with “Mick the
Nipper ” from Bendigo.
Through a large hole in the wall near the fire-
place Ben apologised for the speckled condition of
the nuptial chamber—due, he explained, to the
goats and fowls. By-and-bye he might nail up the
hole with a bag ;it was getting too big. Some
night an enterprising cow would squeeze through
and breathe over a married couple—he’d nail it
up now. He rushed away, and there were sounds
of a man chopping wood.
The following day was Sunday, and the
new wife hinted weakly about going to church.
“ Right, Mill,” said Ben, dropping the saw he was
greasing, “ we’ll go now, though I’ve never been
before. Put on your grey dress and the hat with
the big black feather. ”
He chose a seat directly under the pulpit.
“ Keep yer ’ead agin the mahogany, Mill; they’ll
be dyin’ to see yer face when they come in; don’t
let ’em.”
The church at this time was empty; but it
filled—filled to overflowing. “ Don’t forgit the
mahogany, Mill,” whispered Ben behind his
hand.
Their pew remained as sacred as a Hindu cow.
The coach-driver pointed them out from a crowded
porch, and his audience appeared spasmodically
grateful for the information concerning Mrs. Ben.
The driver admitted regretfully that his friend,
Sam Hopkins, knew her pretty well, thanks —
“wished he knew her as well.” Still, it wasn’t
for him to take away the character of a respectable
married woman. Heard that she could cook as
blanky good a feed as anybody in Pyers,
and if . The organ took it up, and sent out a
moaning “ Adeste Fideles.”
The minister thundered at his stoic congrega-
tion, and charged the air with strange, charitable
precepts. At the end he waved a calm benedic-
tion over his respectable flock: “Go in peace and
sin no more.”
The men leered at Ben and Mill as they passed
out; young girls gathered up skirts and scattered;
obese wives and mothers cannoned in circling,
agitated groujis.
“Thank God the roof didn’t fall upon us this
blessed day ! The idea ! ”
Ben lifted his head and eyed the hostile gather-
ing ; some of them had known him for years—
since the time when he used to drive about Pyers
in a billy-goat tandem. A shout of mocking
laughter followed them to the gate. Ben shut his
mouth; an unknown shame spread to his neck
and face ; something gripped his arm, and a word
hummed in his ear that an ordinary woman never
uses at any period of her life.
So they tramped along, voiceless and sullen,
through paddocks where flowers nodded to a
caressing wind, while the sun warmed per-
fumes from the moist Spring earth. Mill’s right
hand bruised her breast savagely ; the other held
his sleeve.
* * * *
She glanced furtively at him across the
room—his head down, his chin resting in the heel
of his palm.
“ Did I ever say that I was a good girl, Ben ? I
ain’t, Gawd ’elp me ! ”
She thrust herself beside him, shaking and
trembling. Then Benjamin Stokes listened,
almost for the first time in his life, to the com-
monest story in the world—a betrayal, a little
shame, a gradual hardening, a world-defiance.
“The old woman at
the boardin’-house said
she’d clear me out unless
I was obligin’ and civil
to the gentlemen. So
there was presents for
Mill, and gloves planted
in my bed. ... It all
helped to take my head
away from the damned
’ard scrubbin’. I ain’t
old—seventeen ain’t very
old, is it ? Gimme a
chance, Ben—gimme a
chance!”
* * *
Something simmered
in the fireplace; plates
clattered ; a shadowy girl
moved about him all the
afternoon in a dull, half-
fTightened way. He
stumbled outside to the
wood-heap, and tile soft-
eyed collie hung at his
heels for a word
The sun dropped to
the edge of the plains,
drenching the far-off hills
with yellow mists. A
rush of cool air brought
the clang of bells; he
raised a rough and hag-
gard face and spoke a
word to the night a
word h e used wh e n
punching cattle through
an overflow. The dog
fawned joyou s 1 y—
“Away, you beast!”—
and a savage kick sent it

A candle flickered in
the little bedroom, throw-

chintz curtain. “That
bell again ! ” He walked

house. How everyone
knew now everyone
guessed the truth ! What
had happened at the
church to-day would hap-
pen again with sickening
regularity. He might
force the men to respect

that cackling brood in the

the room to fling a word
of hate at this Magdalen
—and fell into a chair,
silenced. The face was
so pretty, so weak—prey
for every libertine. The
minister had said some-
thing about a woman who
wiped the feet of Christ
with her long meshes of
hair; nobody believed it,
of course; if they did,
why was Mill treated as
she had been? He sat
through the long night,
heavy-browed and brood-
ing, until a grey light
from the east whitened
the window-pane.
“ Mill! ” She smiled
sleepily at the word.
‘ ‘ Mill ! ” The sound
of his voice made her
crouch on the rough
pallet; she stared at the
white haggard face in the
half-light.
“Don’t be tTightened,
Mill —don’t be fright-
ened ; I shan’t hit yer.
I’ve been thinkin’ ; and
we ain’t goin’ to church
again to let ’em worry
us. I’ll build another
place overat RedPointon
the hundred-acre patch ; if they come over there
to carry on I’ll be about to receive ’em.”
Her face was hidden from him, but her hand
crept into his big palm.
* * * *
A few hours later Ben led a bay horse to
the front and hopped into the saddle. She came
to the door, her white arms splashed with milk
and flour.
“ It’s a long way to the Point, ain’t it, Ben? ”
“Yeh!”
She stole nearer—obviously to examine the
horse. He threw himself forward and kissed her
on the lips.
N.S.W.
