The Restitution of Dan Mayne,
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Dickson could not understand it. He had watched
day and night till he was weary, and yet had
seen or heard nothing that explained the mystery.
His fruit when ripe for market, his poultry and
cattle when fat to kill, had a trick of vanishing,
melting into memories, in a way not to be ex-
plained by flying-foxes, native cats and dingoes.
He dared not suspect his neighbors—good, kind,
genial hearts —it revolted his soul
even to doubt their honesty. Yet
sundry hints dropped by the travel-
ling storekeeper made him uneasy.
But time passed bringing neither
trace nor sign. He worked and
waited.
* * * *
It was night. Dickson had gone
to bed and was dozing off, when he
was startled by a rapping at the door.
‘ ‘ Who’s there ? ” he shouted through
the slabs.
“ It’s me, Mr. Dickson. Mother
says will you come down and see
dad—he’s real bad and like to die?”
Dickson, recognising the voice,
arose and admitted the visitor, young
Martin Mayne, looking as stolid and
apathetic as usual. “ What’s
the matter with your father ?”
enquired Dickson as he hur-
ried on his clothes.
“Wo don't know at all, at
all ; but he is powerful bad to be
sure.”
They hurried down the track in the darkness,
or rather Dickson strove to hasten, for Martin
would not move from his accustomed pace. As
they neared the house there were manifest signs
of. Dan’s sickness. On the hill, half a mile away,
they could hear his shrieks and groans. “ Holy
Mary ! have mercy on us ! Holy Mother of God !
deliver us ! Holy and blessed saints help me, a
poor sinner! ”
Dickson grew impatient with concern, but
Martin still walked deliberately. “Is there no
one with your father ? ”
“Yes, there’s Fred Jones and George, Abe
Sinclair, Long Dave, and Old Fergusson, and
Sid.’s gone for the Murphys, but I don’t know if
they’ve come ; and I sang out to Garvan as I came
to your place.”
“ Why, you’ve got nearly everybody.”
“ Yes,” said Martin, with the first sign of emo-
tion ; “ we’ve got most of the people about here.”
* * * *
They entered the house by the kitchen, a big
room for a bush house—but the Mayne family was
large, and they were all there with the neighbors
Martin had mentioned. Two candles lighted the
crowd, who were talking and joking merrily, while
a group round the table played cards, quite re-
gardless of the groans and cries from the next
room.
“Would you like to see Dan?” enquired Mrs.
Mayne, coming forward.
Dickson nodded wonderingly, and wa3 shown
into the siek man’s room. One of the
daughters sat by the side of the bed, wherein the
patient rolled and twisted like one of the damned.
“ Holy, blessed ! Is that you, Mr. Dickson ?
I’m glad you’ve come ! I’m glad you’ve come ! ”
Then another spasm took the old man, and he
roared out, “ Holy Mary ! ”
(And, like a mocking echo, a voice came from
the next room : “ I make it hearts and go alone.”)
“I’m a dead man, Mr. Dickson, and I wouldn’t
be after dying with sin on my soul. Holy and
blessed Peter, have pity on me ! ”
“Where is the painenquired Dickson, sym-
pathetically.
(The voice in the next room answered: “I’ve
euchred you, by gum ! ”)
“ Holy Saints ! But I’m a dead man, Mr. Dick-
son. It’s stiff and cold I’ll be in the morning, and
it’s confession I’d make before I die. D’ye mind
them two unbranded heifers of yours ? ”
Dickson nodded.
“ It’s myself ran them in with the last mob I
took to Penrith, and it’s your forgiveness I want,
and I’ll make it right with you.”
“Very well,” said Dickson, somewhat taken
aback. “I’ll—l’ll forgive you.”
(From the next room came a hubbub of voices.
“ I pass.” “ Me, too.” “ And I make it clubs.”
“ Then you’re euchred, as sure as you’re born ! ’’)
“Give us your hand on it, sonny—Holy Mother,
have mercy on me ! There was six bits of hens
and a rooster —perhaps you mind them, Mr. Dick-
son ? I took them in a bag one night. You’ll be
after forgiving me for that ? ”
“What! the fowls, too ? ”
“Only a few bits of chickens,” said Dan, in a
tone almost offended.
“ Well, I forgive you the fowls. But where did
you sell them ? ’’
(“There’s the left-bower, anyhow; that’s good
enough for you.”)
“ It’s after knowing who had them you’d be? ”
And Dan’s little eyes gleamed suspiciously.
“ Never you mind ! Never you mind ! I’ll make
it right with you. Holy Mary ! Holy Virgin ! ”
“I think, Mrs. Mayne,” said Dickson to the
wife, who had just come in, “ hot fomen-
tations might do him some good.”
“Do you think so, now? I was after
trying myself, but Dan is that obstinate.”
The remedy-was tried, the pain eased, and in S
few minutes Daniel dozed off to sleep. Dickson,
seeing no more to be done, declined the cordial in-
vitation to make another hand at euchre, and went
home and to bed again. The next day, calling at
Mayne’s to see how the old man was, he found
him seated in the fireplace.
“ How are you this morning, Mr. Mayne?”
“ Powerful bad, to be sure,” said Dan, making a
face, “ it’s the trouble of the world I had to eat a
bit of breakfast.” Then Dan groaned, drew his
mouth down, and, after a pause—“ I was after
telling you last night about a heifer of yours I took
away in mistake.”
“ Two heifers,” corrected Dickson.
“ One ! Why, man alive, don’t you think I
know two from one ? It was a poor little thing ;
sure to have died the winter —but never you mind,
I’ll make it right with you. That red cow of mine
is near calving, and you shall have the calf,” cried
Dan, benevolently.
“ But you said two heifers,” objected Dickson.
“ Now, do you want to take advantage of a poor
map when he’s sick? It’s the three little bits of
liens you’re thinking of. Never you mind, never
you worry about that. Til make them right with
you.”
The days passed. Dan got about again. The
red cow’s accouchement passed off as successfully
as a royal birth, and Dickson regarded the calf
from time to time with the feeling of future pos-
session.
It was December. Dan’s bush caught fire, and
Dickson ran with other neighbors to help beat it
out. They were glowing hot, steaming with per-
spiration when it was over, and Dan walked with

Time passed, but neither pullets nor calf were
sent up from Dan Mayne’s, nor did Dickson’s
portables seem more stable than of yore. It was
about a year later that Dickson’s sleep was again
interrupted by a rapping at the door. Martin was
there as he had been before, and with a still
more urgent message to summon Dickson
to the death-bed of his father.
“I don’t see how I can do him any
good. I’m no doctor.”
“ You put him right last time,” said
Martin in a tone almost reproachful.
It seemed to Dickson that, as
they got near the house, the howls
and groans proceeding therefrom
were louder than they had been
before— a storm of yells and
A SAFE ANSWER.
FATHER: “ What was the sermon about to-night?”
DAUGHTER (who went far a walk with her boy instead of to church) : “ Oh, about the
debt on the church and collecting money for the heathen! ”
screeches interspersed with appeals to the
Saints and the Holy Family. In the kitchen
the same crowd were playing euchre, as though
they had never left the table since he had last seen
them there. In the bed-room Dan writhed in
agony. _
“ I tried the hot fomentations,” whispered Mrs.
Mayne, “an’ devil a bit o’ good did they do him
this time. I’ve sent for Father O’Halligan, but
it’s doubting I am his reverence will come at all,
at all. You see the last time he came Dan was
having his breakfast, and his reverence was
mightily disappointed.”
“Is that you ?” said Dan in a pause between
his groans; “sure I’m settled this time safe
enough, and it’s rather stone-dead I’d be twenty
times over than suffer the like. And it’s me con-
science pricks me sore, Mr. Dickson, and the
weight of me sins. Lord have mercy on me !
you'd only forgive me I’d die aisier in my moind.”
“Why, did I not forgive you long ago, Mr.
Mayne ? ”
1 It’s the little heifer you’d be speaking of, an’
the bits of chickens. Never you mind about them
—l’ll lave you them in my will. It’s that roan
bullock and the pumpkins on the flat, I’d be
speaking about. ”
“You don't mean to say !” interrupted
Dickson.
“ Now don’t you be hard on a dying man. I’ll
make it up to you. Not a penny shall you lose,
not a penny! and ere Dickson could answer Dan
had gone off into convulsions again, his groans so
heart-rending that even the card-players subdued
their raucous voices for a while.
“ Oh, what shall I do, Mr. Dickson ? sure Dan’s
sinking fast!
“ Had you not better send for the doctor ? ”
Tears were in the wife’s eyes, sobs in her voice,
as she called Mike to harness up the spring-cart.
“ Oh, dear ! Oh, dear ! Holy saints have mercy
on us ! ” She went to some recess in the bedroom
and brought out an old stocking from which she
began to count out gold and silver money.
“ Five pounds ! five pounds! It’s a robbery,
Mr. Dickson, that’s what it is, to charge a poor
body a pound a mile.”
At the clink of the coins Dan stopped groaning.
At thq, sight of the money he fairly screeched.
“ What’s that?”
“Sure it’s the money for the doctor, no less.”
“ It’s a doctor you’re getting, a doctor ! And you
think I could rest quiet in my grave if you gave
five pounds to a doctor ! Holy Virgin ! Five
pounds for a doctor ! And you'want me to die
ruined, so you do ! ”
At. this moment the noise of the spring-cart
drawing up to the house was audible.
“ What’s that? ” cried Dan.
“It’s Mike with the cart, to be sure.”
“ And it’s going to the doctor he is, with five of
my pounds ! Bad luck to him ! Do you think I’d
die at the cost of five pounds ! Bad luck to you ! ”
And the old man, his mouth full of imprecations,
jumped from the bed, huddled on his clothes,
rushed out of the house, unharnessed the horse,
and would not come back again till he had locked
up the stable and hidden the key.
♦ * * ♦
Two months after this night Dickson sold out
his selection to a man from Sydney ; but, amongst
the numerous assets handed over, he failed to
mention his reversionary interest in two heifers,
seven fowls, one bullock, and a ton or two of
pumpkins.

HENRY FLETCHER.
Hanging and Hell .
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
There are at least six Hanging Rocks and three
Gallows Hills in New South Wales. There is one
Hell. I’ve been there, thus :
Assize time in Wagga Wagga and a man on
trial for murder. Another man, myself, on trial
of sobriety, dead broke, hat battered, boots un-
laced, swag all no-how, and so nervous that the
gambolling of Pop scares me. Funny that that
animal is never so delighted as when I shoulder
bluey. I pass the court-house; a knot of idlers
are round the gate, a policeman conies out and
says, “They’ll hang him.” I’m off like a shot,
and in my ears the din of “hang him, bangin’,
bangin’, hang, ’ang, ’ng—ng—ng.” Then I turn
a corner to get out of public gaze. Unluckily, I
pass the side door of the building. I am spell-
bound ; there I see an awful visage, a face with
such a ghastly mouth, and such a dress below the
mouth, for the mouth has taken charge of the face,
like the Cheshire Cat’s grin in Wonderland. Then
the grin and the hair, the mouth and the gown,
the words and the black cap take me by the
shoulders and elsewhere and hustle me on to the
river bank. “Hang, hang, hang—ng—ng,” and
now Ive got them. I know they're on me and so
does Pop, because he whines and fawns on me.
But for Pop I’d have shown the horrors. Now I
only feel them.
Then I walk and walk and walk. It seems two
days, but it's not so wearisome, because I have
music, that rhythm, you know, always in my ears.
It’s company if it’s horrible, and the dog is some
consolation. Next, I find myself by a vineyard
full of angels ; they give me three 'tumblers of
wine and a bottle for the road. They wish me to
stay, and see me through iny recovery, but—that
grin, that mug, that wig, and the little black cap !
I’m off.
Again I travel about a week, in my mind’s eye,
and camp near an old shed under a tree. There’s
a paling fence near, and it looks like the bars of a
cage or prison.
But the tree—the gallo.vs-tree ! The boughs are
scraggy, and the leaves white on top ; there’s a
break m the middle, and a guard round the barrel.
It looks like that head. The gap is the cavernous
mouth, and the soughing of the breeze is “ Hang
him, they’ll hang him ! ”
Still, I’m not so badly off. I can see an angel
or two feeding Pop. A man comes down with a
pint of tea and a plate of soup. He tells me this
is the “ Burnt Hut,” and that the fires are Hell.
Just then he’s called by the overseer, and says
“Hang him.” Now I’m off again. The din
begins, the tree shivers, the head wags, the mug
mutters, some crows perch on the top and make a
black cap, and I fall asleep near the paling fence
—the prison cage.
When I wake I look through. I see Pop,
watching my swag on the other side, and a man
tying the legs of a sheep. That sheep is, to me,
myself. He skins it, and calls to his mate, “ Come
and help me to hang him.” The wretch is going
to hang me, the same good fellow who gave me
the soup. Luckily, I spot the bottle of wine. I
reach for it and drink it, holding the bottle by the
neck. I know that I am being hung—hanged, I
mean; but I’m not going to let them touch me,
myself, the “ alter ego.” They come near me and
fetch the overseer, who brings me a nip of the real
Mackay. It is now a little funny : I’m hung, and
still drinking whisky, and the hangman isn’t so
bad. I suppose the overseer’s the devil, because he
says, in a sort of pitying tone, “ Well ! isn’t this
hell?”
Another snooze, and I awake, it ought to be
dark, but it isn’t. The night is lit up by bush
fires on and around a pinnacle of smoke and flame.
We can see the timbers falling, the sparks rising.
“We” are the men and I. I can see the wig and the
mouth and the grin and the corpse, standing out
with red lights on a murky background. A
calico shroud has been tied round the sheep, and
it now looks like a suspended ghost, i fancy its
soul is gone to—well, blazes was my thought.
Then I become sure of it, because the overseer-
the devil, I mean—calls the men to look at the
Hanging Rock, the pillar of fire, and tells them or
half asks them, “ Isn’t it hell ? ” What with the
Hanging and the Hell, I start again (mentally),
and have my little song of “ Hang him, hang him,
hell, ell, well—not so bad—hang- hell.”
And I awoke; and behold, it was a dream.
“Well, old fellow! Are you better? Can you
cook ? Can have a job, if you like. Only me and
some fencers. We’re out all day. Tackle it to-
morrow. I’ll give you a nip or two - I’ve had ’em
myself.”
But there are few overseers like the one who
used to be at the Burnt Hut, under the Hang-in?
Rock. Isn’t it hell ?
SCOTTY THE WRINKLER
Sauce for the Gander.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ Cupid, child, to me discover
Why I worship Mary, feeling
I am not her heart’s king-lover,
But a slave, allowed to hover
Near her gracious form, revealing
All my weakness and my pain ?
Must I still her slave remain,
Knowing well I love in vain—
Though on heights of wise disdain
Fain I’d live and die above her ?”
“ Lackaday ! ” quoth Cupid, sadly ;
“Often have I seen, with wonder,
Sweetest women fondling madly
Men who taste and quit them gladly.
Nature, angered by her blunder,
Sometimes bids a maiden cool,
Worldly wise, or fresh from school,
Wind a love-yarn round a spool
Just for pastime. Luckless fool.
Doth your heart ache very badly ? ”
EDMUND FISHER.


White-when-he's-wanted.
A STATION SKETCH.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Buckalong was a big freehold of some 80,000 acres,
belonging to an absentee syndicate, and therefore
run in most niggardly style. There was a manager
on <£2oo a-year, Sandy M'Gregor to wit—a hard-
headed old Scotchman known as “ four-eyed
M'Gregor,” because he wore spectacles. For
assistants, he had half-a-dozen of us jackeroos
and colonial-experieneer.
—who got nothing a year,
and earned it. We had,
in most instances, paid
premiums to learn the
noble art of squatting,
which now appears to me
hardly worth studying,
for so much depends on
luck that a man with
a head as long as a horse’s
has little better chance
than the fool just im-
ported. Besides the man-
ager and the jackeroos,
there were a few bound-
ary-riders to prowl round
the fences of the vast
paddocks. This consti-
tuted the whole station
staff.
Buckalong was on one
of the main routes by
which stock were taken
to market, or from the
plains to the tablelands,
and vice - versa. Great
mobs of travelling sheep
constantly passed through
the run, eating up the
grass and vexing the soul
of the manager. By law,
sheep must travel six
miles per day, and they
must keep within half-a-
mile of the road. Of
course, we kept all the
grass near the road eaten
bare, to discourage travel-
lers from coming that
way. Such hapless
wretches as did venture
through Buckalong used
to try hard to stray from
the road and pick up a
feed, but old Sandy was
always ready for them,
and would have them
dogged right through the
run. This bred feuds,
and bad language, and
personal combats between
us and the drovers, whom
we looked upon as natural
enemies. Then the men
who came through with
mobs of cattle used to
pull down the paddock
fences at night, and
slip the cattle in for
refreshments; but old
Sandy often turned out at
2 or 3 a.m. to catch a big
mob of bullocks in the
horse-paddock, and then
off they went to Buckalong pound. The drovers,
as in duty bound, attributed the trespass to
accident —broken rails, and so on —and sometimes
they tried to rescue the cattle, which again bred
strife and police-court summonses.
• * • ■
Besides having a particular aversion to drovers,
old M'Gregor had a general “ down ” on the young
“colonials,” whom he comprehensively described
as a “feckless, horse-dealin’, horse-stealin’, crawlin’
lot o’ wretches. ” According to him, a native would
sooner work a horse to death than work for a
living, any day. He hated any man who wanted
to sell him a horse. “As ah walk the street,” he
used to say, “the folk disna stawp me to buy
claes nor shoon, an’ wheerfore should they stawp
me to buy horses ? It’s ‘ Mister M'Gregor, will
ye purrchase a horrse ’ ? Let them wait till I ask
them to come wi’ theer horrses.”
Such being his views on horseflesh and drovers,
we felt no little excitement when one Sunday, at
dinner, the cook came in to say there was a
drover-chap outside wanted the boss to come and
have a look at a horse.” M'Gregor simmered
a,while, and muttered something about the “ Saw-
bath day ”; but at last he went out, and we filed
after him to see the fun.
The drover stood by the side of his horse, beneath
the acacia trees in the yard. He had a big scar on
his face, apparently the result of collision with a
tree ; and seemed poverty-stricken enough to dis-
arm hostility. Obviously, he was “ down on his
luck.” He looked very thin and sickly, with
clothes ragged and boots broken. Had it not
been for that indefinable self-reliant look which
drovers—the Ishmaels of the bush—always acquire,
one might have taken him for a swagman. His
horse was in much the same plight. A ragged,
unkempt pony, pitifully poor and very footsore—
at first sight, an absolute “ moke,” but a second
glance showed colossal round ribs, square hips,
and a great length of rein, the rest hidden beneath
a wealth of loose hair. He looked like “ a good
journey horse,” possibly something better.
We gathered round while M'Gregor questioned
the drover. The man was monosyllabic to a
degree, as real bushmen generally are. It is only
the rowdy and the town-bushy that is fluent of
speech.
“Good morning,” said M'Gregor.
“ Mornin’, boss,” said the drover, shortly.
“ Is this the horrse ye have for sale ? ”
“Yes.”
“ Aye,” and M'Gregor looked at the pony with
a business-like don’t-think-much-of-him air; ran
his hand lightly over the hard legs, and opened
the passive creature’s mouth. “ H’m, ’he said.
Then he turned to the drover. “Ye seem a bit
oot o’ luck. Ye’re thin like. What’s been the
matter ? ”
“Been sick with fever—Queensland fever. Just
come through from the North. Been out on the
Diamantina last.”
“ Aye. I was there mysel’,” said M'Gregor.
“ Have ye the fever on ye still? ”
“ Yes —goin’ home to get rid of it.”
It should be explained that a man can only get
Queensland fever in a malarial district, but he can
carry it with him wherever he goes. If he stays,
it will sap all his strength and pull him to pieces ;
if he moves to a better climate, the malady moves
with him, leaving him only by degrees, and
coming back at regular intervals to rack, shake,
burn, and sweat its victim. Queensland fever will
pull a man down from 15st. to 9st. faster, and
with greater certainty, than any system of dosing
yet invented. Gradually it wears itself out, often
wearing its patient out at the same time.
M‘Gregor had been through the experience, and
there was a slight change in his voice as he went
on with the palaver.
“ Where are ye makin’ for the noo ? ”
“ Monaro—my people live in Monaro.”
“ How will ye get to Monaro if ye sell the
horrse ? ”
“ Coach and rail. Too sick to care about
ridin’,” said the drover, while a wan smile flitted
over his yellow-grey features, “ I’ve rode him far
enough. I’ve rode that horse a thousand miles.
I wouldn’t sell him, only I’m a bit hard-up. Sellin’
him now to get the money to go home.”
“ How old is he? ”
“Seven.”
“Is he a good horse on a camp”? asked
M'Gregor.
“No better camp horse in Queensland, said the
drover. “You can chuck the reins on his neck,
an’ he’ll cut out a beast by himself.”
M‘Gregor’s action in this matter puzzled us.
We spent our time crawling after sheep, and a
camp horse would be about as much use to us as
side-pockets to a pig. We had expected Sandy to
rush the fellow off the place at once, and we
couldn’t understand how it was that he took so
much interest in him. Perhaps the fever-racked
drover and the old camp horse appealed to him in
a way to us incomprehensible. We had never
been on the Queensland cattle-camps, nor shaken
and shivered with the fever, nor lived the roving
life of the overlanders. M'Gregor had done all
this, and his heart (I can see it all now) went out
to the man who brought the old days back to him.
“ Ah, weel,” he said, “ we hae’na much use for a
camp horrse here, ye ken; wi’oot some of these
lads wad like to try theer han’ cuttin’ oot the
milkers’ cawves frae their mithers.” And the old
man laughed contemptuously, while we felt hum-
bled and depraved in the eyes of the man from
far back. “ An’ what’ll ye be wantin’ for him ? ’
asked M'Gregor. .
“Reckon lie’s worth 15 notes,” said the drover.
This fairly staggered us. Our estimates had
varied between 30s. and a fiver. We thought the
negotiations would close abruptly, but M'Gregor,
after a little more examination, agreed to give the
price, provided the saddle and bridle, both grand
specimens of ancient art, were given in. This was
agreed to, and the drover was sent off to get his
meals in the hut before leaving by the coach.
“ The mon is verra hard-up, an it’s a sair tiling
that Queensland fever,” was the only remark that
M'Gregor made. But we knew that there was a
soft spot in his heart somewhere.
* * * •
And so, next morning, the drover got a crisp-
looking cheque, and departed by coach. He said
no word while the cheque was being written, but,
as he was going away, the horse happened to be in
the yard, and he went over to the old comrade
that had carried him so many miles, and laid a
hand on his neck. “He ain’t much to look at,”
said the drover, speaking slowly and awkwardly,
“but he’s white when he’s wanted.” And just
before the coach rattled off, the man of few words
leant down from
the box and nod-
ded impressively,
and repeated,
“ Yes, he’s white
when he’s want-
ed.”
We didn’t trouble to give the new horse a name.
Station horses are generally called after the man
from whom they are bought. “Tom Devine,”
“The Regan mare,” “Black M‘Carthy,” and
“ Bay M'Carthy ” were amongst the appellations
of our horses at that time. As we didn’t know the
drover’s name, we simply called the animal “ The
new horse ” until a still-newer horse was one day
acquired. Then, one of the hands being told to
take the new horse, said, “ D’yer mean the new
new horse or the old ‘ new horse ’? ” “ No,” said
the boss, “ not the new horse—that bay horse we
bought from the drover. The one he said was
white when he was wanted.’
And so, by degrees, the animal came to be
referred to as the horse that’s white when he’s
wanted, and at last settled down to the definite
name of “ White-when-he’s-wanted.”
« * * *
White-when-he’s-wanted didn’t seem much of
an acquisition. He was sent out to do slavery for
Greenhide Billy, a boundary-rider who plumed
himself on having once been a cattle-man. After
a week’s experience of “ White,” Billy came in to
the homestead disgusted—the pony was so lazy
that he had to build a fire under him to get him to
move, and so rough that it would make a man’s
nose bleed to ride him more than a mile. “ The
boss must have been off his head to give 15 notes
for such a cow.”
M‘Gregor heard this complaint. “ Verra weel,
Mr. Billy,” said he, hotly, “ye can just tak’ one
of the young horrses in yon paddock, an’ if he
bucks wi’ ye, an’ kills ye, it’s yer ain fault.
Ye’re a cattle-man—so ye say —dommed if ah
believe it. Ah believe ye’re a dairy-farmin’ body
frae Illawarra. Ye don't know neither horrse nor
cattle. Mony’s the time ye never rode buck-
jumpers, Mr. Billy ’’—and with this parting-shot
the old man turned into the house, and White-
when-he’s-wanted came back to the head station.
For a while he was a sort of pariah. He used to
yard the horses, fetch up the cows, and hunt
travelling sheep through the run. He really was
lazy and rough, and we all decided that Billy s
opinion of him was correct, until the day came to
make one of our periodical raids on the wild horses
in the hills at the back of the run. Every now
and again we formed parties to run-in some of
these animals, and, after nearly galloping to death
half-a-dozen good horses, we would capture three
or four brumbies, and bring them in triumph to
the homestead. These we would break-in, and,
by the time they had thrown half the crack riders
on the station, broken all the bridles, rolled on all
the saddles, and kicked all the dogs, they would
be marketable (and no great bargains) at about
30s. a head.
Yet there is no sport in the world to be men-
tioned in the same volume as “running horses,”
and we were very keen on it. All the crack nags
were got as fit as possible, and fed-up beforehand,
and on this particular occasion White-when-he’s-
wanted, being in good trim, was given a week’s
hard-feed and lent to a harum-scarum fellow from
the Upper Murray, who happened to be working
in a survey-camp on the run. How he did open
our eyes. He ran the mob from hill to hill, from
range to range, across open country and back
again to the hills, over flats and gullies, through
hop-scrub and stringybark ridges ; and all the
time White-when-he „-wanted was on the wing
of the mob, pulling
double. The mares and
foals dropped out, then
the colts and young stock
pulled up deadbeat, and
only the seasoned veterans
of the mob were left.
Most of our horses caved
in altogether ; one or two
were kept in the hunt by
judicious nursing and
shirking the work, but
White-when'- he’s-wanted
was with the quarry from
end to end of the run, do-
ing double his share ; and
at the finish, when a chance
offered to wheel them into
the trapyard, he simply
smothered them for pace,
and slewed them into the
wings before they knew
where they were. Such a
capture had not fallen to
our lot for many a day,
and the fame of White-
when- he’s -wanted was
speedily noised abroad.
He was always fit for
work, always hungry,
always ready to lie down
and roll, and always
lazy. But when he
heard the rush of the
brumbies’ feet in the
scrub, he became frantic
with excitement. He
could race over the
roughest ground without
misplacing a hoof or
altering his stride, and
he could sail over fallen
timber and across gullies
like a kangaroo. Nearly
every Sunday we were
after the brumbies, until
they got as lean as grey-
hounds and as cunning
as policemen. We were
always ready to back
White-when-he’s -wanted
to run - down, single-
handed, any animal in
the bush that we liked to
put him after wild
horses, wild cattle, kan-
garoos, emus, dingoes,
kangaroo-rats —we barred
nothing, for, if he couldn’t
beat them for pace, he
would outlast them.
* * *
And then one day he
disappeared from the pad-
dock, and we never saw
him again. We knew
there were plenty of men
in the district who would
steal him, but, as we knew
also that there were plenty
more who would ‘ ‘inform”
for a pound or two, we
were sure that it could not
have been the local “ tal-
ent ” who had taken him.
We offered good rewards
and set some of the right
sort to work, but we
heard nothing of him
for about a year.
Then the surveyor’s
assistant turned up again,
after a trip to the interior.
He told us the usual
string of backbloclc lies,
and then wound-up by
saying that out on tne very fringe of settlement he
had met an old acquaintance.

On the Track.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
He was about the best type of sundowner I had
seen. I found him squatting under a tree at a
waterhole fronting Mt. Hutton. With the aid of
two glasses, he was cutting his hair close to the
scalp, leaving only the coating of dirt. His ex-
cuses for boots were bound together with green-
hide and wire; round his feet were pieces of
blanket which he called “Prince Alberts.”
“ What’s the next place ahead ? ” I asked him
“ Dunno.”
“ Well, what’s the manager’s name?”
“Dunno.”
He finished his bush-crop, and had a dry wash,
i.e., he wiped himself with an old shirt dirtier
than himself.
“ I never want to see bosses, let ’lone know
’em,” he remarked. “ All I want’s tucker. Gorra
match on yer?” He lit his pipe. “As for
names o’ places,” he resumed between puffs, “ C
gives ’em names o’ me own, ’cording to the treat-
ment I get there. Fust place yer come to’s
‘ Hardnut,’then yer’ll run agin’ ‘ Saltjunk,’ and,
if yer don’t get bogged on ‘ Stickfast Flat,’ yer’ll
hit ‘ Stinkin’-Stingy ’ safe enough. After that
there’s ‘ Greenhide - Codger,’ ‘ Pannikin - Dog, ’
‘Dust-bag,’ ‘Pinch-o’-Tea,’ ‘Hardcase,’ ‘Dead-
horse,’ ‘Skinflint,’ ‘Hungry-gut,’ ‘Pipe-o’-weed,’
< Meengy-beggar,’ and 50 more, from Bleedin’ Hill
to the bounds o’ Boggaroo.”
“You seem to have had bad luck,” 1 ventured.
“I dunno. I’m alive; and, luck or no luck, lit
get to where I’m goin’.”
“ Where’s that ? ”
“ ‘ Feg-out.’ ”
E.S.S.


[♦Note.— This very singular narrative occurs at the end
of a book of navigating notes found among the papers of
Capt. Close, who traded between Sydney and England
about the decade 1820-30, in command of the brigantine
Three Brothers, owned by Hutchinson and Sons, of
biverpool—a well-known firm in those days. The first
page is endorsed in the handwriting of Capt. Close,
"Obtained from a native of Easter Id., March, 1822.”
Capt. Close died in 1853, and the book—a solid old pocket-
book bound in leather and clamped with brass—came
into the possession of his daughter, Mrs L. M. Doric,
a widowed lady who is still living in Surry Hills’
Sydney. It was from her that I obtained it. The name
on the fly-leaf, “ Thomas Finlayson, mariner,” is neatly
written in the straggling fashion of the last century,
and the entries which follow —chiefly rough calculations
and personal notes—are all in the same hand, and in ink
now faded with age. The narrative at the close is shakily
written in ink or fluid of different color, but undoubtedly
*>y Finlayson. I have altered it only by omitting a few
obscurities and incoherencies. It closes abruptly as
Bhown.— Roderic Quinn.]

LEAH
FOR THE BULLETIN.
“My name-—or the name I have always
passed by—is Thomas Finlayson,* and I was born
in 1780, at Southampton, England. I sailed in
seven ships before meeting the adventures which
began with the sailing of the convict barque
from Plymouth. I was well-born and well-
schooled, but a wild lad always—smuggler at
one time, served under Nelson at another, helped
to loot Spanish ships in the Mediterranean, and
ran before the guns of English frigates on the high
-seas. I was wounded many times, but only three
occasions I remember—once at Trafalgar, when
our guns shot fire and death ; once by a Spanish
donna when I snatched a kiss ; and again when a
sallow Yankee ran me through in a boarding
encounter.
“Sept., 1814, we weighed anchor for New
Holland. I had shipped as third mate. We
bad with us a cargo of men and women
from English gaols, a pitiful crowd who knew just
;o much of the world as was ill for them. The
vomen were almost all ugly, withered creatures,
vith souls seas-under in malice. Almost all, I
slid, but there was an exception. Aye, and she
was a wonder. She had eyes that turned you
■ound at a cable’s length, and stood you still.
When they turned on you, you trembled hard.
They were grey, some said, and some said blue,
and many held they were hazel. I—who had seen
them night and day, and felt their mischief-
know they were all these and more. Her face was
colored a dusky warmth, paling in anger to a dull
ashiness. She was slim and lithe, and the quietest
nights she’d come and stand alongside you, and
her feet would make no more noise than a feather
falling on the sea. Her name was Leah. One
time the doctor called her by her prison number,
and she looked at him. For a week after he went
about plucking the collar of his coat and twitching
his lips. How she came amongst that abandoned
crowd Ido not know. She was dumb; it was
said she had never spoken. A convict told me
she had killed a lord.
“ ‘Killed him with a kiss,' I said, and laughed.
Afterwards I knew how near the truth I had
steered when I said these words.
“ Her presence grew upon me, and I became a
slave. God made me an ill man to my enemies,
but a faultless friend to my friends. Yet at this
woman’s wish I would have buried friend and
enemy in the same death, and the world with
them. When I tell you that everyone in the ship
was likewise fascinated, a glimmering of her
beauty will strike you, and something of her bright
eyes’ magic.
“A week after we sailed from England the
captain found her out. I cursed the captain, and
the first mate cursed him, and the second, and
every one of the crew. Yet he took Leah to his
cabin and held her there, and through storm and
calm went about like a man in dreams.
“He bore a charmed life, and it was needful.
One black night a dark bulk shoved him over, but
rising with a sea he caught a rope and hauled him-
self on board. Another time a marlinspike fell
from the shrouds and bruised his arm. Some
hand stabbed him in the shoulder while he slept,
but none would say whose hand. And yet again,
while he lay ill of this wound, the doctor gave him
arsenic in place of a sleeping-powder. But no
danger could tame him or chase him from the
convict woman’s side.
“ One sunset I saw more hate on men’s faces
than is good to see. The captain and the woman
stood on the poop, gazing at the west. The men
looked at the captain, and every face was ashen
grey. Not a foot moved, each mouth was sealed.
Yet when the captain drew the woman’s head
down, folded her in his arms, and kissed her red
lips, some turned away and choked and others
hissed oaths.
“We came at length to Sydney and landed the
convicts. With them went Leah. We watched
her walking away, throwing a glance now and
again at us, and a smile flickering on her lips.
“ Then within me yawned an emptiness, and my"
heart felt vacant. I was not alone in this : we
were all a changed and cheated crew. The cap-
tain went to and fro with a sullen gloom on his face.
The two mates talked in whispers and looked
towards the shore with wistful faces. Three times
the captain went to Governor Macquarie, golden
presents in his hand. On each occasion he re-
turned pale and angry. At last came the eve of
our departure. A strange nervousness attacked
us. Our breaths came quick, we paused with
irresolute feet, our eyes gazed into vacancy. We
seemed like men bereft of sense. When a man
asked one thing, his mate answered something
different. We were a ship of absent-minded offi-
cers and dreaming crew. Our minds had gone
astray from their moorings. We wereMrifting on
aTl n U \r C^ sea > and no man knew whither.
Next day, with a real west wind, we weighed
anchor, T-he sails filled, and the ship leapt away.
Ihe captain stood by the steersman, his teeth set,
Ins body bent forward. The shore was lined by
scai let soldiers and grey convicts. We passed
h ort Macquarie, and these vanished. One by one
we cleared the tree-clad points to starboard
and ran along the scrubby shore on our left. Then,
as we rounded a point and saw the ocean before
us, a sudden gun boomed down the waters, and a
flag floated from its staff. They were telling us
that a convict had escaped. We heeded neither
their gun nor flag, but held on beneath the lash of
that hot wind, a doomed crew with a stolen prize.
* * * *
“I write of witchery, or worse. No man
was on that ship but ate his heart—none but hated
his fellow. Whipped by the gale, the ship flew
east with a breaking sea at her heels. When at
last it fell calm we were many hundred leagues
from New Holland. And now began the first of
many horrors.
A moon of burnished copper stood above the
horizon. A long swell swayed the sea. The crew
loitered about with a suppressed breathing, and a
great sultriness was abroad. The creaking timbers
and the straining cordage alone broke the stillness.
I stood by the bulwarks, a hot forehead on my
hand. Somewhere there was wine for the hungry
heart within me. A thousand curses on the knaves
that stayed it from my lips ! Suddenly a cry rang
out:
“ ‘ The doctor, quick ! ’ He came and bent over
the captain. ‘ Apoplexy !’ he exclaimed.
‘‘ The officers exchanged glances. He turned
and read them.
“ ‘I am guiltless, before God,’ he said.
“ He opened a vein in the dead man’s arm, and
started back with a pale face. The blood was
thick as jelly and black as jet !
“ ‘Gentlemen,’ he cried, ‘if this be poison ’tis
a wondrous drug.’
“We sailed away, and Leah went to the first
mate’s side. On the fourth night he, too, .stag-
gered along the deck and fell dead.
“ The doctor came and opened a vein. Again
the blood was black and thick. Then he uttered
a fearful oath, caught up his sword, and made
straight for Leah, crying out that he should kill
her. But ere he reached her the second mate ran
in, and broke his forehead with an axe. We sewed
the two bodies in one shroud, loaded it with shot,
and sank them.
“And now birds in flocks and sharks in shoals
followed in our wake, smelling blood in the breath
we breathed. Day and night they darkened the
air and whitened the underwaters of the sea. The
sun set sullenly, the moon rose in crimson, and the
stars burned red. The sky and sea at all hours
were full of portents.
“A week after the second mate followed the first,
and each man knew that the prize went to the
strong of arm.
“PEOPLE WE PUT ON THE LAND.”
HE : “ They don't look very healthy , Emily. We might
try feeding them—perhaps the old hen hasn't
milk enough for them all.”
“ How the first stroke of the fight that followed
was struck I do not know. Two men swayed from
the captain’s cabin, locked in a death-grip, and a
third stabbed them both. Then each man found
a foe and set himself a task. When the sun set
many were wounded and more killed. Men that
I had sailed with for many years lay dead or
dying, and my arms were red. Such things will
man do for a woman.
“ Then came a flash of horror. I looked on that
red scene, and loathed the heart within me.
‘This ship,’ I said, ‘ will sail to hell.’
“In the dark I lowered a boat and provisioned
it while the dead were above deck and the living
below. I took the oars and pulled away. I had
taken but a few strokes when something splashed
from the ship’s side and swam towards me. I
rested on my oars and waited. It was Leah. A
great desire rose in my heart to beat her shapely
head and sink her from man’s sight for ever.
Instead, I leaned out, caught her in my arms, and
lifted her to my side. Between the desire and the
act she had raised her eyes.
* * * *
“ Late that night a little light sparkled
north of us. At first it was no bigger than a ruby
on a velvet pall. It grew at length till the sea about
“The dumb woman sat and watched with
untiring eyes."
it glowed crimson. A tressy blaze streamed
on the wind. Cries of men in dire urgency, tierce
laughter and drunken songs came across the sea.
Again and again my heart yearned to put about
and save them, but always on the point of action
I looked on Leah and my purpose failed. There
was something in her eyes that stabbed resolve.
“ While we watched, a light shot up the sky,
and the woman clapped her hands. The ship
belched smoke and sparks. Red fragments
spattered the darkness, and the sea flushed
angrily. Then came a long, low boom and dark-
ness, and I was alone with the woman.
* » * *
“ Of the sea we now sailed into, mariners
speak with bated breath. They have named it
the Desolate Region. It lies many leagues from
land As we entered its eastern limits, the birds
of the air turned on their course and deserted us.
The sharks refused to follow further. No sign of
life broke the glassy waters.
All day the sun burned dully
in the heavens. All night the
moon sent down a dim, dead
stare. But worse than these
was the silence that shrouded
that sea. No whale spouted,
no fish splashed. Only a soli-
tary man gazed into the dumb
creature’s eyes and drank chill
draughts. When at last the
wind came it blew from many
quarters. It sent our frail
boat hither and thither till
all reckonings were lost, and
so we sailed many days.
* * *
“Last night a strange
white wonder happened. Why
did God take me from my
fellows and portion me these
weird experiences? I had
been content to die as most
men —either by man’s hand
or the Death Angel’s unseen sword. To see
death as he now approaches is to gaze on a
veiled stranger. Last night the dumb creature
crept to my side. A white moon stood in the
zenith, and the ghost of a star beside it. The
sea slept. I took the woman’s face in my
hands, and kissed her lips. Her magical eyes
gazed into mine, chaining my soul and choking
my breath. It seemed I had never loved before.
And yet deep within me an angry voice was
calling me to kill her. As I held her there
it seemed as if a sudden gust from many graves
blew in my nostrils. It was as though the dead
had opened their mouths and laughed. I
rose and looked round. My hands trembled,
my knees knocked, and I shook the woman
off. I gazed across the sea with a beating
heart. In the west many faces looked out upon
me. Grave, grey faces were they, ranged on a
white island, and staring through the night.
“ ‘ This is the Land of Death,’ I said ; ‘ for this
I was bom.’
“ Then I folded my arms and bowed my head.
But the woman would not let me be. She wound
her arms around my neck. I called on her to go,
and cursed her ; for little doubt had I that she had
brought me to this pass. She did not heed me,
but drew my head upon her lap. Her fingers
trembled through my hair. She
bent down till her hot breath
touched my neck, and then she
kissed.
“ I shoved her from me and
sprang up. Where she kissed
was as hot as fire. Her lips
were red as glowing embers and
sharp as a dagger’s point. She
crept away and crouched in the
boat’s stern, and watched me
with wild, bright eyes. The
blood raced through my veins
and a heat Hushed me.
Then, on a sudden, a cold
wind seemed to blow
through my bones and
chill their marrow.
After came a gentle
warmth and a desire
to sleep. Through
all these changes
the dumb woman
sat and watched
with untiring eyes
and a smile of
strange meaning.
‘‘ The mo o n
slipped down the
sky till she lay on
the horizon. Her
white round face peered
between the great, grey
faces in the west. I beheld
the scene with dreamy eyes,
knowing that death was nigh.
“ I sleep, and a thousand things rub
nettled skins against me. I wake, if it can be
called waking, to go from sleep to stupor, and a
hissing laughter and a rustling as of many leaves
fall faintly on my ears.
* * * *
“ Now it is clear to me how the dead men
died. The woman kissed them and their blood
thickened. Fitter for her a dank, dead wood, dark
misty caverns, and unsunned places, than a strong
man s arms and a beating heart. Better that
those eyes should glimpse behind green glistening
jungle than thus through brown tresses lure warm
flesh and blood to doom.

Othello: a Sack-Blocks Version,
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
We had camped, and had been talking of books
and the different characters pourtrayed in them.
“Ever read Shakespeare?” he enquired, as we
rolled together a couple of small logs, preliminary
to lighting a fire.
“ Oh, yes ! ” I replied ; “ Shakespeare was a pet
study of mine for years.”
He seemed to get interested.
“ He’s a good ’un.”
“ Not too bad,” I said.
“ That there lago was a bad egg.”
My thoughts recurred to Othello’s Ancient.
“ Yes ; lago was a bit of a villain.”
“A villain!” he muttered, as he took off his
hat and fanned some embers into flame. “He
was a fair brute, that cove was. See how he
kidded the darkie he was acting ‘ square ’ to him,
when all the time ’e was tryin’ to do ’im.”
Then he paused and looked at me.
“ I’ve met blokes like ’im ‘ out back ’ —out
back ! ” He spoke slowly, and, apparently, feel-
ingly.
I looked at him.
Then he continued : “I think that darkie cove
was square enough, and the girl, too —the one he
cleared out with an’ spliced. But that there lago
was a cow —a fair cow ! Like it strong ? This
’ere’s good tea.”
I was thinking of the fair Desdemona.
“Not too strong,” I said.
N.S.W.
F.S.
Day and Night.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Day goeth bold in cloth of gold,
A royal bridegroom he ;
But Night in jewelled purple walks—
A Queen of Mystery.
Day filleth up his loving-cup
With vintage golden-clear;
But Night her ebon chalice crowns
With wine as pale as Fear.
Day drinks to Life, to ruddy Life,
And holds a kingly feast.
Night drinks to Death; and while she
drinks —
Day rises in the East!
They may not meet ; they may not greet ;
Each keeps a separate way :
Day knoweth not the stars of Night,
Nor Night the Star of Day.
So runs the reign of Other Twain.
Behold ! the Preacher saith
Death knoweth not the Light of Life,
Nor Life the Light of Death !
V. J. DALEY.


"Clinched!"
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The south-east gale, tearing madly in upon
the desolate stretch of t lr Ninety-Mile Beach,
drove the stinging rain invo her cheek, and dimly,
through the drift and darkness, she cpuld see the
ghostly ridges of the surf that pounded out a
massive bass to the fiend-like shriekings of the
wind. “ Like the voices of lost souls ! ” she said,
with a shudder, as she ploughed wearily up the
sand-hill towards the light that was her beacon—
whether of hope or despair she knew not yet.
By one of those coincidences so strange as
almost to compel disbelief in their fortuity, the
man and the woman were talking of her as she
crept shivering to the door of the weather-beaten
wooden cottage, half-buried, on its windward
side, in sand.
“ On such a night as this, now?” asked the hus-
band—a large, grey man, bush-bearded, and with
kind, sad eyes under shaggy eyebrows.
“ Yes ; even on such a night as this ! ” the wife
answered, with a resolute constriction of her thin,
pale lips. Her face, too, was thin and pale, and
square, and rigidly well-featured; the face, in
short, of a good woman in the worst sense of the
term.
A fiercer gust than common took up the
unrelenting spirit of her speech ; then, in the
succeeding lull, there fell upon the door a
small, faint knocking, like the tapping of a
sparrow’s beak. The man’s startled eyes, as
he looked round suddenly at his wife, said
plainly enough that he believed her test had
come, and as plainly her eyes answered that
she was ready. But all her tongue said was
“ Open ! ”
The wind dashed in and put the lamp out
before they had more than a glimpse of
a white face backed by the outer black-
ness, but the man, putting forth a
gentle hand in the dark, drew some-
thing softly in and closed the door.
“ Ah ! ” was all the woman said,
as she turned again from the relighted
lamp and saw the kneeling figure
just within the threshold. And she
made no move as her husband lifted
into a chair the draggled and storm-
beaten creature that had been her
daughter. Not his ; he was her second
husband. But it was upon him the wide,
wild eyes turned in an agony of question-
ing, and his were the answering words.
“ Poor soul! —poor soul ! It was the
right thing for you to do. Better late than
never. Better late”—he repeated, holding
the forlorn young head against him as
hi turned towards the watching woman-
“ than never. Say it, Dorcas ! Say it,
you too, for God’s sake! ”
She shook her head—the slow, cold shake
of dogged pertinacity. “ Better ” —her hus-
band’s shaggy brows went up—“never!”—
they fell again into a frown.
“Your own daughter, woman!” he said,
letting his big, rough hand slide gently from
the rain-drenched hair to the worn and
pallid cheek. “ Your daughter—good God ! —
not mine ! ”
An honorable tear travelled slowly down his
rugged nose, poised for a moment, and fell warm
upon the girl’s cold hand. It was but a single
drop, but it thawed in an instant the frozen
fountain of the wanderer’s weeping. She clung
suddenly and desperately to his side. “Yes,
yours ! —yours ! —yours ! ” she repeated between
her rapid sobs. “ Though it seems—I am no
longer —anybody else’s ! ”
“ She is right! ” the woman answered to the
man’s unspoken appeal. “ I have no daughter ! —
whatever you may choose to call her ! ”
The stepfather, with one hand on the girl’s frail
shoulder, shook the other in the air.
“ By God ! Dorcas,” he said, slowly, “if you’re
a Christian, I’m glad I’m the heathen you’re al-
ways callin’ me.”
“ You needn’t blaspheme ! Thou shalt not take
the name ”
“Ay, ay— I know ! But, never mind texts just
now —unless you can find me one to justify eastin’
off your own flesh an’ blood.”
“ And if thy right hand offend thee, cut ”
“ To be sure ; to be sure! I might ha’ known
you’d find Scripter for to back you up in—well, in
a crime—that’s just my opinion of it.”
The woman sniffed contemptuously as she walked
to a chair and sat down. “ The opinion of the
ungodly ! ” she said.
“ So be it,” the man said, with a sudden change
of tone. “ But, at all events, it’s the ungodly’s
house—an’ the girl’s got to stay.”
He threw some wood on the fire, and clumsily
put the kettle down to boil. Then he pointed to
the inner room. “ Go in there, my dear, an’ square
yourself up a bit. How far’ve you come ? ” he
added, looking at the sodden boots, as she got un-
steadily to her feet.
“ From Yallarang,” the girl said, with a scared
glance at the sphinx-like figure on the chair.
I came that far in the train, and then I followed
the beach. ”
“Poor soul !” the man muttered again, with his
head in a cupboard. “ Eleven mile ! In wet sand !
On such a night! ”
The woman watched with
scornful disapproval the blun-
dering incompleteness of
his unaccustomed prepara-

tions for supper, till at last, when he took down a
frying-pan, she got up suddenly and all but
snatched it out of his hand. Without a word
he stood aside. It was a good sign, he thought.
“She’ll come round all right!” he told himself,
feeling reflectively at his grizzled whisker. “ She
must have a mother’s heart somewhere about her ! ”
But the woman, stooping over the fire, divined
his thoughts.
“You ain’t to think,” she said, without looking
up, “ that I’ve changed my mind ! But it just
made me sick to see you fumblin’ around. As for
Oar—that girl, I mean —you can do as you like !
But I know what /’ll do !”
‘ ‘ Gall her Carrie, Dorcas ! —call her Carrie, as
you were goin’ to. And, as for what you’ll do-
just take thought to yourself what you will do, if
you drive her away again !”
“How can I drive her away? The house, as
you say, is yours !”
“ Ay ! —I can give her a roof. But she won’t
live long under it as a stranger to her own mother!
Come now, Dorcas !—you was a girl once, your-
self ! Just put yourself in her place !” —and the
man laid his hand on his wife’s emblematically
angular shoulder.
She shook it off. “You’re makin’matters no
better by supposin' such a thing ! ” she said angrily.
“ And the roof’s all the likes of her can expect!
If it ain’t enough, she can go !”
He stooped his mouth close to her ear.
‘ ‘ What, woman ? Go ? To the streets of Mel-
bourne, most likely ! Shame on you, Dorcas, I
say ! —shame on you ! ”
If she felt any shame, her face gave no sign of
it. “That’s what she’s come from, as like as
not! ” she answered, as she put the chop-laden pan
on the fire, and the man, with a puzzled kind of
disgust on his face, looked down at her for half-a-
minute before he spoke again.
“ Well —even say ’twas so ! Didn’t Christ for-
five that woman in the Bible? Mary—Mary
lacdougall, or something like that! ”
The woman said nothing of his blunder. She
knew well enough who was meant, and just then
the daughter came timidly back to the room—the
piteous ghost of a fair-haired girl of eighteen or
nineteen. The stepfather gently placed her at the
table and sat down beside her.
It was a silent meal, save for the tumult of the
storm without. The mother kept her eyes on her
plate. The girl stole furtive glances at her. The
man thought it best to say nothing and let things
work. But, presently, noticing the rain had
ceased, it struck him that it would be a good thing
to leave these two alone together for a time. “I
may be only spoilin’ things, after all! ” he thought.
“ Women’s hard things to reckon up ! ”
He got up and filled his pipe. “ Think I’ll have
a look at the weather,” he said, and stepped out
into the darkness.
His tobacco didn’t last long in the furious wind,
but for half an hour he tramped to and fro beside
the thundering surf, puzzling himself over ques-
tions—sexual, sociological, and religious—with
which vastly more sophisticated intelligences have
grappled to no better purpose. Then again the rain
came driving in from seaward, and he went back
to the house.
A sudden fear seized him as he saw that his wife
was alone, and—evil omen in her case —engrossed
with those Old Testament ferocities, which are
the utter negation of the spirit of the New.
“ Where’s Carrie ?” he asked, sharply, and his
wife, without looking up from her Bible, pointed
to the inner room. “ Oh, that?” he said, relieved,
but standing yet with hat on head and the rain
sparkling on his beard. “ Best thing, too —she
was dead-beat. Have you ” —he softened his voice
to a half-whisper—“ have you spoke to her,
Dorcas ? ”
“No!”
“ No ? ” He pulled his penthouse brows together
—then relaxed them. “Time,” he told himself,
“give her time, old man—that’s the only chance.”
“Where are you goin’ to sleep?” the woman
asked, looking suddenly up at him when she found
that he said no more.
He pointed to a sofa that was the most
luxurious article in the room.
“ Very well,” His wife closed her lips and her
book with a snap.
“ I’m goin’ to bed.”
The man, left
alone, betook him-
self to the pipe
o f consideration.
“It’s the best
way,” he told him-
self, as he smoked,
“she’ll be for-
given before mornin’.” Then he locked the door
and put the key in his pocket.
* * * *
Lying by the broken wanderer, the cold,
hard pietist struggled long and fiercely with the
instinct which, even in her, would not be entirely
silenced. But the combination of iron pride with
hide-bound holiness was too strong for it, and at
last the mother slept, without having uttered the
word for which the wretched daughter vainly
waked and waited. And the- gale—growing
fiercer as the night went on—shrieked devilish joy.
For an hour or two the miserable girl lay shud-
dering and listening ; then, suddenly, but with
the quiet deliberation of despair, she arose and
dressed herself, haphazard, in the dark. Then for
a moment she stood irresolute. “No,” she said to
herself, “ I will not kiss her, ” and stepped noise-
lessly into the outside room.
The lamp burned low. The old man on the
sofa slept with one large knotty hand hanging to
the floor. He didn’t stir when the girl lightly
kissed his corrugated forehead.
“Good-bye!” she whispered. “You would
have saved me if you could.”
Then she went to his coat, hanging over a chair-
back, for she knew, of old, where he was wont to
put the key. But the wind, as she let herself softly
out, snatched the door from her hand, and a bang
that shook the rafters shook also both the sleepers
into sudden consciousness. Then, to the man —■
sitting up and staring about him—appeared fran-
tically the white-robed woman.
“She’s gone, William —she’s gone! And, oh,
my God ! I never opened my lips to her. Quick !
—the water ! —the water ! ”
“ Murderess ! ” rose to the man’s lips, but didn’t
pass them. It was the time for action, not for
words, and in a moment he was out amongst the
sand-hills, staring this way and that in the hope-
less gloom. Then he collected himself and ran
down towards the mocking chorus of the sea. It
was mockery in vain, though, for as he rushed he
caught sight of a small darkness upon the faint
glimmer of the sand.
It was she ! Midway between the mother who
had spurned and the waters that waited to wel-
come, she had dropped out of consciousness, and
lay, face downwards, under the drenching rain.
And to the old man, as he picked up the draggled
heap, came an inspiration.
“I’ll clinch this,” he muttered, and, with the
girl on his strong old shoulder, ploughed his
weighted way back to the cottage. But he didn’t
go in. “ There wouldn’t have been time,” he said
to himself, as, upon the seaward side, he laid his
burden upon the heaped-up sand, and stood with
his face set sternly to the drifting rain.
Three or four minutes he waited thus before he
picked up his load again, walked round to the
front, and laid the soaking figure before the
waiting woman—shaken suddenly, at last, out of
the frozen moralist into the frantic mother.
“ Dead ! ” she said, with a quietness more agony-
fraught than any shrieking. “ Drowned ! And I
never opened my lips to her.”
The man watched her keenly from beneath his
bristly brows. He suffered with her suffering,
but again he told himself that he must clinch this
thing.
“ Well,” he said, with as good an imitation of
cynicism as was possible to a nature of coarse gold
—“ you said ‘ better never ! ’ you know. An’ now
it is never, an’ no mistake.”
She stepped out at him with tortured eyes and
clutching hands.
“ Man —man ! ” she said, “ what can you know
of a mother’s heart ? ”
“ More’n she does herself, apparently,” he said
with a sudden jubilance that made the miserable
mother start away as from a whip-stroke. But
then, with the acuteness of accelerated emotion,
she snatched his meaning from his eyes.
“ Not drowned ? ” she gasped, and fell on her
knees beside the girl—already opening the eyes of
hopelessness.
“ Drowned ? No—not much ! Never been in
any water but the rain.” He brought a bottle
from the cupboard. “There, give her some o’
that, hot an’ strong; an’ get her clothes off. I’m
goin’ to leave you together for the present.”
With a smile, he rekindled the universal com-
forter, put his coat on over his wet shirt, and
went outside. The wind yelled at him and the
rain smote him, but he smoked imperturbably on,
saying softly to himself at intervals, “ Clinched I
clinched ! ”
ALEX. MONTGOMRRY.
A Christmas Idyll.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Scene —Bouverie-street, Carlton, Yic.
Passing down that somewhat squalid street the
day before Christmas, about 3 p.m., I saw half-a-
dozen barefooted, dirty-faced urchins, who were
gravely discussing Christmas and its joys.
“ I don’t know if I’ll get anything from Daddy
Christmas,” said the largest girl; “I didn’t last
year. ”
“ I’ve got sunthin’ already,” said the urchin they
called May, probably after the Duchess of York.
“What is it, May?” _
“ Look ! ” she said, with much glee and pride.
I looked, too, inquisitive as to her Christmas-box.
It was a cat —a dead cat —which had evidently
dried to a mummy on some adjacent land. This
cat the poor little beggar was nursing with as
much pride and enjoyment as if it were a guinea
doll.
“Let’s have a nurse, May, will you? ” pleaded
the biggest girl. »
“Just a little while,” said May.
And this was a Bouverie-street Christmas !
STEELE GREY.
Star and Star.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
You have crossed my life with your fair, sweet face.
You are filling my lone heart’s vacant place;
Your whispered words and your arms that cling
Are a link in Love’s remembering.
For your forms are alike as the angels are,
Your faces are moulded as star and star.
I have taken your hand that is soft to take,
I have held it long for an old love’s sake ;
I have played with the curls of your golden head,
As I played with gold curls in a day long dead.
And your rose-red lips I have tasted seem
Like the red-rose lips of a fading dream.
I held her once in my arms of old,
I kissed her twice, but her lips were cold;
I clasped you close to my heart to-night,
A heaving bosom of snowdrift white ;
But there’s never a snow could quench the fire
That burned in your passionate lips’ desire.
With the pleading words ’tis a wrong to name
You are holding your brow for the brand of shame?
There is never a pleasure I could not prove
In the name of the passion you think is Love :
There is never a freedom I might not take
But—l spare you, girl, for an old love’s sake.
I could blacken the fame you hold so cheap
While moonbeams sorrow and red stars weep,
But—l drop your hand though it give you pain,
I dream no dream on your lips again ;
What reck if your heart like mine shall break !
T will spare your soul for her white soul’s sake.
W.H.O.


Collop's Mother .
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
He was an ancient mariner, and of the old
school, a fellow-passenger on s.s. Hauroto, Auck-
land to Sydney. We foregathered, refreshed to-
gether (he didn’t take water in his), and subse-
quently conversed of many things—the way to
set a foretopmast stunsail ship-shape and Bristol
fashion, religion, the superiority of sail to steam,
education, blackbirding (he had been there), and,
lastly, filial affection.
“ I ain’t wot you’d call religious,” he remarked,
“ but I believes in a religious eddieation. I don’t
hold with this yer new-fangled sekkilar system.
I’ve knocked about permiscuous, an’ I says early
religious trainin’ is a first-chop thing. As for
what you calls filliul affeckshun, I don’t take no
stock much in fathers—mine brought me up on
holystone and rope’s-end, dern him !—but no chap’s
any good onless he’s allfired fond of his mother.
You take it from me.”
-1 acquiesced.
“ ever come across Bob Collop—Captain
Collop, that is ? ” he asked with seeming irrele-
vance.
I intimated that I had not the voluptuous plea-
sure of Mr. Collop’s acquaintance.
“ Well, he’s a r-unnin’ down to the Islands just
now, in the Martha and Emily schooner. Years
ago me and him was sailin’ our own schooners on
the Chinee coast, Ningpo to Shanghai. Lots of
Australian chaps was on that lay then. Well,
Collop he was an axample of early pious trainin’ an’
filliul affeckshun, an’ no bloomin’ error. No, there
wasn’t many as could touch Collop ! ”
I hinted that the recountal of the manifold
virtues of Collop might prove a means of grace to
myself. This is the story of Collop—his piety and
filial affection:—

My name's Joful—John Joful. Collop
was a saint, if ever was one. But you under-

wern’t any—not at sea, at'least—them days. He’d
take his tot reg’lar and his religion didn’t show out
startling, only about onee every four months,

I knowed when he had one, for he’d go off on a
reg’lar religious rampage, so to speak. We would
be lyin’ in the river and one of his China sailors’d

orderin’ him around like as if he was a Christian
converted Chinkie, and disremembering as Collop
was master mariner, and he was only four foot
odd, and Collop six foot and inches. An’ when
Collop begins talkin’ about his poor old mother the
little Methodee says:
“ I don’t want for to ’ear that rubbidge. Pay me
ten dollars, then I’ll give you some medicine.”
Collop’s face turned kinder queer, but he planks
the dollars, and the missionary he lugs out three
powders in white paper, and says, a-curlin’ his nose:
“ You’ll take one of these now, one at eight, and
one if you can’t sleep.”
Says Collop—soft as a sou’-east trade :
‘ ‘ Which will I take now ? I’ll take ’em for my
poor old mother’s sake.”

self. What would a gent of your figger take and
sign articles to be my regular chaplain ? ”
“ This fellow’s drunk and a fool,” says the
other, heavin’ his eyes aloft.
An’ I see his ’and (Collop’s ’and) go softly,
slowly behind him, and afore I could move Collop’s
arm comes round dragging a great rug from off
his bunk, and quicker’n you could splice the main-
brace he had dumped the gospel-grinder in the

and ties up the rug with parson inside just as you

He turns to the bundle of missionary, opens the

“ Chook ! Chook ! Chook ! ” Like as if he’d a

r l iien he made his chaplain take all them three

“ Chaplain, pray for me ! ”
An’ the little chap begins to pray like mad, but
whether for hisself or Collop I couldn’t make out.
After a time Collop peeps into the parcel again an'

“Pray for my poor old mother, chaplain ! ”
He prayed.
“ Sing a hymn, chaplain ! ”
He sang.
“Preach me a sermon, chaplain. Lots o”ellr
fire in it, please.”
He preached.
But by-an’-by Collop would call him to prayers
less frequent, and, at last, his head falls back an’
he dosses off like a tired hinfant. Down I dives,
chucks the pistol through the port, out knife, an :

never did see. I gives him a stiff second-mate’s
R*P four fingers up, believe me—an’ passes the

Collop wakes up, fresh as paint. “ A just man
made perfeck am I, Joful, you drunken old sin-

But it all showed the goodness of his heart,

know, if Collop hadn’t a had them yer things, he’d
’ave killed that missionary—what do you say?
But not he, not Collop. He just made that little
cove pray and preach for him like the Levide ’»

Let’s have a tot, a soldier’s supper and turn in
Sydney.

Wansbury's Second Wife .
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ Nothing but her; her, her! Oh, yes, I know
you don’t talk of her, but it’s her you’re thinking
of. I was good enough to take up with to look
after her child, but now the kid’s dead, too, I’m tc
be nothing. Nothing, am I? ” Her voice rose tc
a hoarse shriek.
Wansbury sighed. Though only a surfaceman,
he was yet conscious of inherent gentlemanliness :
but this creature would yet drag him to her own
level. She could not forgive his fits of abstrac-
tion ; she had no generosity, no delicacy. A coarse,
bad-tempered woman, that was Wausbury’s second
wife.
“ Struck down by the buffer ! ”
“ Stand back, he’s dying; can’t live ten
minutes. ”
“ Look out, here’s his wife! ”
She came stumbling across the railway-line, hei
draggled skirt blown back from her shapeless
figure, her big red face sodden with crying. There
was something half-hidden in her hand.
The dying man seemed to shrink into himself as
she knelt clumsily beside him.
“ Charley ! ” Her hot breath was on his face.
“ I’ve brought you something—her portrait. I’m
behind you, Charley, where you can’t see me.
Can you see her ? I'm holding it before you ; I’ll
hold it there till—till ”
And the coarse hand did not falter till the
glazing eyes of the dying man closed for ever.

O. CLYDE.


Nikoa.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A white man, thin, brown-skinned, and
ragged, was walking along the reef at Henuake,
one of the Low Archipelago. In his hand he
carried a turtle-spear, and every now and then he
would examine the deep pools that, at intervals,
broke the hollow crust of the reef. Behind him,
carrying a basket, came his native wife.
The tide was very low, and the outer edge of
the black wall of reef, covered on the top with
patches and clumps of round yellow and pink
“ WHAT BIT TEE OTHER ONE?"
NEW-CHUM " I have never seen such peculiar
horses befawoah. Such a queeah cullah,
both of them, don’t you know ? especially
the grey!”
MR. JUMBUCK: “ The horse with all the spots
on him is what we call a flea-bitten grey.

markable! But, bai Jahve, what bit the

coral knobs, had dried, and under the fierce sun-
rays a sickening odor arose from the countless
marine-growths and organisms.
Presently the white man sat down upon a weed-
covered boulder on the brink of a pool, and waited
for the woman to come up.
* * * *
The man’s name was Falkiner, and he was
about the poorest beach-comber in the group.
Not many years ago he had been a different man ;
but he had made money fast in those days, and as
fast as he made it he had spent it in drunken
orgies at Auckland, Papiete, and Honolulu. Then
his luck turned, and from being a man of might
and substance and the owner of two pearling-
schooners he had sunk to living on Henuake,
planting cocoanuts for an American firm. Fifteen
months before they had landed him and his wife
and four native laborers, and about twenty thou-
sand seed-cocoanuts. Telling him to be careful of
his provisions, and that the schooner would be
back again in six months, the captain had sailed
away. ,
The woman came up, and, taking the
basket off her shoulders, sat down beside him.
For a while neither spoke. The man was tired
and savage, and the woman knew his mood too
well to speak until she was spoken to. Away on
either hand stretched the black waste of reef ; m
front, the oily, glassy ocean with here and there a
flock’of snow-white sea-birds meandering on the
wing or floating on its smooth surface ; and shore-
wards the long low line of verdure fringed by the
dazzling white beach. . .
“Show me,” said Falkiner, pointing to the
basnet. .
Nikoa opened the basket and showed him a
young turtle of about 201b. weight.
“That will do us, Nikoa, for a day or two.
Perhaps the kau puaka will see now that I want
nothing from them. ”
Now by kau puaka (crew of pigs) the white man
meant his native laborers, with whom he
had quarrelled. When his provisions ran
out—and especially his liquor—and they all
had to live upon native food, his morose
temper soon caused a breach, and it had
gradually widened day by day till at last
the white man and his wife had to seek
their own food.
“ Harry,” said the woman,
“why use such bitter words ? These
men of Raroia are quick-blooded, and
who is to know of it should thy hot
tongue bring death upon us two sud-
denly in the night ? ”
“What do you know, what have
you heard ? ” he asked suspiciously.
“This,” she answered quickly:
“but two days ago two of them came
to me and asked would I go with them
in the boat and seek some other island,
for they are wearied of living here
and getting naught but foul words
from the white man.”
The ragged man looked savagely at
her for a moment, then snarled :
“Well, you sad-faced devil, you can
o-u. I don’t want you any more, curse
The woman’s eyes flashed tire: “ that is the
devil in thee that speaks because it calleth for
more grog. Now, listen. It will be well for thee
to be friendly with these men ; for they are four
to us two ; and they have the boat; and they have
money—much money.”
“ Money,” said Falkiner ; where could they
get money on Henuake ? ” and he laughed incredu-
lously.
Then she told him.
* * » *
Two days before the four natives had been
searching for robber-crabs in a dense puka scrub,
when they had found, lying on the ground, a
boat’s water-breaker. One of them had taken
hold of it to lift it up, and found it to be too
heavy As he placed his hands under each end,
the bottom gave way and a great mass of silver
coin poured out in a heap, ihey each set to work
and made four strong baskets. Into these they
divided the money, and hid it away, i hen they
consulted. , . „ ., « A
“ Let us tell the white man,” said one, and
when he sees all this money he will wait here on
Henuake no longer, but take it and us away with
him to Raroia. ” ,
“ No ” said the others, ‘ let us hide it until the
schooner comes back for us. We can steal it on
board at night-time. Why tell the white man ? He
will keep it and perhaps kill us.”
Put they had told Nikoa, and she had urged
them to let her tell the white man—the money
,vas his, she argued. Were they not his men ? Was
he not a good man to them until all his liquor was
o-one ?—only then had he become sour and moody.
And so they let it rest with her. And now she
told him. . „
u You’re a good girl, Nikoa, said tne
white man, pleasantly. “ We 11 take the boat
and go back to Raroia and let Henuake take care
of itself. Tell the men we will divide the money
evenly—and let us be friends again.”
Nikoa smiled. Loyalty to her husband was
always her first thought, and she thought with
delight of her home at Garumaoa on Raroia —
the village of her childhood, where she was
as happy as the day was long. A year
and a-half ago Falkiner
had bought her from the
chief, and with unques-
tioning obedience she
had followed him to
lonely Henuake. He
was occasionally a great
brute to her, yet, al-
though she was sicken-
ing to return to her
people, she had no
thought of doing so
without the white man.
They rose and walked
back to the line of
palms on the beach —
the woman laughing and
talking joyously and the
man planning black treachery.
In another hour the four brown men had come
baqjc to his house, each carrying his basket-load
of silver dollars. .They were emptied on a mat
and counted out in piles of hundreds. There were
over four thousand dollars. Falkiner divided it
into six shares—one for each of the men, and one
for Nikoa.
Then each brown man tied his share up in a
piece of cloth and handed it to the white man—to
mmd till they got back to Raroia.
“ Whose money was it before? ” they asked him
that night as they sat in his house smoking.
He shook his head. It was mostly in American
dollars and half-dollars and Chilian half-dollars.
He had heard of some human remains being found
in Henuake long years ago, and that a whaleship
had been lost there some time about 1850. Per-
haps the money had come from her—whaleships in
those days often carried as much as five thousand

Then the men went away to their own hut to
sleep, and Nikoa, the woman, slept too.
When he was sure she slumbered soundly,
Falkiner carefully examined and loaded his Colt’s
revolver, and placed it in the chest with the
dollars.
* * * *
At daybreak they pulled out of the quiet
lagoon and headed for Raroia. It was calm, and
the day became hot, yet the four men paddled un-
wearyingly all day, with but short intervals of rest.
At dusk a faint air sprang up, and they hoisted
the sail.
“ Sleep, strong men, sleep,” said the white man,
“ Nikoa and I will steer by turns till it be dawn.”
The four natives lay down. The one who was
pulling the bow-oar was a lad named Te Rangi, a
cousin of Nikoa. As he coiled his body into the
confined space where he lay Nikoa threw him a
mat to keep off the chilly night air. Then she
slept also.
• * * *
Suddenly two shots pealed out over the
ocean, and, as the woman sprang up ter-
rified, a third. There, under the first
flush of the rising sun, she saw
three of her countrymen
lying either dead or
dying, and Fal-
kiner pointing
his pistol at
the mat-cover-
ed figure in
the bows.
She seized
his arm. “Harry,
’tis Te Rangi,
my brother. Let
him live ? ” He
shook her off
with a savage
curse, and fired.
The bullet broke
the arm of the
sleeping lad, who
sat up and gazed
in terror at the
savage face of the
white man.
Again the woman caught
his hand and begged for
the boy’s life. He was but
a lad, she urged ; he was,
too, of her own blood — how
then could he betray ? There was
land not far distant. Let him have
his life now and he could be landed
there.
But Falkiner again thrust her aside,
and, this time, sent a bullet through Te
Rangi’s heart.
The dead men had been thrown overboard
and Falkiner had changed the boat’s course to
W. by N. He would get to Samoa, he thought.
Once there, he need have no fear. He had spared
the woman’s life simply because he knew that she
would never betray him. As soon as she got over
losing Te Rangi she would be all right again and
find her tongue.
A few miles ahead of the boat was a cluster of
low islands, uninhabited all but one. Falkiner
knew them well, and presently he said to the
woman:
“ Nikoa, we will sleep on Napuka to-night, and
in the morning get as many young oocoanuts as
we can for the boat, and perhaps we may find a
turtle on the beach. Then we go to Samoa.”
She nodded her head. The wild hatred of the
man that now filled her heart kept her from
speaking.
* * * *
The current in Napuka Passage was run-
ning out fiercely, and the boat could scarcely
make headway against it, even with the strong
S.E. trade filling her big sail.
“ Let us make fast to the edge of the coral till
the tide turns,” said the white man, and he let the
boat’s head fall off a little, at the same time stand-
ing up to get a better view.
But a sudden whirling eddy brought the boat
up against a great knob of coral, and Falkiner lost
his balance with the shock and fell over the side.
When he rose to the surface he was a hundred
feet away from the boat, which had swung round
head-to the current, but was hard and fast on the
coral knob.
“Push her off, Nikoa,” he called out, “else I
am taken out to sea. ”
Nikoa stood up and laughed. “ Even so, killer
of my brother! The heavy box of money, for which
thou sheddest the blood of four men, keeps the
boat firm on the rock. It is a judgment on thee.”
Above the roaring, hissing and swirling of the
water her voice reached him, and, still struggling
madly to gain the sides of the passage, he was
borne out into the blue depths beyond.
Then Nikoa, shielding her eyes from the sun
with one hand, saw a splash in the water, and
THAT'S WHERE HE GAME IN:
MRS. BLOOMER : “ George! ”
GEORGE : “ Yes, my dear!”
MRS. B. : “When you've finished your
