me-I’ve lost that tiresome stud again!”
heard a faint cry of agony, and knew that a shark
had taken the murderer.
* * * *
The boat soon lay high and dry on the
coral knob, and Nikoa, lighting a cigarette, sat
and smoked awhile. Then she took out the
baskets of coin, and cut the mouths open and
poured the blood-stained money into the wildly
sweeping waters. When the last one was emptied,
she lay and slept till the tide turned.
When she awoke the boat was drifting in quietly
to the land, and a canoe full of light-skinned men
with strong, wiry beards and moustaches was
alongside.
“ Who are you ? ” said one.
“Nikoa of Raroia,” she answered. “My man
fell out of the boat and was eaten by a shark.
What men are ye?”
“ We be of Tetopoto,” they replied, pointing to
the farthest island; “had there been men with
you we had killed them. Rut we will not hurt a
woman.”
* * * *
For years afterwards the children of Na-
puka and Tetopoto,would find silver money in the
holes and pools of the reef.
LOUIS BECKE.
Old Jim.
A PATHETIC TALE OF BUSH VERACITY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
We was camped away back on the river, above
Menindie, doing a bit of fencin’. Prises was all
right and we was as merry as locusts.
There was seven of us and none in the church-
yard. 1
Sittin’ around the camp-fire one night after the
tucker was stowed and with our pipes going strong,
Old Bill starts to piteh about using of the stock-
whip.
“ I mind,” said Bill, “ going across country with
a big mob o’ cattle from Darling Downs to Mel-
bourne. I weren’t too slow with the whip in them
days, but, Lord bless yer, boys, I weren’t in it
with Old Jim and Stringy Bark. If Jim rounded
up a mob and did a bit of cuttin’-out you’d all say
it couldn’t be equalled, let alone beat. But later
you’d see Stringy turning ’em when they bolted
and then you’d wonder which was best man. It
was all settled one fine day, and never did you see
two such ringers as them two.”
“ We had run the mob down on a arm of land
'tween the ’Bidgee and the Billybong, and yarded
'em. There was plenty of water and plenty of
feed, and we reckoned we’d camp for a few days
aid do a bit of washing.
“The second day we was lying around smokin’
and chaffin’ when Stringy—’e fancied ’imself—
gets a bit flash with his whip, and real pretty it
were to watch him. Presently ’e picks out a big
gum, with nice, smooth bark, and writes his gal’s
name, standing 17 foot away and cuttin’ it beauti-
ful—better’n he could with a pen. Then he swings
the whip free like and cuts ‘ Post Orfice, Bal-
ranald.’
“We all reckoned Old Jim was done bad, but
the game wasn’t over by a lot. Old Jim, who had
been strokin’ his bit o’ leather affectionate-like,
starts and cuts a envelope round the name
and address and puts on two stamps—
N.S.W. twopennies. All of us calculated
that Stringy was clean wiped out, but it
warn't so. He comes up again, flourishes h s
whip, crosses out the stamps, and puts on the post-
mark and date. We goes now and gets sorry for
Jim, but again we was mistook. The old ’un spots
Tom, who had been down for a dip, a-comin’ up,
and he just waits until Tom was at the proper
distance, and then— mind yer, boys, without Tom
knowin ’ anything about it—gives his whip a flip
and takes two sixpences clean out of his trouser.i-
pocket.
“Poor Stringy was done, and the new-chum,
who was makin’ the damper, faints clean away.
“Ah!” sighed Old Bill, with tears of bitter
regret stealing down his weather-beaten cheeks,
“ them was the two cleverest stockmen I ever
seen. There ain’t none like ’em now.”

THAT QUIET HACK


A LOCAL OPTIONIST.
THE REMNANT: “Poor devil! no wonder he spits it out!”


Dirt Cheap!
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Dirt cheap!
The expression was eternally on the tongue of
the little Baptist clergyman who had haunted the
auction-sales of the city week after week, month
after month, for so many years.
It inevitably followed his return home after
these buying expeditions of his :
“Dirt cheap, my dear ! It was really too great
a bargain to lose.”
A SNAKE-STORY.—THE FIRST OF THE SEASON.
It annoyed the wife far more than if he had
described his purchases as not worth the money.
Her replies were tinged with bitterness, and the
shadow grew in her eyes.
“ Things that we don’t want are dear at any
price," she would tell him.
She was thinking with a sickening despondence
of the things that they really did want, the bare
necessaries of which no man takes heed till they
are taken from him.
For years he had been “looking out at the sales,”
and instead of the necessaries there had come at
various times, to the shabby parsonage door,
packages of butter-knives, dozens of cards, with
six knives on each card, boxes of Afghan jewellery,
breast-pins, studs, brooches, ear-rings, dozens of
towel-horses, cases of ornaments (all made after
one pattern, and that an unbeautiful one), twelve
little bronze kettles, three baths, a load of foolscap
that stood on the floor of the study, and reached
half-way up the wall, many boxes of door-handles,
many dozens of gilt-framed oleographs, and of
photograph frames, with two lay figures, such as
dressmakers use for draping their skirts.
Their house was so crowded with these bargains
that it had all the appearance of a second-hand-
furniture shop. There were two sideboards in the
dining-room. In the narrow hall there were three
hat-stands of various designs, and three hall-chairs.
Superfluous toilet-tables and chests of drawers
stood on the landing upstairs. Put in the laundry
were the two mangles, the three bottomless cots,
spades and hoes for which no garden afforded
work, a bedstead that the house refused to accom-
modate.
But the study was the central receiving-place.
Every drawer in the two chests, every pigeon-hole
in the tremendous secretary, was crammed with a
heterogeneous collection that ranged from a table-
spoon to a whitewash brush.
As time went on, every bedroom in the parson-
age received a spare bed, a spare toilet-table, one
or perhaps two spare wash-stands, and innumer-
able spare towel-horses.
The towel-horses and the butter-knives pre-
dominated over everything.
“One hundred and forty-four butter-knives!
Two dozen cards, with six on each ! ”
The minister’s wife counted them. Her pantry
was so often butterless that the irony of this pur-
chase struck her sharply.
They were d*rt cheap,” he assured her.
“ We can never use them.”
They will come in for the children. They will
be nice presents for them when they marry.”
Twenty-eight each for the five of them ! ”
We can use some of them ourselves.”
I only hope that the children will have more
butter to eat than their mother has had.”
Was it any wonder that she was bitter?
She had remonstrated with him, coaxed him,
argued with him, scolded him, abused him, wept
even—all in vain.
It seemed as if a passion for buying had spread
ineradicable roots through his whole nature, until
at last he found himself pinioned, as a great tree
holds the earth, in a grip of iron, against which
his will was powerless to struggle.
All the happiness of the home wore away. Its
peacefulness was destroyed. The perpetual friction
of the two opposing wishes spread itself over
everything.
The children sided unconsciously with their
father. They could not understand why their
mother should be angry with him when the cart
came to the door with all those nice packages in it.
1 hey assisted gleefully in the excitement of carry-
ing the towel-horses upstairs. The arrival of the
Bargains made their only gala-days.
Their father’s face was always full of kindliness
and good- humor at such times as these ; his voice
was a trifle softer and more genial; his eyes
warmer.
If only their mother would not stand like a sentry
on guard in the doorway, with that grim, kill-joy
expression on her lips ! Their childish hearts were
disturbed a little ; it vexed them to see their mother
look so crossly at the nice things, and they began
unconsciously to wish that she would be out when
the Bargains came.
They grew to understand that when their father
came into the house with that genial smile, and a
specially gentle movement as he opened doors or
walked upstairs, a cart would shortly follow.
They did not fathom the meaning of the hope-
less look on their mother’s lips. It was because of
them, her little ones, who would have to go with-
out so much in days
to come, that she re-
sented this waste of
money that should
have been spent so
differently.
“ The children are
vexed because I don’t
smile and look happy
when his loads arrive, ”
she said to her sister.
Her eyes were grow-
ing hard as well 'as
bitter. To have her
children passing their
quick, cruel judgment
is not an easy burden
for a mother’s heart to
bear. Hers ached
under it. But she
said nothing.
The carts continued
to arrive. The bar-
gains multiplied ex-
ceedingly. As the
years went on the
collection grew more
and more varied, more
and more eccentric,
more and more use-
less.
Not only the ground
space was covered with
the accumulations,
but the walls, too, were monopolised by the
piles that leant against them.
A footfall in the hall had the deadness of echo
that one notices in walking in the furniture-
department of a warehouse.
He was such a good fellow, too, her
husband. The best man in the world. She
knew that.
But there came a time when her endur-
ance had reached its uttermost limit,
and nothing but intense anger against
him filled her heart. She went
to her sister, and said that she
could bear no longer the terrible
strain of wanting money, and
seeing it so ruthlessly wasted
under her own roof.
“ I feel as if I must leave him;
go away and never come back,”
she said.
“Send them away,” said the
sister. “Send the things away
some day when he is out, and
sell them all at auction, and
spend the money that very in-
stant. That is what I would
do.”
It was certainly a brilliant idea.
There was daring in it —but
it was not impracticable. The
more she thought of it, the more
it attracted her.
At last she gave herself up to it,
and fearlessly faced the consequences.
He was called into the country for a few days,
and in that time she arranged everything with
the promptness and care of a general. Carts came
to the house, but this time they brought nothing,
and took much away. One after the other the
rooms were cleared of all their superfluities—the
bottomless cots, the lay figures, the towel-horses,
cards and cards of butter-knives.
They were all gone at last. She walked Tip and
down the hall for the sheer pleasure of hearing her
footfalls. They had been deadened for so long.
“ To-morrow,” she said to the children, “ I shall
have a nice pink cheque, and you shall have boots,
and hats, and a doll each, and new brooches, and
all manner of good things.”
The children’s eyes were full of naive surprise.
It was always father who spent, and mother who
would not spend.
The mother understood that look in their eyes,
and it hurt her. But she was so full of delight at
the happy accomplishment of her plans that she
laughed gaily with them.
But not yet the accomplishment !
She had planned, and carried out her plans. All
her part of the plot had been fulfilled. It was the
gods who interfered. On the afternoon of the
morrow, as she was dressing herself to go to town,
and learn the prices that her goods had brought, a
great van drove up to the front door.
The children were talking to someone in the hall.
She went out into the landing and leaned over
the bannisters and looked down.
It was their father ! He had come home a day
before she expected him. Her heart-beats were a
little swifter, but she laughed to herself, and said
nothing mattered now that the things were out of
the way—and then men came in through the front
door carrying the contents of the van. Back-
wards and forwards they went. And the children
ran out on the doorstep and helped, too.
There were the two bottomless cots, the lay
figures, the towel-horses. And there, in the baby’s
arms, were the butter-knives. It was cooing over
them lovingly.
She made a queer little choking sound, and her
husband looked up and saw that tragic listener.
I really could not resist them, ” he said, coming
up the stairs to her. “The lot came in just as I
was passing the rooms, and I bought them in dirt
cheap ! ”
He had bought them all back again !
LOUISE MACK.
Devil-May- Cave.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Although not a banner nor badge do I bear,
I’m one of the army of Devil-May-Care.
Who leads or who follows we care not a curse,
For we hew our own places for better or worse.
It is idle to ask me what land gave us birth ;
We’re the most cosmopolitan crowd on the
earth.
The white or the brown or the yellow may share
The toil and the spoil of the Devil-May-Care.
Who ’listed us first ? Well, the army began
When the missing-link started in business as man ;
For the first one that rose from four feet on to
two
Was a bold, dashing fellow who loved something
new.
He took all the chances and proudly he strode
As the boss of the lot in his sylvan abode.
He cared not a bone for cave-lion or bear,
This first of the army of Devil-May-Care.
Since then we have battled in many a land,
Each square-foot of earth bears the mark of our
hand.
We’ve trampled on cities and countries and men,
And reddened the rivers again and again.
We’ve harried and slaughtered, we’ve battled and
burnt,
But still from it all little wisdom we’ve learnt;
For something goes wrong in dividing the share
That should fall to the army of Devil-May-Care.
By the “ Law of the Strongest ” all justice is
Might :
For the God of the Law is the God of the Fight.
But the battle has changed in the course of the
years,
And sovereigns are armies and shillings are
spears.
And a row of accounts that a grocer may scrawl
Can ruin the bravest and best of us all.
For the fox now is lord in the lion’s old lair—
Hard luck for the army of Devil-May-Care !
For the might of man’s cunning is more than the
sword,
And the soldier may fight, but the talker is lord.
So, wrestle and struggle and fight as we will,
The dealers in lives have the best of us still.
There are folks who hang on to the Law of the
Text,
Who shiver through this world and into the next:
We laugh at their ways, but the grovellers fare
Better far than the sons of the Devil-May-Care.
With never an aim but the love of the fight,
With seldom a thought of the wrong or the right,
Hell-for-leather we go for the fun of the thing,
And it’s little we reck what our going may bring.
For a shilling we tackle all foes that may come,
That the merchant may travel with shoddy and
rum :
And the roads to new markets the nations prepare
With the bones and the blood of the Devil-May-
Care.
There are some of our troopers who stick up the
mails,
There are others who hunt them to death or the
gaols ;
Each one of our soldiers (as chance may behave)
May be blessed as a hero or cursed as a knave.
One stripped to the pelt may a Maxim defy,
One ’gainst the whole world may a doctrine deny ;
For a need or a whim (as the Fates may declare)
Rush forward the hosts of the Devil-May-Care.
We’re slaves to the fat-bellied men of the town,
For themselves and their purses we lay our lives
down :
In trades and professions, by day and by night,
With pen or with pick or with rifie we fight.
But if there’s a lull in the fighting, alack,
We’re turned on the roads with a swag on our
back !
Full partners with Bluey—and how do we fare ?
The broken-down sons of the Devil-May-Care.
We fight for the good and we fight for the bad,
For the thought that’s divine, or for somebody
fad ;
For the little or great, be they e’er so absurd,
For a color, a tune, or a rag, or a word.
But (instead of thus sillily “ going it blind ”)
If we got all the Army to be of one mind,
Our sleek, stall-fed owners would soon be aware
Of the strength of the army of Devil-May-Care.
The fat son of Prudence drives past in great state.
He lives on the wealth that he cannot create ;
He covers us deeply with dust as he flies —
But it’s nothing at all to the dust in our eyes.
Plod on, plod along—’tis the law of the game,
It is chance that ordains (flesh and blood are the
same),
And the man who rolls by in his carriage and pair
May have marched in the army of Devil-May-
Care.
Sydney.
ZEF.
SHE HAD CEASED TO LIVE.
“Where are you living now, Jane?”
“ Nawhere, mum; I’m married !”


ANOTHER TRIAL
FOR THE BULLETIN
We had just finished supper. Supper 1
dry bread and sugarless tea. Dad was tired out
and was resting at one end of the sofa; Joe was
stretched at the other, without a pillow, and his
legs tangled up among Dad’s. Bill and lom
squatted in the ashes doing their exercise by the
dismal firelight, while Mother tried to put the fat-
lamp into burning order by poking it with a table-
fork.
Dad was silent; he seemed sad, and lay for
some time gazing at the roof. He might have
been watching the blaze of the glorious moon or
counting the stars through the gaps in the shin-
gles, but he wasn’t—there was no such sentiment
in Dad. He was thinking how his long years of
toil, his hopes and worry had been met and
rewarded again and again by disappointment and
adversity—wondering if ever there would be a turn
in his luck, and how he was going to get enough
out of the land that season to pay interest and
keep Mother and us in bread and meat.
At last he spoke, or rather muttered dis-
jointedly, “Plen-ty—to eat —in the safe.” Then
suddenly, in a strange and hollow voice, he
shouted, “ They're dead—all of them! I starved
them / ”
Mother did get a fright. She screamed. Then
Dad jumped up, rubbing his eyes, and asked what
was the matter. Nothing was the matter then.
He had dozed and tuf.-.ed in his sleep, that was
all; he hadn’t starved anyone. Joe didn’t jump
up when Mother screamed —not altogether; he
raised himself and reached for Dad’s pillow, then
lay down and snored serenely till bed-time.
Dad sat gloomily by the fire and meditated.
Mother spoke pleadingly to him and asked him
not to fret. He ran his fingers uneasily through
his hair and spat in the ashes. “ ‘ Don’t fret ’ ?
When there’s not a bit to eat in the place—when
there’s no way of getting anything, and when—
merciful God ! every year sees things worse than
they were Before.”
“It’s only fancy,” Mother went on. “And
you’ve been brooding and brooding till it seems
far worse than it really is.’’
“It’s no fancy, Ellen.” Then, after a pause—
“ Was the thirty acres of wheat that didn’t come
up fancy ? Is it only fancy that we’ve lost nearly
every beast in the paddock ? Was the drought
itself a fancy ? No—no.” And he shook his head
sadly and stared again into the fire.
Dad’s inclination was to leave the selection, but
Mother pleaded for another trial of it—just one
more. She had wonderful faith in the selection,
had Mother. She pleaded until the fire burned
low, then Dad rose and said : “ Well, we’ll try it
once more with corn, and if nothing comes of it ;
why then we must give it up.” Then he took the
spade and raked the fire together and covered it
with ashes—we always covered the fire over before
going to bed so as to keep it alight. Some morn-
ings, though, it would be out, when one of us
would have to go across to Anderson’s and borrow
a fire-stick. Any of us but Joe—he was only sent
once, and on that occasion he waited at Anderson’s
to breakfast, and on his way back successfully
burnt out two grass paddocks belonging to a J.P.
• * * *
So we began to prepare the soil for another
crop of corn, and Dad started over the same old
ground with the same old plough. How I remem-
ber that old, screwed and twisted plough ! The
land was very hard, and the horses out of con-
dition. We wanted a furrow-horse. Smith had
one — a good one. “ But him in the furrow,” he
said to Dad, “and you can’t pull him out of it.”
Dad wished to have such a horse. Smith offered
to exchange for our roan saddle mare—one we
found running in the lane, and advertised as being
in our paddock, and no one claimed it. Dad
exchanged.
He yoked the new horse to the plough, and it
took to the furrow splendidly—but that was all;
it didn’t take to anything else. Dad gripped
the handles—“ Git up!” he said, and tapped
Smith’s horse with the rein. Smith’s horse
pranced and marked time well, but didn’t tighten
the chains. Dad touched him again. Then he
stood on his fore-legs and threw about a hundred-
weight of mud that clung to his heels at Dad s
head. That aggravated Dad, and he seized the
plough-scraper, and, using both hands, calmly
belted him over the ribs for two minutes, by the
sun. He tried him again. The horse threw
himself down in the furrow. Dad took the
scraper again, welted him on the rump, hug it
into his back-bone, prodded him in the side, then
threw it at him disgustedly. Then Dad sat down
awhile and breathed heavily. He rose again
and pulled Smith’s horse by the head. He was
pulling hard when Dave and Joe came up. Joe
had a bow-and-arrow in his hand, and said, “ He’s
a good furrer ’orse, eh, Dad ? Smith said you
couldn’t pull him out of it.”
Shall I ever forget the look on Dad’s face ! He
brandished the scraper and sprang wildly at Joe
and yelled, “ Damn y’, you whelp, what do you
want here?”
Joe left. The horse lay in the furrow. Blood
was dropping from its mouth. Dave pointed it
out, and Dad opened the brute’s jaws and examined
them. No teeth were there. He looked on the
ground round about —none there either. He
looked at the horse’s mouth again, then
hit him viciously with his clenched fist and
said, “The old , he never did have any!”
At length he unharnessed the brute as it lay —
pulled the winkers off, hurled them at its head,
kicked it once —twice —three times—and the
furrow-horse jumped up, trotted away triumph-
antly, and joyously rolled in the dam.
Dad went straightaway to Smith’s place, and
old Smith he was a dirty, mean, despicable
swindler —or something like that. Smith smiled.
Dad put one leg through the slip-rails and pro-
mised Smith, if he’d only come along, to split pal-
ings out of him. But Smith didn’t. The instinct
of self-preservation must have been deep in that
man Smith. Then Dad went home and said he
would shoot the horse there and then, and
went looking for the gun. The horse died in the
paddock of old age, but Dad never ploughed with
him again.
m * ♦ *
Dad now followed the plough early and late.
One day he was giving the horses a spell after
some hours’ work, when Joe came to say that a
policeman was at the house wanting to see him.
Dad thought of the roan mare, and Smith, and
turned very pale. Joe said : “ There’s ‘ Q.P.’ on
his saddle-cloth ; what’s that for, Dad ? ” But he
didn’t answer—he was thinking hard. “ And,”
Joe went on, “ there’s somethin’ sticking out of
his pocket—Dave thinks it ’ll be ’ancuffs.” Dad
shuddered. On the way to the house Joe wished
to speak about the policeman, but Dad seemed to
have lock-jaw. When he found the officer of the
law only wanted to know the number of stock he
owned, he talked freely—he was delighted. He
said “Yes, sir,” and “No, sir,” and “ Jusso, sir,”
to everything the policeman said.
Dad wished to learn some law. He said : “ Now,
tell me this : supposing a horse gets into my pad-
dock —or into your paddock—and I advertise that
horse and nobody claims him, can’t I put my
brand on him ? ” The policeman jerked back his
head and stared at the shingles long enough to
recall all the robberies he had committed, and
said : “Ye can—that’s so—ye can.”
“ I knew it,” answered Dad ; “ but a lawyer in
town told Mulrooney, ov»* there, y’ couldn’t.”
“ Couldn’t ? ” And
the policeman laughed
till he nearly had the
house down, only stop-
ping to ask, while the
tears ran over his well-
fed cheeks, “ Did he
charge him forrit ? ”
and laughed again. He
went away laughing,
and for all I know
the wooden-head may
be laughing yet.
* * *
Everything was
favorable to a good
harvest. The rain fell
just when it was want-
ed, and one could al-
most see the corn
growing. How it en-
couraged Dad, and
whatnew life it seemed
to give him ! In the
cool of the evenings
he would walk along
the headlands and ad-
mire the forming cobs
and listen to the
rustling of the rows of
drooping blades as they
swayed and beat
against each other in
the breeze. Then he
would go home filled
with fresh hopes and
talk of nothing but the
good prospect of that
crop.
• » *
Joe found some-
thing on a chain.
He had never seen any-
thing like it before.
Dad told him it was a
steel - trap and ex-
plained the working of
it. Joe was entranced
—an invaluable posses-
sion ! A treasure, he
felt, that the Lord
must specially have
sent him to catch
things with. He
caught many things
with it willie-wag-
tails, laughing - jack-
asses, fowls, and most-
ly the dog. Joe was a ...
born naturalist, a perfect M‘Gooey in his way,
and a close observer of the habits and customs of
animals and living things. He observed that
whenever Jacob Lipp came to the place he always,
when going home, ran along the fence and touched
the top of every post with his hand. The Lipps
had newly arrived from Germany and their selec-
tion adjoined ours. Jacob was their “ eldest,

about 14—a year younger than Joe — ahd a fat,
jabbering, sausage-faced youth he was. He often
came to our place and followed Joe about. Joe
never cared much for the company of anyone
younger than himself, and therefore fiercely re-
sented the indignity. Jacob could only speak
German—Joe only understood pure unadulterated
Australian. Still Jacob insisted on talking and
telling Joe his private affairs.
This day, Mrs. Lipp accompanied Jacob. She
came to have a “ yarn ” with Mother. They didn t
understand each other either —but it didn’t matter
much to them—it never does matter much to
women whether they understand or not—any way
they laughed most of the time and seemed to
enjoy themselves greatly. But outside Jacob
and Joe mixed up in an argument. Jacob shoved
his face close to Joe’s and gesticulated and talked
German at the rate of 200 words a minute. Joe
thought he understood him and said : “ You want
to fight ?” Jacob seemed to have a nightmare m
“ Orri'ght, then,” Joe said, and knocked him
down.
Jacob seemed to understand Australian better
when he got up, for he ran inside, and Joe put his
ear to a crack, but he didn’t hear him tell Mother.
Joe had an idea. He would set the steel-trap on
a wire-post and catch Jacob. He set it. Jhcob
started home. One, two, three posts he hit. Then
he hit the trap. It grabbed him faithfully by
three fingers.
Angels of Love ! did ever a boy of 14 yell like it
before ! He sprang in the air—threw himself on
the ground like a roped brumby—jumped up again
and ran all he knew, frantically wringing the
hand the trap clung to. What Jacob reckoned
had hold of him Heaven can only tell. His
mother thought he must have gone mad and rnn

TOTTIE : “ All at once he put his arm round my waist, and said he

* • * *
The corn shelled out 100 bags—the best
crop we had ever had; but when Dad came to sell
it seemed as though every farmer in every farming
district on earth had had a heavy crop, for the
market was glutted—there was too much com in
Egypt—and he could get no price for it. At last
he was offered 9Jd. per bushel, delivered at the
railway-station. Ninepence ha’penny per bushel,
delivered at the i ail way-station ! Oh, my coun-
try ! and fivepence per bushel out of that to a
carrier to take it there ! Australia, my mother!
Dad sold—because he couldn’t afford to await a
better market, and when the letter came contain-
ing a cheque in payment, he made a calculation,
then looked pitifully at Mother, and muttered—
“ Seven poun’s ten / ” *
STEELE RUDD.
Told by a Dandaloot : —“No, Bill; I
never get fur from the Coffee Palace. If you go
out on the roads there’s coves rushin’ at you from
all directions. I didn’t like it. I went over oppo-
site one day to get my picture took. I says to the
girl ‘ I want to be phot-oed,’ and she says ‘ All
right, sir; twelve-aftd-six. Thank you. Step
into the lift.’ And she smiles so sweet, Bill. I
sees a big box with a boy in it, and, thinkin’ she
wouldn’t put me- wrong, steps in. I hears her say
to her mate ‘ Goin’ to be a rise in sheep ’; and,
look here, Bill, that was the only interestin’
remark I heard all the time I was down there. I’d
a married that girl if I’d stayed. I was jest
bondin’ on her one of my looks of intelligence
when I finds me and the boy flyin’ up through the
ceilin’ like aironites. My word, I was frightened,
and before I knew where I was I was jammed into
a chair, my napper screwed up in a vice, and
there was a feller at the other end of the room
hidin’ hisself. I sings out like blazes, and he
comes out, lets me go, and hands me this. Says
he ‘ It’s a very good picture. ’ But it ain’t like me,
is it, Bill ? ’ Why, the top of it’s wrinkled like a
Mudgee ram, the eyes of it’s starin’ like a ‘ Queens-
lander,’ and the mouth’s like a open portmanter.
It ain’t me, is it, Bill ? I come hofje next day ;
there’s no place like the Bogan.”

Phases.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
INTRODUCTORY.
Of bees and birds, in wondrous words,
Of waves, and winds, and flowers, and trees,
A man shall take his pen, and wake
In human hearts, the joys of these.
And one shall tell that all is well
On earth and sea ; and one shall rail;
And one shall sing of endless spring,
And one shall voice the winter’s wail.
I, too, had eyes for kindly skies,
And ears for singing birds and bees j
I, too, had times of riant rhymes,
But not in joy I made me these.
I.
And so this night of nights is o’er,
This fearful night of strife and pain ;
And I lie here, and far before
The young Day climbs the hills again.
With ripe, red lips, and eager eyes
With liquid lights of love a-swim,
He comes to seek the Night that lies
With heart and soul aflame for him.
She bows to his imperious sway,
She twines her arms around his head ;
Her robes unloosed, her hair astray,
They make the hills their bridal bed.
Such subtle splendor, glory, grace ;
Such streams of light as skyward flow—
But Death and I were face to face,
Aye, cheek-by-jowl, an hour ago.
11.
I have not asked for overmuch
To light and gladden this, my span,
For vulgar wealth, or any such
As marks the goal of moiling man.
I had my crown of thorns to wear,
I have not made the weak my spoil,
I have not sought to shun my share,
To shirk, or shift, my mete of toil;
Nor envied rank that princes give
In lands beyond the sounding seas ;
I had my span of life to live,
And in my heart I dreamt of these :
A little work, a little play,
A little home, my own to call,
A little joy to glad my way,
A little love —and that was all.
And sometimes, when the spirit stirred,
To voice my thoughts with patient pen,
And joy to think that some stray word
Might touch the hearts of distant men..
111.
I slept, the springtide round me stirred
So pure, and sweet, and undefiled ;
And in mine ears methought I heard
The laughter of a little child.
I dreamt of dormant things within,
That swelled to flower in wondrous ways,
I dreamt of worlds to woo and win,
I dreamt of love and golden days.
I woke, and, 10, the sun had fled,
The stars were hid in inky pall,
The winds were in the boughs o’erhead,
And storm and darkness over all.
IV.
It lies so still, this heart of mine,
Within my breast the livelong day ;
It lies so still, it makes no sign,
So cold and calm, it claims no sway.
But when the sky a splendor takes
And sunset turns to night again,
Like some poor widowed thing it wakes
To consciousness and bitter pain.
And so, forlorn and desolate,
It sings the stars its song of woe,
And moans and mourns its buried mate—
The treasured dream of long ago.
V.
Mine eyes, that served my heart so well,,
Are blind with tears for all her woe,
Who drooped, and pined, and sought hsiroelll-
And died, despairing, long ago.
Wine ears arc vexed of strife within,
My soul to dismal dearth is wed,
And all my powers arc strangled in
The sordid strife for daily bread; / ,y.
And all my golden dreams are dead,
And all my days are days of pain,
And all my life, a flame unfed,
That out to darkness dies again.
Till lo ! in my despair I cry,
In this my grievous agony :
If God thou be, O God on high !
What pity bast thou shown to me ? ”
VI.
My thought went out with tireless wing,
I rom north to south, and east to west,
To find, perchance, and homeward bring
Some peace for all my great unrest.
It could not glean through all its scope,
Design or purpose, scheme or plan,
A resting-place for faith and hope,
Or care, supreme, on earth for man.
But everywhere incessant strife,
With death the one supreme decree,
And at the springs of human life,
Impenetrable mystery.
VII.
I deemed myself, of all men born
To be the sport and spoil of fate,
Neglected most, the most forlorn,
The most oppressed and desolate.
And I went forth and cried my woe,
In street, and square, and market-place,
That all who passed might hear, and know
My mete of grief, my dearth of grace.
Ah, pardon me ! I was too lost
In contemplating self, to see
The lives around me tempest-tossed,
The haggard want and misery.
Ah, pardon me ! I did not know
How poor my grief, how mean my lot
Beside the sum of human woe—
It dawned on me, and I forgot.
The cry died out upon my lip,
And so, from poor humanity,
Through suffering companionship,
Redemption came at last to me.
And there was light again, and then
The mists that clouded time unfurled,
And I beheld the streams of men
Progressing slowly through the world.
They came from all the ends of earth,
With hearts that held for days to be,
Through fruitful years, or years of dearth,
The sacred seed of Liberty.
VIII.
In soil or sand, the scattered seed
Resolves itself in ways untold ;
And so the smallest human deed
Hath consequences manifold.
For good or ill, I know the race
Will reap the seed a man has sown.
And no one lives devoid of grace,
And no man’s life is all his own.
I know the act is ill that pits
A man against his kind, and good
The act that closer draws, and knits
The bonds of human brotherhood.
I know that round me all the base
Affects me imperceptibly,
And all the sins of all my race
Like ghostly spectres shadow me.
They haunt my steps, whose pathway lies
Halfway betwixt the night and morn—
A ghastly crew, that mock the cries
Of help from children still unborn.
A link betwixt the quick and dust,
The past and future meet in me,
I tremble at the awful trust,
The grave responsibility.
Brisbane.
FRANCIS KENNA.
‘Scene, Coolgardie, or thereabouts. New
arrival to his mate, who is washing, “ Say, Jim,
surely you ain’t finished. Why, you have only
washed one leg.” Jim (an old Coolgardie hand) :
I—yes, can’t afford more water. Must bo
•economical. You see, I wash once a quarter.”
New arrival :“ A quarter each time ! ” Jim :
"“Exactly. This shoulder stands for January,
February, and March. The other for April, May,
June. And so on. I have just washed July,
August, and September—that stands for nine
months. It’s our almnnur."
On the Brink.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Her face was a mask, usually impene-
trable. The lines of pain were smoothed out by
force of will and the mouth was made to smile,
but the man who loved her could see a shadow
deep down in her eyes. All the world saw the
slighting manner and the cold indifference of her
•.husband —he wore no mask—and some pitied the
wife, who was only a girl, and some said she did
not care. But one man knew that some day the
mask must fall, and he watched and waited.
How the blood surged through him as his mare
climbed the last hill, quickened her paces over the
last mile of scrub, and splashed through the water
which sometimes, after the wet season, rose
perilously close to the homestead ! The bamboos
swayed and rustled and creaked together, and the
water was. dazzling beneath the hot sun. His fists
clenched involuntarily as he recalled some slight
to the woman he loved, and the veins rose on his
forehead as he heard again the insolent, sneering
laugh. Some day, he said to himself, he and that
man would find themselves face to face in the
lonely mallee, and then He laughed.
His face softened, and the angry heat cooled.
The husband was away, and this one afternoon
was his, his alone, and who knew but the world
might end to-night ?
* * * *
She sat and talked to him in the cool, dark
room with her little child leaning against her knee.
The shadow lurked in her eyes, but the mask was
still there. The man had not watched in vain.
“Sing tome,” he pleaded, and presently there
rose the low vibrating notes of a born singer, soft
at first, then louder, fuller and richer, trembling
and throbbing from a passionate heart —and the
mask fell. There was agony in the voice and a
tempestuous cry to be free, free ! It shivered with
despair and almost died. The child had stopped
her play, her little face working with the coming
tears. Suddenly she burst out sobbing :
“Oh, mummy, don’t, don’t, don’t! ”
She ran to the piano and flung herself upon her
mother.
“Hush, little one ! ” said the woman, tenderly.
Her hands were trembling as she touched the soft
hair soothingly.
“ She can’t bear me to sing,” she said apologeti-
cally as the child went back to her play, “ she
thinks I am in pain.”
“In pain, oh, my God!” said the man. He
saw the look of anguish which the music had
brought to her face ; he saw the trembling of her
lijjs. His hour had come, and with a mad exulta-
tion he seized her in his fierce embrace. . . .
“ At dusk when the child is asleep,” lie whis-
pered, as he sprang to his saddle and galloped back
to town.
* * * *
She trembled so that she could scarcely
stand as she drew her cloak about her. It was a
hot, moist night, yet her teeth were chattering.
“ Come quickly,” he whispered. “ I have a
boat to take us across the water, and there the
buggy is waiting. We can catch the mail-train.”
He tried to draw her on to the verandah, but
her face turned back to the little cot, standing dim
and white in the corner. He felt her hesitate.
“She will be quite happy,” he said. “He is
fond of her, and she is so young.” He turned her
face to his own, and drew her to the door.
The child was dreaming. She moaned softly.
In her dream, she heard her mother’s voice rise
once more, as the chords crashed on the piano. Her
nerves were quivering. She started up in her cot.
“ Oh, mummy, don’t, don’t! ” she cried out,
shrill and piercing.
The woman’s hesitation vanished.
“She would live to curse me,” she said. She
loosed the wrap, and it fell at her feet. The man's
eyes followed her as she ran to the little bed and
took the child to rest in her arms.
“ Hush, my darling ! Hush, my little lamb !
Mother’s here.”
For one moment he looked, then he went out on
to the verandah and closed the door.
His hour had gone.

MAMOOSE.
“BLIND TO ALL HER FAULTS."


The Dispersion of Mrs. Black .
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“I know ( ” <said old Bill, meditatively,
“ that it takes more ’n one to get up a proper dis-
persion, but you will find that old woman Black
were dispersed pretty wide, for all that.
“Yer see, it were this way.
Old Sol Black were a whaler down
at Maketu, and, like all sich, he
got a Maori wife and a lot o’ kids.
Well, his old ’ooman met with a
haecident one day in war-time.
Old Sol used to help the tribe in
battle, and on this hoccasion they
got well licked, and Sol’s wife
got eat Sol thought he would
get a white woman to look after
the kids, and he goes to Auckland
in the Flying Fish and brings
one back.
“ She were a widder wot never
had no kids of her own, which
was just into Sol’s hand. She
were a big, fat heifer, and it took
a tidy plarster to make her a frock,
so she seldom wore one. She
minded the kids all right till they
got spliced or went off on their
own hook.
“ Arter that the old ’ooman used
to go about the pah jabbering and
chiacking with one and another.
She were generally barefooted, just
like a Maori woman. She kep’
pretty straight, ’cept when there
was any rum or square-face about;
then she’d get her whack anyhow.
“ They’d shifted to Rotorua by
this time, and their whar& was on
the lake shore ; but most of the
natives lived on the hill. Old Sol
was away ketching crawfish in the
lake when one on ’em on the hill
got up a five-gallon keg o’ rum.
The old ’ooman smelt it, and was
up there on the tear quick. It
were dark when she tacked for
home, pretty well boozed. There
was a lot of hot-springs and cook-
ing-holes and biling-pools o’ all
sorts just off the track ; but, Lord !
she’d been along that track all
hours.
* * *
“Well, there wasn’t no one
at home, so nobody missed her,
anyhow. The old go-ashore which
was their bell was a-sounding for
morning prayers, for they prays
twice a day, do then natives, quite
public in the church, and when the
first lot got a bit on the track they
saw a blanket which had caught
by a corner on a old ti-tree stump,
right acrost the track, and the
further corner was just on the edge of-the bilingest
of all them biling springs.
“ When they looked in there was a lot of hile
on the top, and as the water heaved and rolled
with the heat of hell a lot of whitish, yallerish,
flaky and ropy-looking stuff was a-rolling round
with the hile and water. There was some o’ them
fresh green ferns on the further edge a-hanging
over, and by-an’-by a bigger bile-up than common
fetched some white rag out from hunder the ferns
all lapped and twisted round summut hard. The
niggers ’ud got to know afore this what was the
matter from the blanket, and when they saw the
white rag they was certint, cos them was all her
togs the night afore.
“ They fished out the rag and found it were
lapped and twisted round the neck and breast of
poor Mrs. Black. The head was biled off and like-
wise the arms and backbone was broke through the
middle, what with the rousting about it got when
the bile was most wiolent, and I alius heard say she
were a tender critter.
“ They didn’t know what to do all to wonst.
They thought if they got her out and put her in a
soap-box she’d run through the jints. A’ larst
one on ’em who’d bin to school said the Rumuns
used to put their dead ’uns into jars arter burning
on ’em to ashes, and stick ’em on the mantel where
they was alius handy to cry over. He thought a
callybash, such as they put birds and pork in when
they was biled down in their own fat, would about
fill the bill, and it would comfort old Sol to have
his wife in a funeral burn, as he called it. He
were a rare schollard for a native, that chap.
“ They agreed to that, and got some old bent
tin and pumpkin shells, and a canoe baler and a
long-handle shovel, and ladled the old girl out.
The bones was mostly low down fooling around in
the heddy where the bottom of the hole ought to
be.
“They kep’the bones separate, and when the
head come out from hunder the ferns it were quite
white and nothin’ on it to speak on. Some of the
red hairs was a-twisted round the other pieces, and
some was in locks of hair by themselves among
the hile. Bein’ as she were a copper-topped ’un, the
hairs weren’t as good to see as black ’uns, but they
was very careful.
“ They got a good large gourd a couple of foot
high, open at the top, and they put all the small
goods in ; but the worst job was skimming off the
hile, for they said they would like her to be all
there. But she weren’t, for I see a good-looking
Maori wench a-fishing out the finger and toe-nails
with a pawa shell, and she wore ’em for a necklace
the next day.
“ The shovel come in wery handy for skim-
ming the hile. The worst was keeping the water
out; and they said if that was scooped up and put
in the hipu, it might be unpleasant for various
reasons to Sol when he went to weep beside the
hurn.
“ At larst they got her fixed and a flat bit of tin
on the openin’/ and they said they’d stand the
jimmy on top of that to give it a proper finish like.
It were a melancholy business, some o’ the women
was a-crying and a-howling all the time. The
bones they tied together wery neat and put ’em in
a case o’ titara bark what had had smoked heels
in. Then they concluded to put it in the store
out of the way of the kids, who might think she
were pork if left in the whare.
“ When Sol came back he was quite pleased
with the pains they’d took, and he had a good
look at her and gave her a nod when he left, but
he didn't go and weep beside the hurn, and the

SILAS SNELL.
Socialism in Oar Own Time.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
At Wanaaring, by the placid Paroo, where in the
early sixties the man who couldn’t swill rum out
of an oil-drum without unhitching his belt, and
chop his corns off with a tomahawk, was looked
upon as outside the pale of progressive society, a
blackfellow and his lubra were lately employed at
one of the pubs. The lady was a model of
sobriety, but her lord having acquired an un-
quenchable thirst for overproof rum, his services
were dispensed with. Mrs. Blackfellow was
known as “ Pigweed Mary,” owing to her pre-
dilection for that succulent vegetable, which grows
to such profusion in the Wanaaring region that
A FANCY-DRESS ITEM.
EDIE : “ I am going as a powder-puff, Laura, and I thought you might go in black-silk tights.”
LAURA: “As what?”
EDIE (triumphantly): “As a hair-pin!”
the Chinese gardeners’ revenues are much re-
duced when the herb escapes the drought; her
husband was “ Boko Billy so called because he
had lost his near eye while attempting, in a giddy
moment, to capture another my-fella Mary. After
Boko’s dismissal he used to hang round the back
of the pub. every evening and anxiously await the
coming of his missus—which unusual display of
affection on the part of her hitherto fickle consort
became the talk of the town.
It ultimately came out that Pigweed Mary, on
completing her day’s work, would breast the bar
and call for big fella rum. This she would toss off
like a connoisseur, but with stoio fortitude she
held the liquor in her mouth until, with pouting
lips, she fell into the arms of Boko Billy, and,
under cover of a voluptuous embrace, the thirst
and nerve-cravings of the Paroo warrior became
assuaged.
Which was the most satisfactory kiss I ever
heard of.
J.H.S.
Hunter’s Dog.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Bunter and I joined the bank as juniors about the
same time, and were always good friends. He was
managing at Bungburrah, and I had been acting
as relieving - manager for some time when old
Sloper, the inspector, got influenza and I had to
take an inspecting trip which included Bung-
burrah.
I found Bunter very comfortable, as the branch
is a good one with lots of shooting all about.
Bunter was a good all-round man, but shooting
took first place amongst sports when he was con-
cerned. The apple of his eye and pride of his life
was Ponto, a pointer - dog from imported stock
with a pedigree as long as the price Bunter paid.
And Ponto was real good and worthy of the love
and care Bunter lavished on him.
Bunter was popular, but his great friends were
the Smiths, of Tookabobbity station, about twelve
miles from Bungburrah, so when I had finished
my work late on Friday night he proposed going
out to Smith’s when the bank closed on Saturday
and returning on Sunday, so I could go off by the
coach early on Monday morning. Which we did.
Smith was also a great sportsman, and he had a
pedigree pointer named Ponto ; but Ponto Bunter
was better than Ponto Smith, and when the four
of them came in to dinner —I stayed with Mrs.
Smith while they went shooting—Bunter had the
best bag as usual, and Smith didn’t like it.
We had music in the evening—Mrs. Smith and
Bunter were both musical, so Smith and I played
cribbage and smoked while they sang duets. But
I sat facing the piano, and made up my mind to
give Bunter a friendly caution.
Driving home next day Bunter was chuckling
about Ponto’s victory and Smith’s jealousy of it,
so I said, “ Look here, old man, don’t you make
him jealous of his wife, too. It appears to me she
thinks too much of you.”
Bunter flicked his horses once or twice before
speaking, and then, “ I believe you’re right, old
fellow, but you needn’t be afraid. I’ve too much
respegt for old Smith.”
* • • •
When I got back to Sydney old Sloper
was well enough to come to the office, and I must
have caught the influenza from him —anyhow, I
was away sick for a month, and towards the end
of it I heard Bunter was back at the H.O. in dis-
grace—Mrs. Smith !
First day I was at the office Bunter came up
with, “ Hullo, Mac., hope you’re better?”
“Yes, thanks,” said I, “but what about you?
I thought you had too much respect for old
Smith.”
“ Well, yes, and so I had. But then he kicked
my dog.”
D.D.


Second Love.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
In the earliest days of his wooing the grey-eyed
Max called his love his “ rose of Sharon,” and he
delighted infinitely in her flower-like face—eyes
like baby violets, skin pure as an English dog-rose,
and lips geranium-red.
The first vision he had of her sent his thoughts
whirling down the steep of years to the time when
he was 22, and had found another rose—a rose
that he had named a star when he had perceived
how unattainable it was.
He was 45 now; his hair was iron-grey and
stubbly, his beard grey also.
He had been taking life in earnest, and had put
flower-fancies of woanen behind him to hold jewel
ones instead.
The difference of ideas is recommended as a
helmet of salvation to those bent on scaling the
ladder to fame or gathering up booty of earth.
It is so easy to pick a flower, to hold it close and
draw its sweetness into one’s breath—then to press
it tenderly and lay it away—a heart’s forget-me-
not:
But a jewel !—one may not wear jewels with
shabby garments. It is not fit to go decked with
them in the daylight of a work-a-day world. They
shine at assemblies—the mazy dance is made for
them.
And Max called his love his “ rose of Sharon ”
—perhaps the manner of her arrayment prevented
him ever naming her a lily.
Her mother was popularly al-
luded to as the Angel—this since
angels presumably have to do
with the matches laid at the
doors of Heaven. She had mar-
ried two daughters excellently
and anticipated a particular suc-
cess for this, the third, whom her
jewel fancies had called Pearl.
“ She requires a good setting,”
said the mother, “ and she would
grace a countess’s coronet. ”
Max was the Australian re-
presentative of a large soft-goods
firm, and he drew an income of
something like =61500 a year.
Pearl’s mother mentally prodded
the word “soft-goods.” It was
not pretty. It savored of trade-
in fact, it was trade.
But then, he was the son of a
German count; there was a castle
home somewhere in the region of
the Black Forest that would come
to him if he outlived his still-
thriving preceding generation.
And he had been in the Army.
When Pearl’s smile first came
across his path he was 45, and had
been so long using'his thoughts as
Bputgen uses his rays that life’s
superficialities bewildered him.
After two dances, a garden-
party, and 'an “ at-home,” he -was
convinced he and the years had
made a blunder between them.
He was not 45. If the years said
lie was, they were out in their
calculations. He knew for a fact
that he was only 22. The years
had been running in a ring like
hares, and they had come back
to number 22. They might have
been to 45. That was possible.
Remembering past heart-burnings
and sorrows, loneliness and book
consolations, they must have been
to 45, But they were back again
at 22. *
Even some of his clerks seemed
to recognise the fact. They told
him some absurd jests that only
youth very young could smile at.
But he was young enough to laugh.
They heard him afterwards
chuckling behind the glass parti-
tion that made his room. He had
been seen bounding upstairs two
steps at a time. He scribbled
aimlessly on his blotting - paper;
put away his spectacles for natty
gold eye - glasses. Anyone can
wear eyeglasses if he and his
nose can agree about it.
Also Max stopped playing golf
and chess, and took up dancing
and tennis.
Reissler, one of his clerks on
■£3oo a year, aged rapidly from
the hour when he (Max) began to
grow young. Which was, per-,
haps, the compensation the years*
allowed themselves.
Reissler had fair hair and a poet’s smile, which
is qualified by taking so long to come and being
so far away when it is there. Likewise, he was
22, and also out of joint with the years.
When Max took up tennis Reissler threw it
down. When Max developed the toe light and
fantastic, Reissler felt his lot was to lump through
the furrows of life as a yoked ox.
Pearl herself, in these days, wore a fine color,
fluttering eyelashes, and a love-story in her eyes!
The excitement of hovering on the brink of the
misty unspoken gave her a fresh zest in life.
Max’s tenderness to her exceeded any other
tenderness that had entered into her life. He
desired to serve her and to please her. He was a
relict of chivalry. His eyes every day told her
she was the morning-star of his life.
She was pleased with his flower-fancy of her—it
was fragrant with daintiness.
His love declaration was so fervent that she was
convinced her steadfastness to him would be life-
long.
“ I lofe you !” he said. “I lofe you ! There is
not one of my small thoughts that does not lofe
you ! ”
lie did not rush a caress in upon the heels of
his words as other lovers had essayed to do. In-
stead, he lavished a look on her that was like a
kiss dropped on the hem of her gown.
The novelty of this young-old worship held
thrilling joys to her.
With childish abandonment she slipped her
small hands into his large wide ones, and her
replying—-“ And I love you!” was whispered in
a voice vibrating to a satisfying pitch of love's
ecstasy.
She believed him—which was not to be wondered
at. But she believed also in herself—alas ! which
was to be deplored.
So they went into love together.
“ It has taken me forty-five years,” he told her,
“to find out the meaning of life. Most men are
not taking so long to find it. ”
“ You're the dunce-boy,” she said teasingly. “ I
found it out twenty-three years sooner than you.
And I know it quite as well. ”
The marvellous freshness of love’s atmosphere
stimulated him and sent him into the world a very
youth again. The light in his eyes was of exceed-
ing happiness, for he had turned the key in the
prison-doors that held captured the haggard dreams
of yesterday.
He was free, alive, alert among the flowers of
the Garden of Eden. To enjoy the full measure
of the glory and security of this Garden, one
should walk in wide outer darkness for a space.
“ The only reason the world was made was that
men and women might love each other,” he said,
regarding the Maker’s earliest conception as the
Government or the philanthropic may occasionally
regard bricks and stones—good for asylums and
roadways.
Space held men and women. God saw they
needed bowers for love-making. So He made the
world.
Reissler’s love-declaration was a dirge of despair,
and Pearl’s eyes grew misty over it. He had a
special facility for stringing together love-phrases.
His opinion upon the unchangeableness and in-
tensity of his love ran into oaths pleasing to an
emotional girl.
Max had chiefly said, “ I lofe you ! You are an
angel, Pearl ! I lofe you so ! ”
But the gladsomeness of Max’s second youth did
not make Pearl nearly so glad as Reissler’s pre-
mature age made her restless and sad.
She pulled life into little pieces with Reissler,
whose smile now brought thoughts of a printed
tragedy, the ink of which is still wet.
Sympathy is a tide with its ebb and flow.
Max no longer needed those waves of pity that
had risen towards him for his forlornness, so they
rose on Reissler’s shore and left Max looking into
a heart whose emotion had sunk low.
“You grow cold to me ! ” he reproached her :
“is it that you no longer have any lofe for me ? ”
PANTOMIME PRINCE: “ Sow do you like my new dress, Mr. Legshowe?"
MANGER (after critical examination) : “Delightful! My wordl You’ve got ’em all on!”
“I love you,” she said with her lips alone.
But to stir up wells of gladness in another’s
heart such words want the eyes to be in unison
with the lips.
He saddened visibly.
“ Then you will marry me !” he urged. “ Why
should we wait longer ? ”
She told him she wanted to see life—which made
him feel that some of its dust was falling on her
head.
“ Life was only made that men should marry,”
he said solemnly. “To wait is to tempt death.”
Her mother was equally impatient. She was
distressed. She was annoyed. Finally she was
alarmed.
“ Why should you delay ? ” she said ; “it is full
time you were married. Be married at Easter.
You young people think so much of a moon.”
“ I want to see moonlight in another country.”
“Well, Max will take you round the world if
you ask him.”
But she did not ask him, and Easter slipped
into the grave where the year’s first three months
lay with shut eyes.
“June is the month for a wedding,’’ said Max,
and fell to work making up his books against a
honeymoon.
“ I will leave the books to Reissler ; he thinks
no work can be done without him.”
But Reissler fell sick with a very bad cold and a
shattered heart, and took to his boarding-house
bed.
“You can send the books to me of an evening,”
he said to Max, “while I can use my brains.”
Max laid his wide hands on the young shoulders.
“Well try all we can to shove the world on till
you’re about again,” he said ; “ if we can’t move it
it must stay still. But you are booked here for a
week.”
Reissler lay there for a fortnight, very sick
indeed.
Max gave him some evenings that had been
hall-marked Pearl, and he gave them with a cheer-
fulness that made them valueless in Reissler’s eyes.
I ve just dropped in,” he would say ; or, “I’ve
come to knock away an hour or two ” —and his
overcoat pocket produced alluring delicacies.
He spoke to Pearl about Reissler.
‘He is very ill, my lofe,” he said. “Poor
Reissler! he never did haf quite the back-bone
of a man.”
“ What is the matter with him ? ”
“He is taking a long time to get over some
pleurisy that has had him.”
Perhaps it was the sudden swelling of the tide
of sympathy that made Pearl’s breath come so
hurriedly.
“ He will get better ? ” she asked.
“He will,” Max answered; “but I have news
for him that will take away his pleasure in life.
He is to be reduced nearly ,£lOO a year. ”
“Oh she said, and looked at him with large
e yes of distress. ‘ ‘ Oh, don’t tell him yet —please 1 ”
Max promised her and drew pleasure from her
sorrowing.
“My angel!” he said, “how soft is your
heart! ”
She might have cried with absolute safety, so
enraptured of her pitying tenderness was he. For
a few fleeting minutes he envied Reissler his illness.
But as day followed day, and Reissler’s condi-
tion roused fears among the very few who cared
for him, Pearl grew grave and white. She was
almost a lay figure in the love-scene now.
‘‘He is poorer than ever,” said her mother,
You were sensible before—don’t lose your sense
when he is <£loo a year poorer.”
“ But it makes me care more,” said Pearl dis-
mally.
“ He will probably be an invalid for life,” said
her mother.
“Yes, I know. I know. And I could nurse
him,” said the girl; and she went away to indulge
in dreams which showed her Reissler’s head sink-
ing on her shoulder and she herself administering
mutton-broth.
She could imagine him now uncheered, uncared-
for, alone in the desert of a boarding-house—
without the mutton-broth.
Max assured her that his bodily needs were
supplied.
“ I think he wants his mother ;or perhaps—it is
possible—his heart’s dearest,” he said ; and he
tried to enfold her.
But she slipped away. “ Why ? ” she asked.
He laughed. “We are all alike,” he said. “W<
always want them when we are ill—our hearts’
dearests. Why—what is the matter, my angel ? ”
She told him a small, incoherent story. It wa
about Reissler and his love—a close friend of hers.
He listened carefully, watching her. When she
paused, expecting passionate denunciation, he
brightened.
“ I have it,” he said, and ran his fingers through
his hair. “ They must meet. My angel with the
soft heart, you shall take his lofe to him ! I will
prepare him. Then—we close the door and leave
them together ! ”
“ But—she is engaged to some one else ! ”
“ Oh, true! that I forgot. Um ! that is not
goot. That is not goot! It must be hard for Got
himself to arrange these things. What shall we
do, my angel ? ”
She put out her hands supplicatingly, and her
face whitened.
“You must take me to him,” she said, and her
hand slipped into his in its old way.
A sharp silent minute dropped in between them
and made angular the beautiful roundness of
Max’s youth. The years ran back again to 45,
and his face went grey.
“My Got!” he said, “I had not thought of
this. lam not prepared.”
“ I can cure him,” she whispered. “We cannot
let him die. I love him—l can cure him. And
you will help us—oh, Max, you have such power.
You must not let him be reduced ! Eor mv
sake ! ”
“I am not prepared, Pearl!” he said again.
“ My Got ! I had not thought of this.”
The heaviness of this sorrow he could not shake
off. His second youth had no recuperative power.
He put on his spectacles again and saw To-day
with the eyes of an elderly man, and he strove to
get under way with life again.
“I am too old ! too old !” he said ; “but my
arm is strong enough to kill myself and him. I
hate him ! I cannot do him goot. They may
starve! ”
But they did not. Reissler’s salary went up to
.£(300 a year, and Max’s down to little more.
“ He will not marry now,” Pearl’s mother said
to her—“it went too hard with him. He may
remember you in his will—but the castle will pass
to strangers.”
But for sheer infatuation Pearl had no equal.
“ We can have a cottage—and love,” she said.
N.S.W.
LILIAN TURNER
A Miner’s Soliloquy.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
At night when I come from the gully
To my lonely old camp by the dam,
As the dusk is beginning to sully
The glory of Sol’s oriflamme;
Whilst the battered old billy is boiling
I gingerly seek my abode,
To find the result of my toiling
A weight to the load !
And my pulse goes a beat or two quicker,
And I feel just a little bit mad,
Por I kept from the women and liquor
And saved the few bob that I had.
Just see how your “ Providence ” jockeys
To the death his unfortunate hack !
I must “ bandicoot ” spuds from the cookies—
Or go on the track !
I wouldn’t reply to her letter;
I’d give ’em a pleasant surprise. . .
Down, Rowdy! and camp there, you’d better—
It’s only the smoke in my eyes.
Down, dog ! I ain’t grieving, you duffer,
Dame Fortune shan’t see that we fret;
We’ll show the foul witch we can suffer
And laugh at her yet.
For Fortune’s a surly hound, Rowdy;
Yap at her, she’ll yap back at you ;
And because her old phizog is cloudy
Is no reason why ours should be blue.
Wag your tail at her, lad ; don’t malign her ;
Now we’re going to skip from the scene,
For we’ll roll, the first chirp of the minah,
For Jembaicumbene.
Just a weight! And she bottomed at forty—
A miner will know what it means.
A weight! and the prospects were rorty
After driving her into the teens ;
How I tigered and timbered, and numbered
The days that would keep us apart,
For Hope sang a song that had slumbered
For years in my heart.
Sang the same old sweet lie of the faces
I loved and should soon see again ;
The same old sweet song, with the traces
Of tears in its tender refrain ;
Sang of Home till my idiot vision
Brought Fancy to paint in his school,
And Fate mocked aloud in derision
The dream of a fool.
Hark, Rowdy ! They’re singing up yonder—
Your playmates away on the hill:
Will they miss me, the darlings, I wonder,
Now the creaking old cradle is still ?
Will they miss the gruff miner a-roaming,
Whose surliness never was meant;
Who yearned every night for their coming,
Who sighed when they went ?
. And their laughter—an echo of Heaven—
Comes rip-rippling down on the air;
But I cower, the veriest craven,
As the wind brings the sound to my ear ;
For the laughter, so ringing and pleasant,
Has brought me a theme that I dread,
Has mingled the Past with the Present—
The quick with the dead.
For the rush of a dark Northern river,
Its swirl thro’ the rocks and its roar,
The lap of the tide and the quiver
Of bamboos that girded its shore,
The touch of a hand and the whisper
Of words that are searing mv brain,
The song of a child and—our Mizpah !
I live o’er again.
Merry miners themselves, are the children,
In a claim never boomed on the mart,
And the gold that they raise is bewild’rin’
From the hard granite rock of the Heart;
Tho’ deep as Hell’s gate —they will plumb it;
Tho’ fouler than Lethe its flood,
They will bring, thro’Love’s shaft, to the summit
The pure and the good.
As I sit -in the deepest dejection,
Their voices fall soft as the rain,
And the starved, thirsty soil of affection
Drinks greedily in ev’ry strain,
Till the shadows that haunt me have vanished
To the Past where the shadows belong;
By the magical wand are they banished
Of an innocent’s song.
Old Earth has no music that gladdens
The heart like the glee of a child ;
The tone of the aged but saddens
The soul that the song has beguiled ;
Oh ! the bright, happy carol of Child-life,
So free from old Care and his frown ! >
So joyous and gay in the wild-life,
So choked in the town !
I shall miss them as twilight is glowing,
And I brood by the camp-fire alone,
With the butcher-birds’ melody flowing
As liquid and pure as their own ;
I shall miss the glad radiance darting
From each sweet, sunny face in its glee—
Ah ! they don’t know the pang of the parting
To Rowdy and me !
For I leave the old gully to-morrow,
To gaze on its beauty no more,
But the voices that softened my sorrow -
I’ll hoard with the treasures of yore;
When my last camp is pitched in the valley
That’s shaded by Death’s gloomy wing,
May the voices that sang in the gully
My requiem sing !
Forest Reefs (N.S.W.)
FRANK G. M'COY.


“A Case of Affinity.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ . . . They meet somewhere as by Fate, and see
each other, and they instantly know, as by a kind cf
instinct, that they are consorts.” — Swedenborg.
Warooma is a decidedly ugly township—
the usual repugnant combination of weather-
boards and corrugated-iron. In the spring, the
country round clothes itself with a sort of spurious
pleasantness ; the grass, then, is long and green.
But as soon as the sun becomes hot, the greenness
dies away and leaves the land naked and shameful
—dried-up grass, yellow sand, crooked dog-leg
fences, and all as fiat as back-block beer.
There is a creek, which the residents much prize
and call a “river.” It comes from somewhere
where there are hills and rains. On its long
journey, it grows by degrees smaller and slower
until, a few miles beyond Warooma, it spreads
itself out into a shallow frying-pan, where the sun
frizzles it up.
But by the township it is fairly deep, although
narrow, and there are gum-trees along its banks.
It is here that the young men and maidens of
Warooma “ hunt in couples,” and get fixed up for
life. Or a part of it.
Here also is the scene of a romantic tragedy dear
to the hearts of Warooma people. It took place
more than twenty years ago, but it is treasured up
in their memories, and will, no doubt, descend
from parents to children for ever and ever. They
can still point out the old gum-tree, with its long
and suggestive branch reaching over the water to
which the young man hung himself, and also the
identical pool in the creek where the faithless
maiden who jilted him, with true artistic instinct,
afterwards drowned herself.
But I did not learn all these things until after
the adventure which I am going to describe.
m * * +
About an hour after I had alighted from the
coach for the first tune in Warooma, I left the
hotel intending to take a stroll and some fresh air
before going to bed.
I walked down to the end of the main street and
stopped there, uncertain whether to go on a little
way into the country or return.
While I stood there hesitating my eyes fell on
an old hut a few chains along the road, and I ex-
perienced an eerie sensation of familiarity with it.
It was a tumble-down shanty without door or
windows, and surrounded by a few posts—the re
mains of a fence. As I looked at it the feeling
passed off. I had never seen it before.
GOOD BUSINESS.
LITTLE MARY ANN SMITH (to storekeeper-postmaster): “ Please put a stamp on this ’ere letter.
Mother’ll send the eggs to pay for it when the ’eus lays them.”
Alniost unconsciously I walked on until I came
~0 an old wooden bridge that spanned the creek,
As I ; stepped on to it the same feeling of some
long-'ago familiarity with the place came over me.
It was a lovely evening, clear and cool. The
moori was right overhead, and its soft light had
transformed the scene into one of grotesque
beauty.
When I revisited the place by daylight I hardly
recognised it.
I crossed the bridge, and strolled along by the
side of the creek. I had not gone far when I came
to a sadden standstill.
* * * *
Just before me was a large gum-tree with one
long branch extended over the creek, and under
the branch stood a girl. She was perfectly
motionless, gazing intently into the water. She
held a large sun-hat in her hand, and the wind
had ruffled her hair into disorder. The moonlight
fell full upon her. In figure she was small and
slight, and the outline of her face was regular and
beautiful.
Drawn by an irresistible influence, I walked
right up to her.
She turned round and smiled without any ex-
pression of surprise in her pretty face.
“ Good evening, Lucyl” I said mechanically.
“ Good evening, Frank ! ” she replied, wrinkling
her forehead perplexedly ; “ why do you call me
Lucy ? ”
“ I am not Frank ! ” I answered, rather irri-
tably, “ and I don’t know why I called you Lucy.
I have never seen you before.”
“Nor I you!” she said, slowly; “and yet I
seem to have known you long ago. Have you
never been here before ? ”
“ Never.”
“ Well, I have never been away for any time,”
she said.
“ Will you tell me why you came here to-night? ”
I asked.
“ I came to meet you,” she said, dreamily, and
then she shook her head as though confused, and
added “ I don’t mean that. I don’t know why I
carne.” Her eyes looked straight into mine, full
of bewilderment. _
We were standing close together, and our hands
came into contact. I took her hand into mine. It
was very small and soft. She did not seem to
notice. A sudden wave of passion took possession
of me. I slipped my arm round her waist and
drew her against me, and bent my head to kiss
her, but she hid her face in my coat.
“Lucy,” I said awkwardly; “I want to kiss
you ! ”
“No, don’t!”
“ I will—l must,” said I.
She turned her face up to me, smiling resignedly.
“ If you will, you will,” she said.
I kissed her on the forehead. It wasn’t quite
what I had intended, but my face was hot, and I
felt ashamed.
Then she slipped out of my arms and stepped
back. That kiss seemed to have transformed her.
Her whole manner changed in an instant: from
being soft and yielding, she became quick and
alert. Her eyes fairly danced, and her lips curved
mischievously. Then she broke into a peal of
merry laughter. It was the prettiest laugh I have
ever heard, but at the time it was disconcerting.
How you did blush ! ” she cried ; “ and you don’t
know how to kiss at all ! ” Then she turned round
and started to run for it. But she hadn’t a chance.
I held her firmly, and, in spite of her struggles,
kissed her pretty mouth and eyes again and again.
Then she ceased struggling, and her lithe arms
crept round my neck and her lips grew softer and
tinging,
Then we drew apart, both her hands in mine,
nd gazed at each other silently,
She broke the tension. “I must go!”
“ Oh, don't go yet,” I answered,
“I must!”
For a moment I hesitated. She seemed to guess
my thoughts.
“Please, Frank!” she said, looking straight
into my eyes.
A sudden feeling of tenderness came over me.
I kissed her hands and let her draw them
away.
ISLAND EXPERIENCE.
LITTLE GIRL (to visitor, who is relating his adventures in the South Seas) : “ Grandpa’s
been on the islands, too."
VISITOR (looking towards the old gentleman with the Botany Bay limp): “ Where,
my dear?”
LITTLE GIRL: “ Cockatoo! ain’t you, Grandpa?"
“ Good-night! ” she said.
I stood there for some time after she had dis-
appeared, and then I slowly and unwillingly
started homewards, like one who has half awakened
from a pleasant dream.
* * * *
On the following morning I was standing
at the hotel door talking with my friend Jameson,
when who should appear on the scene but my
mysterious sweetheart ! She was approaching us
by the side of an over-dressed young man. I saw
her glance towards me and away again.
As she passed us she looked me straight in the
face for a second and then looked away
again without a sign of recognition.
“Who was that girl?” I
»sked Jameson.
“A daughter of Nelson’s.
Going to marry the man with
her in a few weeks. Pretty,
isn’t she ? ”
“Think so?” I said coolly,
a black anger under my skin —
“she looks rather fast ! ”
“What a fool I was,” I
thought to myself, “to let the
little minx escape ! ”
A lost opportunity is an
awful thing to have on one’s
conscience.
» * *
Jameson and I were
strolling along by the creek
when we came to the gum-
tree under which I had found
“ Lucy.”
“Do you know, Jameson,”
I said, pointing to the long
branch —“I have an absurd
inclination to hang myself to
that!”
He stared at me astonished.
“ What on earth made you
say that ? ”
“I don’t know!” I an-
swered, smiling at his serious-
ness ; “it was just one of those
idiotic ideas that sometimes
cross one’s mind.”
“You haven’t heard the
story of this tree, then? ”
“No ! What story ? ”
“Well,” he said, “about
twenty-five years ago, a young
fellow hung himself to this
tree. He was very much in
love with a girl who jilted
him. She drowned herself a
few days afterwards. I can’t
remember all the yarn, but
heaps of people in Warooma
will tell you about it. It
was strange that the idea of
hanging in connection with
this particular tree should have
occurred to you.”
“ Just a coincidence ! ” I
replied. “Do you remember
the names of the romantic
young people ? ”
“The man was Frank
Towers,” he answered.
“ And the girl was Lucy something,” I sug-
gested.
“Yes, Lucy . . . but how do you know ? You
must have heard the yarn.”
“Perhaps so ! ” I answered ; “ let’s go home and
get lunch.”
* * * *
I wonder how many more lives Lucy and
I will have to toil through before things come
right ? or whether we shall go on meeting
and parting in that identical spot for ever
and ever? Meanwhile, I am going to marry
Alice. Poor Alice ! We have been engaged for
so long that I almost forget I was ever in love
with her.
Such is life.
Vic.
ARTHUR WATTS.
The Coach of Death.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
There’s a phantom-coach runs nightly along the
Western creeks,
Her four black steeds step lightly, her driver never
speaks.
For welcome or for warning she shows no lamp or
light,
A shadow till the morning she steals across the
night ;
She never wants for passengers, the back-creek
settlers say,
A price so moderate as hers the poorest purse can
pay ;
The lost one, missed by measure of days or maybe
weeks,
Pays lightly for the pleasure of coaching down the
creeks,
And station-hands and squatters, alike in ease
reclined,
May praise the whirling trotters whose hoofs out-
strip the wind.
You hear no lead-bars creaking, no footfall on the
ground,
In silence past all speaking the flying wheels go
round ;
The horses have no breeders, their driver has no
name,
But he swings his reefing leaders like a man that
knows the game;
And through the stony ridges where swift hoofs
strike no fire
They want no wayside changes, these steeds that
never tire.
They’re fit to “ stay ” for ever, and are never short
of work ;
They run the Darling River from Menindie Lake
to Bourlce,
Where a thousand watercourses, bank-full or bound
with drought,
Have seen the silent horses go glidingin and out,
Where the stars in red battalions are marshalled
in the sky
To watch the black - maned stallions with the
muffled hoofs go by.
They fear him on the Barwon and curse him on
the Bree,
And wish his goal a far one, where’er that goal
may be;
But ever back and forward, in silence, source to
mouth,
He runs the rivers Nor’ward and runs the rivers
South.
In drifted flood-wrack sailing on every swirling
stream
He hears his patrons hailing, and checks his noise-
less team ;
In belts of timber shady, when all the holes are
dry,
His guests are waiting ready when the phantom
wheels go by,
For every man in good time must book for Further
Out —
It may be in the Flood-time —it may be in the
Drought!
Her load of clay-cold faces she never carries back,
The dim wheels leave no traces, the shoeless hoofs
no track;
But all along the river in the bends that she has
passed
The giant gum-trees shiver in a strange and icy
blast;
The clumps of scented sandal are tainted with her
breath,
And teams are hard to handle behind the Coach
of Death.
She lifts no mud in winter, and stirs no summer
dust,
Her pole-bars never splinter, her lock-bolts never
rust;
Her parts are stout for wearing and strong her
simple gear,
She’ll run without repairing from year to deathful
year ;
When every coach is rotten on the Western water-
shed,
And Cobb and Co. forgotten and all their drivers
dead !
N.S.W.

Monk’s Scheme.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Monk was a tall, dark, thin, morose man, who
looked as if he had been built hastily and upon
the run-’em-up principle. There was in his actions
, , ~
an uncomfortable suggestion that he would
assuredly collapse in the next gale. He had high
cheek-bones, meagre, dusty-black whiskers, and a
long, pendulous nose, afflicted with a twitch that
made him a continuous joy to the small boys
1 , . . . . /
going heaven-wards under compulsion via the
little wooden chapel on the corner. Monk had
small, misty eyes that floated in their sockets
without volition. He was a ponderous and melan-
choly man, and spent most of his time out of
doors, backed up against his house, or shuffling
dejectedly up and
down the neglect-
ed garden walks,
“making inven-
tions,” my land-
lady said.
I had come to
live next door to
this man, and as
he was the only
view from my room
window I present-
ly knew him pretty
well by sight, and
used to wonder
what he was living
for. He struck me
as one who might
possibly have done
some good in the
world, but for his
feet, the largest
and most inartistic
extremities I have
ever seen out of
the pages of an
American comic
paper. He was
apparently so con-
scious of the hope-
lessness of any
physical effort on
his part, hampered
and handicapped

wide, flat, un-
wieldy feet that
were lark-heeled,
pigeon - toed and
over - r u n with
foreign growths,
that he had fallen
into comparative
inertia, and con-
tinually brooded
over his boots,
looking down upon
them with a hurt,
accusing expres-
sion that was un-
speakably pathe-
tic.
Under these cir-
cumstances, it de-
volved upon Mrs.
Monk to keep the
house going, which
she did by running
a music-school for
infants and begin-
ners. None of the
pupils ever got
beyond the begin-

tortured piano
wailed and shriek-

in our street, and no dog could be induced to live
in the neighborhood.
Monk was an inventor; he had invented a patent,
ventilated, damp-proof coffin that had turned out
t F eat suecess f° r another man as a butter-cooler,
I believe, and he was now busy on a new boot
that supplied its own motive-power and would
walk alone.
