beloved dead is hurried underground in this
country—’specially in summer. Likely enough
you’ve lost a dear one yourself an’ been harrared
up when they tore the departed from your bosom
to bury it in the dismal tomb. Bodies turn very
quick this time o’ the year. My idea ’ll change
all that—mourners ’ll be able to keep deceased by
them for months if they want to —keep him quite
fresh an’ sweet.”
“ When there’s a epidemic about—typhoid, in-
fluenza, scarlet-fever—it often happens three or
four’ll die in one fambily in the course of a month
or so. What’s the consequence ? People are
bothered out o’ their wits with funerals, and
ruined, most like, with the fearful expense
of em an the cost of openin’ up the old grave or
buyin’ new ones. My scheme does away with
superfluous buryin’ an’ extra wear an’ tear. Save
up your dead till the epidemic’s over, an’ then
bury ’em all together in a job lot ! ”
“ How is it done ? Easy enough. Cool storage
that’s the idea ! We build a refrigeratin’ cham-
ber, same as for freezin’ mutton. Advertise
funerals at ten shillin’s a head. People rush us
with orders. We store the bodies iirthe freezin’-
room till we have a good hearse-load, an’ one
cartin does for the lot. It must have struck you
—the ridiculousness of puttin’one solitary coffin
in a big hearse, an ; hirin’ a lot of undertaker’s men,
an’ employin’ two, maybe four, horses to haul it
to the cemetery, when you might save money,
time, an’ wear an’ tear by makin’ one journey of
it ’ _ I used to puzzle over that when I was in
business, but couldn’t get over the difficulty of
storin’ ’em. I’ve hit on the idea at last, an’ there’s
millions in it ! —millions in it! ”
Monk had been pecking nervously at my knee
with his dexter finger all through this explana-
tion ; he finished with an energetic blow, and
backed off, flushed and triumphant, to note the
impression.
“S’pose people want to keep the late lamented
for a while,” he continued, fearing that I was still
unconvinced—" heart-broken widow, an’ all that,
you know, wishes to have the mortal remains of
her loving husband where she can drop in an’
see him any time—we store him on ice under
glass, with paper filagree all round him, everythin’
lovely, an’ charge so much a week for cool storage.
Won’t that catch on an’be pop’lar? Blesh you!
we’ll have the place full of ’em. Money! why
we’ll be rollin’ in it, take my word ! We would
have a branch freezin’ works in each suburb. Most
mourners, ’specially widows, ’ud rejoice to have
deceased near, where they could go an’ weep over
him on Sundays for a few months, an’ put flowers
on his still, cold breast. There ain’t much satis-
faction in a mound of dirt for a sorrowin’ widow,
besides the cost of trammin’ it to the cemetery.
Now, what d’ye think of the prospects of the
Metropolitan Funeral Reform and Mortuary Cold
Storage Company? ”
Monk had risen, and his concluding words were
spoken with the air of a man who had accomplished
a great thing, and who realised the fact.
I temporised. I said the idea was a brilliant
one. I think I succeeded in convincing him that
it dazzled me. It was, I said, an inspiration, the
master-stroke of a great and original mind. It
should not be trusted to the hands of the inexperi-
enced, but should be brought under the notice of
the acutest intellects in the country.
Monk brightened.
“Would you advise me to move the Govern-
ment ?” he asked eagerly.
“ That s the idea—move the Government.”
He arose again, glowing with resolution, but his
cumbersome feet weighed him down, and he sat
once more and sighed reproachfully.
Now, would you undertake to move the
Government, and stand in ? ”
I explained that I was not on good terms with
the Government, that the Government was preju-
diced against me for various reasons, and my
appearance in the matter would be likely to bias
the powers against his epoch-marking idea. So
he left me, dragging his feet after him, resolved to
move the Government in sundry places.
About a week later Monk whispered through
the fence that the Government was visibly moved
but I heard no more of the Metropolitan Funeral
Reform and Mortuary Cool Storage Company
and presently Monk was leaning up against the
sunny side of his house again, gazing dejectedly at
his feet. Edward Dyson.
EDWARD DYSON.
A Song of Winds.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Woe to the weak when the sky is shrouded,
And the wind of the salt-way sobs as it dies !
Woe to the weak, for a great dejection
Droops their spirits and drowns their eyes;
Woe to the weak who tire of fetters,
Of grim life-fetters that gall and bind !
For the sea tells stories of death made lovely,
And a siren sings in the nor’-east wind.
It wanders the coast like a tombless spectre,
And drips dank dew on the drooping leaf ;
And the soul grows pensive with dim suggestion?
Of grey, old troubles and ancient grief.
Tis grave and low, and with woeful plaining
Sighs death-notes under a sky of grey,
And who hath an ear may hear the voices
Of pale men dead on its streaked sea-way.
In fading twilights o’er sullen seascapes,
A lost, wan wind ’neath a dead, grey sky,
It swoons to land like a weary swimmer,
Sobs and falters and turns to die.
Seeking a tomb in dark, coast caverns,
Where the wet rust reddens the fretted stone,
The wandering sea-thing sinks to silence —
Sinks and dies with a last low moan ;
A last low moan and deadly stillness,
Then the sudden crash of a league-long sea,
And fresh from his den in the white ice region
The wolf of the South is speeding free.
Cleaving the air with his chill, grey shoulders,
And trampling the sea to foam beneath,
The wolf of the South goes howling nor’ard,
A mastless hull in his long, white teeth.
And flying high, a far, faint phalanx
Wings its way to a northern clime,
Sending feathers of sad sound downward,
Singing songs of an evil time :
An evil time, for the black night chases
And darkness swallows the trailing flock ;
An evil season of wild, white weather,
And foam and tumult on reef and rock ;
Of yellow floods on the northern rivers,
And fierce waves swaying from crest to trough ;
Of creaking schooners wearing seaward,
And signals crying—Stand off ! stand off !
Of frothy flakes on the wild waste flying,
And anxious faces and fateful news ;
Of close-reefed topsails and battened hatche:,
And straining engines and racing screws.
Of pumice-stone and brown weeds riven,
Riven and flung on the hissing sand ;
Of the squadroned waves and their mighty charging
And the stern repulse of the frowning land.
Of whipped, white faces faring stormward
With smothered words and wrecked replies ;
Of trees blown down on the windy ridgesg,
And stormy shoutings and tempest cries ;
Of eyes that dance to the wild wind’s music,
Of strange sweet thrills through the calm-sick
form ;
Of Storm, throned king on the mad, white ocean,
Of Storm the Monarch—all hail to Storm !
RODERIC QUINN.
Consolation.—Song Words.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
It could not grieve rue much (not much) to hear
that thou hadst died,
So many seas between us twain had long my love
defied ;
The giant hills my gaolers were ; and all Earth’s
green and blue
The painting of the gilded cage that hid me from
thy view.
Now, Earth’s sad meaning ever lost, unmoved I
pace its shore;
Eor us the hills have sunken down, the seas divide
no more;
And in the watches of the night I tell my love
to thee,
Whom Death but captured from the world to
render back to me.
Maoriland.
CONSTANCE CLYDE.
Pirate Newnes’s paste-pot paper Tit-Bits
prints that the people of Nelson, M.L., live chiefly
on locusts, which grow to an immense size down
that way. But it omits to state that in Brisbane
the regular Sunday dinner consists of woolly-
aphis-shinbone soup, followed by roast haunch of
Rockhampton mosquito and stinging-tree pie;
also, that the fashionable toothpick of an Adelaide
johnnie is a gilded bristle from an earthworm’s
eyebrow ; likewise that centipede curry is sold in
every Melbourne restaurant at 3d. the large plate,
with a glass of oyster’s milk thrown in.


The Worthington Pumping
Engine Co.
The Worthington organisation is the largest
in the world exclusively engaged in the
manufacture of pumping machinery ; and its
offices and agencies are established in every
important centre throughout the globe. The
eminent engineering firm of Messrs. James
Simpson and Co., Ltd., Grosvenor-road,
London, who are over a century old, and
who always stood as the leading English
manufacturers of pumping machinery
arranged in 1884 with Messrs. H. R.
Compound Worthington Surface Condensing Hydraulic
Pumping Engine.
Worthington, of New York, U.S.A., to take
up the manufacture of the larger sized
Worthington engines. No stronger evidence
than this could be afforded to prove the
efficiency of the Worthington engines. And
no better guarantee could be given than is
sustained in the fact that the Worthington
Pump to-day represents the combined ex-
periences of two such world-famous firms as
Messrs. H. R. Worthington and Messrs.
James Simpson and Co. One flattering
testimony to Worthington value and import-
ance is adduced by those manufacturers, who
Compound Mine Pump.
(Lehigh Pattern.)
put upon the market Duplex pumps, which
are mere attempts to copy the original
Worthington Duplex.
* * * *
For economical pumping and certainty of
reliability, the Worthington pump is being
adopted for lifts as low as 15ft. ; and except
for intermittent or temporary work, putting
down pumps of the centrifugal type, would
not be entertained by engineers who have
had experience with both types. The
Worthington
has the ad-
vantage o f
the centrifu-
gal pump as
regards con-
tinuous flow
in the suction
and delivery,
while its efficiency in lifts of over 30ft. is
about double that of the other type.
• * * *
The large volume plunger chambers and
single suction and delivery chambers render
the Worthington Pumps eminently fitted for
muddy river waters, and no better example
of their success can be
cited than their em-
ployment on a very
large scale to pump the
muddy waters of the
Hoogley at Calcutta.
They .are also largely
utilised at the Water
Works Intake Station,
Mississippi River. All
over the world they are
used for pumping water
into filter-beds or
settling reservoirs.
When the water is
full of sand of a gritty
nature, outside or ex-
ternally packed plungers
are resorted to.

son and Co. supplied to the West Mid-
dlesex Water Works Company’s pump-
ing station Hampton, two vertical triple
expansion high duty Worthington Pumping
Engines when the two Bull Cornish engines
were pulled down. Subse-
quently they supplied a third
engine capable of lifting 16
million gallons water per day
to a head of 120 feet, and
working with a boiler pressure
of 120 lbs. per square inch.

stations four

It must be borne in mind that the Worth-

In the
United
States Messrs. Henry R. Wor-
thington have equipped over
1000 cities with water-works
pumping machinery. The
greater portion of the crude oil
which comes through many
miles of pipes in America is
pumped by Worthington En-
gines, for they work so smoothly
and steadily that engines of
400 h.p., working against a
pressure of 18001 b. per spuare inch, can be
employed without bursting the mains.
* * * *
At the Chicago Exhibition the Wor-
thington Engines presented the most striking
exhibit of pumping machinery ever seen,
and pumped 70,000,000 gallons every 24
hours. This grand exhibit represented
£40,000. In England, Europe, Asia, and
Africa Messrs. Simpson and Co. have sup-
plied Worthington Engines with magnificent
success.
* * * *
The pumping at the Johannesburg Water
Works, South Africa, is done by Worthing-
ton Engines. At Bombay Worthington
Compound Jet Condensing Worthington Pump.
Engines manage 60,000,000 gallons ©f sewage
every 24 hours. At Osaka, Japan, at Hong-
kong, and at all the principal Indian cities
Worthington Engines are employed upon
waterworks. The Worthington Engines em-
ployed by the Sydney Metropolitan Board of
Water Supply and Sewage at Crown-street
Pumping Station have a capacity of 12,000,000
and 8,000,000 gallons per day respectively.
The Board certifies that they perform their
work economically and most satisfactorily.
These Crown-street engines are well-worth
inspecting, because they are the largest
Compound Condensing Packed Plunger Pump.
installation of pumping machinery in the
Southern Hemisphere.

manufacture will be continued in the future
as in the past, and with every prospect of
securing results which have heretofore been
as gratifying to the Company as to that
portion of the public with which it has come
into business contact.

When Barney was at last persuaded to
start, the effects of the week’s spree combined
with the heat to make him delirious, and his mates
had a hard job to keep him in the saddle. Sud-
denly they came across an immense black snake
sunning himself in the dust of the road. The
moment Barney saw it his hair fairly stood on end,
and, despite all efforts to hold him, he succeeded
in falling off his horse, and, volleying curses, made
a grab at the snake. The reptile, fully roused,
showed fight, and when Barney in his drunken
fury seized it amidships, fastened on to his arm.
“ Bite me, you crawler ! I’ll chew you alive,”
said Barney as he made a return bite at the snake.
And for every bite the snake gave Barney he got
one in return, till they had nearly chewed one
another up. At last the snake gave in honorably,
being twisted and bitten out of knowledge, and
Barney, now nearly sober, came to his mates look-
ing rather white, and declared that he felt blanky
bad on it. He was hurried back to town and
taken to a doctor, who remarked after examina-
tion : “You can rest satisfied, my man. The
alcoholic poison in your blood when you were
bitten was quite enough to counteract any poison
the snake was able to inject. Indeed, I think you
gave him quite as good a dose as he gave you.”
And Barney, when he tells the story, enquires
where the blank nowadays will you get liquor like
the home-made rum they gave you at Ipswich in
the sixties.
The voices of a hundred thousand travellers
declare that the French steamship service from
Australia to Europe is equal to any steamship ser-
vice in the world. The vessels are of the largest
and swiftest, and the accommodation is specially
designed so as to give the greatest possible con-
venience to patrons. The cuisine—a special feature
—is justly praised. More, as preparatory to u
Continental trip, a voyage by the Armand Behic
or the Australien is a charming preliminary, since
one gains quite a nodding acquaintance with the
indispensable tongue of parley-voo. Yet there
need be, no apprehension of difficulty, for all the
officers speak English, and English stewards and
stewardesses are carried. The time of voyage
from Adelaide to London is 30 days, and a 14-
days stop-over privilege enables Australian won-
derers to sip the charms of France or the delights
of Paris. And so the tourist enters Europe by its
most glittering gates. There is no route to the
Old World so attractive as the route of the Mes-
sageries Maritimes.*

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When first I felt struts upon Stella,
Quite vainly I strove for her love—
She would laugh if I kissed her umbrella,
Or fondled her four-button glove—
A laugh truly heartless and freezing,
As she said, with a curl of the lip,
Contemptuous, rather than teasing,
“Don’t you wish I was under the whip?”
More caustic the question than teasing—
“ Don’t you wish I was under the whip ? ”
Being wise in her young generation,
She knew no suspicion of doubt
That love is a passing sensation
Which women are better without.
She had studied the game circumspectly,
And a widow once gave her the “ tip ”
That “ boys ” become bullies directly
Their sweethearts get under the whip—
Tyrannical creatures directly
Their sweethearts get under the whip.
But 0, there was rapture in courting
A maid of such singular strength ;
On her text (it is slangily sporting)
She often addressed me at length,
Yet I never once ceased persevering
(The prize was too good to let slip,
Her charms were so fresh and endearing)
Till the filly was under the whip ;
No, I never once ceased persevering
Until she was under the whip.
We had gone to a picnic together;
And another girl threw me a look,
As warm as the midsummer weather,
And clear as the neighboring brook.
My girl in a moment grew fonder,
On my arm she inflicted a nip.
Happy thought! Ruby Ray, flirting yonder,
Would serve very well for a whip :
The sylph making eyes at me yonder
Would serve very well for a whip.
Then, then was my chance of achieving
A mastery over the jade ;
That bosom, reluctantly heaving,
Her one little weakness betrayed.
Without any amorous parley
She drew my left hand to her hip,
And sweetly addressed me as “ Charley.”
Hooroo ! She was under the whip—
She put such expression in “ Charley,”
I knew she was under the whip.
Her manner grew gentle and pleading,
Whilst mine was quite stony and cool ;
She sadly rebuked me for heeding
The tricks of “ that fast little fool,
Who made such a mess of the salad.”
Well, ere we returned from our trip
In the words aforesaid of the ballad,
My angel was under the whip
In the beautiful words of the ballad—
At last she was under the whip.
And now she’s a tractable charmer,
And I am despotic at times ;
In tones that distress and alarm her
I sum up her failings as crimes.
So she settles the matter by crying,
And holding my neck in a grip ;
Which proves, beyond honest denying,
I’ve got her right under the whip—
Stella makes no attempt at denying
She’s glad to be under the whip.
EDMUND FISHER.


The Dental College and Oral
Hospital of Victoria.
It is only of late years that dentistry has
taken its place as an indispensable branch of
medical science, although the medieval sur-
geon or blood-letter was not only contem-
porary with the dentist or tooth-drawer and
the barber, but, more frequently than not,
united all three functions in his single per-
son. But the tendency of progress is to dis-
count versatility, to encourage specialisation,
and to insist on the excellence in a single art
or science which can only be won by life-long
devotion and study. Hence the dentist, first
as the specialist of a single art, and, secondly,
as the invaluable coadjutor of the medical
scientists of to-day. The human body is so
delicate and complex a machine that its well-
being and harmonious working depend on
the perfect regulation of all its parts ; neglect
a single one of them and disaster is sure to
overtake the whole. And the teeth are an
exceedingly important factor in this wonder-
ful structure, for on them the stomach
THE DENTAL COLLEGE AND ORAL HOSPITAL OF VICTORIA.
THE PROSTHETIC LABORATORY. THE FILLING ROOM.
Photos, by Falk.
depends for the proper preparation of its
fuel, and the whole body is therefore sen-
sible either of their effective or defective
working. This is why
the development of
dentistry as a special
science has won the
co-operation and sup-
port of the medical
faculty throughout the
world. All modern
efforts are directed to
the conservation of
the teeth, and here the
time - worn adage of
‘ ‘ prevention being
better than cure " has
a very special appli-
cation.
* * *
Although Victoria
has taken so foremost
a position as a centre
of liberal education, it
is singular that in Mel-
bourne the special
study of dentistry owes
its birth and progress
to the enthusiasm and
courage of a private
individual, who has
founded a college and
hospital on an ambi-
tious scale entirely at
his own expense, and
has won for it respect
as well as recognition
notwithstanding the
perpetual irritations of
petty local jealousies
and the initial distrust
with which the inno-
vator is invariably re-
garded. (It may here
be noted that in Melbourne the women’s,
children’s, and eye and ear hospitals all owe
their inauguration to individual efforts.)
The Dental College
and Oral Hospital of
Victoria, founded by
Mr. E. Lenthall Old-
field in January, 1895,
has made a wonderful
record in the infancy
of its existence, and
has proved itself a valu-
able addition to the
existing charitable and
educational institu-
tions. Patients who
cannot pay for dental
services are treated
here for dental and
oral complaints, free
of charge, in the same
manner that their
other affections are
treated at the gene-
ral hospitals. Fifty-
seven of the leading
Melbourne physicians—ladies and gentle-
men co-operate with Mr. Oldfield in
this charitable work-in the administra-
tion of anaesthetics, and in performing sub
sidiary oral operations.
THE LECTURE ROOM.
Photo, by Falk.
Last year's figures will give some idea of
the extent of the hospital practice. During
this term there were 3192 extractions ; 431

and Principal.

fillings ; 2636 teeth in-
serted with a view to
the preservation of
the health ; and 1664
patients attended.

The College has been
fitted up in a very
efficient and attractive
style, and includes
class-rooms, lavatories,
and a fine lecture-
room, nothing, indeed,
being wanting to
render the educational
advantages as modern
and complete as those
of England and America. At present
there are 22 students, 7 ladies, and
5 post - graduates. The curriculum em-
braces lectures and practical training in
chemistry, metallurgy, materia medica
and therapeutics; general elementary ana.
tomy and physiology; pathology and surgery;
dental anatomy and physiology ; dental
pathology and surgery ; operative dentistry
(fillings) and mechanical dentistry. The
students have also the advantage of the hos
pital practice.
* * * *
Mr. Oldfield offers two scholarships every
year, tenable for four years, one available to
the pupils of the State schools of the pro-
vince and one to those of the private schools.
Either male or female candidates under the
age of 17 years may be submitted, the sub-
jects being Latin, geometry, arithmetic,
algebra, English, and either Greek, Fiench,
or German. One of the interesting features
of the College is the inclusion of women stu-
dents, and their conspicuous success in the
new profession which is thus opened up to
them. The best available lecturers and
demonstrators attend the College daily, and
the education of the students is carried on
with an undeviating system and precision.
» * * *
Prior to the foundation of this College
a complete course of instruction in technical
dentistry was not to be had in Australia, and
students had the alternative of either going
to London or America, or depending on the
somewhat primitive or uncertain system of
studying under local practitioners. There
was rarely any public guarantee of the
quality of teaching secured, or of the
amount of capacity
attained ; and fre-
quently men who
desired and would
have done credit to
the most expert tuition
in dentistry, found
that such tuition was
unprocurable without
a long journey or
great waste of valu-
able time. It is
hardly necessary to
dilate on the supreme
advantages of the col-
legiate system, which
achieves with certainty
a definite result in a
given time, and main-
tains the same high
standard of efficiency
throughout.

A New Departure in Life
Assurance .
One of the distinctive features which have
hitherto characterised the difference between
the old-fashioned system of frequent bonus
distributions and the more modern system
of accumulating bonuses for a fixed period
of years has found expression in the varying
extent to which the custom of granting cash
surrender values has been carried.
There is, and probably always will be
considerable divergence of opinion among
experts as to the exact proportion of the
reserve which it is safe and expedient to
refund to outgoing policy-holders, more
especially during the earlier years of a
policy.
The Equitable has for many years been
constantly urged to combine in one policy
the attractive features of the tontine system
and the cash surrender value feature on a
liberal but safe basis; the Society has, there-
fore, at the special request of its General
Agents, and of a great number of its policy-
holders who desire to increase their assur-
ances, decided to issue a policy of this
character embodying the most attractive
features of the tontine principle as introduced
and established by the Equitable years ago
combined with liberal surrender values both
in cash and paid-up assurance, the actual
amounts of which will be GUARANTEED
by the Society.
While the Equitable Society strongly
advocates the continuance of policies in
force, and earnestly recommends policy-
holders not to surrender their policies or
encumber them with loans except in extreme
emergency, it is believed that this liberal
and attractive policy will fully meet the re-
quirements of the times, embodying as it
does the following
GUARANTEES :
The immediate paying of the face of the
policy in the event of death.
Incontestability at the end of the second
year.
Absolute freedom as to residence, travel,
and occupation.
A guaranteed Cash Surrender Value, the
amount of which increases annually, after
the third year.
A guaranteed surrender value in Paid-up
Assurance, increasing annually, after the
third year.
A loan, at 5 per cent, interest, on the third
or any subsequent anniversary of the policy’s
register date.
A cash Bonus at the end of the period of
twenty years or fifteen years.
If the policy is continued beyond the
period, an annually increasing surrender value
is guaranteed, the amount of which will not
be less than the entire reserve on the policy
in cash, or its equivalent in paid-up assur-
ance.
If the policy is continued beyond the
period, it will receive dividends from the
surplus subsequently earned by the Society.
Thirty days’ grace is allowed in the pay-
ment of all premiums.
such is
THE GUARANTEED CASH-VALUE
POLICY
OP THE
EQUITABLE SOCIETY.
THE FOUNDATION STONE OF ALL.
“ £8,442,502 of surplus over all liabilities ”
is the motto on the streamer of the Equit-
able. That means strength, surplus strength,
strength in reserve, and strength is the one
great thing that the policy-holder looks for
in the company which insures him. If he is
a healthy man (and the society insures no
other) he has a contract reaching into the
indefinite future. Death may overtake him,
it is true, within a month or a year, but the
average healthy man who holds an Equit-
able policy may live thirty years or so, and
some of them will live forty or fifty, and the
great question with the holder of a life con-
tract is, “What will the ability of my
company be when this contract becomes a
claim, and what preparation is this company
making to-day to render that contract good
beyond a peradventure ? ” Dividends are
good, options are good ; there are plenty of
good things in good life assurance ; but the
foundation-stone of all is security ; and while
the man or the company who has pound for
pound of valuable assets to meet all liabilities
is solvent, the man who has tens of thousands
of pounds and the company that has millions
of pounds over and above all liabilities are
safer to trust with the contingencies of a re-
mote and unknown future than those who
have honestly, but merely, provided pound
for pound.
The Record of the Past is the Best
Guarantee of the Future.
During the past thirty - six years the
Equitable has accumulated, in the trans-
action of its business, Total Assets of over
£41,800,000,
•out of which it now holds, for the benefit
of its Policy-holders, Total Surplus funds
amounting to over
£8,400,000,
which greatly exceed the sum of the Surplus
Funds which have been accumulated and are
now held by any other Life Assurance Com-
pany in the world.
During the past ten years the Equitable
has made Total Surplus earnings of over
£9,500,000,
which have been larger than those of any
other company, and has, after paying divi-
dends to Policy-holders, increased, during
the same period, the amount of Surplus held
for its Policy-holders by more than
£5,600,000,
which greatly exceeds the increase of Surplus
accumulated by any other Life Assurance
Company in the same time.
ONE PACT
IS WORTH
A Thousand Theories.
All the Twenty-Year Tontine policies of
the general class issued by the Equitable
Society twenty years ago, whose Tontine
Periods expire in 1896, have present cash
surrender values—
GREATER THAN THE
SUM OP THE PREMIUMS PAID.
Note (1) that this return is during the
lifetime of the assured, and (2) that it is
in addition to the protection afforded by the
assurance during the twenty years.
This is true, whether the policies were
issued at the low rate charged in cases where
it is expected in the beginning that pre-
miums will be paid for life, or whether they
were issued at the higher rate, providing for
the payment of premiums for twenty years
only.
The largest returns are shown on policies
issued on the 20-year Endowment form,
which show a return of the total amount
PAID IN PREMIUMS, with INTEREST AT RATES
BETWEEN FIVE AND SIX PER CENT. PER
ANNUM.
These are actual results based on past
experience.
It would be wise for a person intending to
assure his life to study the record of the
Assurance Company proposed to him, and
learn the facts upon which the prospect of
future dividends and profits are based. In
other words, let him ascertain for himself the
results that have been secured by the Com-
pany suggested, in the accumulation of surplus
during its history as well as its average
profits in recent years.
In entering into a contract which may not
terminate for thirty or forty years, it will
well repay the Assurer to give the subject
the careful investigation that would be de-
voted by him to any other affair of like mag-
nitude and importance. Due inquiry having
been made, let the best company in which
to assure be selected—one whose past record
and present financial condition justify the
belief that in the future it will afford both
the greatest security and the largest profit
of any.
The surplus of any life assurance company
is the result of good management in the past,
proof of financial strength in the present, and
indicative of good profits and dividends in
the future. The Equitable has by many
millions of pounds the largest surplus of any
life assurance company in the world, havitig
over eight million pounds sterling of surplus
over all liabilities.
This seems to be a fitting time to call the
attention of policy-holders to the fact that,
as the Equitable Society’s business is con-
ducted on the mutual basis, its interests are
their interests, and that they, being mem-
bers of the Society, share in its prosperity
and profits. Therefore, even if for none but
selfish motives, they should recommend their
own Society to all intending assurers. By
this means they may enhance the profits
accruing to their own policies, and at the
same time extend to others the benefits
enjoyed by themselves as members of this
great Society.

The Tramp.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
She laid the stick and her baby on the
grass while she untied the rope that tethered the
calf. The length of the rope separated them.
The cow was near the calf, and both were lying
down. Every day she found a fresh place to
tether it—since tether it she must, for there was
no one to go after it but herself. She had plenty
of time, but then there was baby ; and if the cow
turned on her out on the plains, and she with
baby She was afraid of the cow; she had
been a town girl, only she did not want the cow
to knovv it. She used to run at first when the cow
bellowed its protest against the penning-up of its
calf. This suited the cow, also the calf, but the
woman’s husband was wroth, and called her —the
noun was cur. It was he who forced her to run
and meet the advancing cow, brandishing a stick
and uttering threatening words till the enemy
turned tail and ran. “ That’s the way ! ” the man
said, laughing at her white face. In many things
he was worse than the cow, and she wondered if
the same rule would apply to the man, but she was
not one to provoke skirmishes, even with the cow.
It was early for the calf to go “ to bed ” —nearly
an hour earlier than usual; but she felt so weirdly
lonely. Partly because it was Monday, and her
husband had been home for Saturday night and
Sunday. He had gone off before daylight this
morning ; he was a shearer, and fifteen miles as the
crow flies separated them. She knew of no one
nearer, unless the tramp. Ah ! that was why she
had penned the calf up so early. She feared more
from the look of his eyes, and the gleam of his
teeth,.as he watched her newly-wakened baby beat
its impatient fists upon her covered breasts, than
from the knife that was sheathed in the belt at his
waist.
Her husband, she had told him, was sick. She
always said that when she was alone and a tramp
came—and she had gone in from the kitchen to
the bedroom and asked questions and replied to
them in the best man’s voice she could assume.
But this tramp had walked round and round the
house, and there were cracks in some places, and
after the last time he had asked for tobacco. She
had none to give, and he had grinned, because
there was a broken clay pipe near the wood-heap
where he stood, and if there were a man inside
there ought to have been tobacco. Then he asked
for money, but women in the bush never have
money.
At last he had gone, and she, watching through
the cracks inside, saw him when about a-quarter
of a mile away turn and look back at the house.
Then he went further in the direction that she
would have him go; but he paused again, turned
and looked behind him, and, apparently satisfied,
moved to the left towards the creek. The creek
made a bow round the house, and when he came to
it she lost sight of him. Hours after, watching in-
tently in that direction for signs of smoke, she saw
the man’s dog chasing some sheep that had gone
to the creek for water, and saw it slink back sud-
denly, as if the man had called it.
More than once she thought of taking her
baby and going to her husband, but as yet she had
not set her will against his as with the cow, and
so dared not. Long before nightfall she placed
food in the kitchen, and a big brooch that had
been her mother’s she put upon the table, because,
if the man did come back and robbery were his
object, it was the only thing valuable that she
had. And she left the kitchen door open—wide
open; but this was not wise.
How she fastened the doors inside ! Beside the
bolt in the back one she drove in the steel and the
scissors; against it she piled the stools and the
table. Beside the lock on the front door she
forced the handle of the spade, under the middle
bar, and the blade between the cracks in the floor-
mg boards. Then the prop-stick, cut into lengths,
held the top as the spade held the middle. The
windows were little more than port-holes; she had
nothing to fear through them.
She ate a few mouthfuls of food and drank a
cup of cold milk, for she lighted no fire, and when
night came no candle, but crept with her baby to
bed. J
• * * •
What woke her? The wonder was that
she had slept: she had not meant to, but she was
young, very young. Perhaps the shrinking of the
galvanised roof—yet hardly, that was too usual.
Something had set her heart beating wildly, and
the very air she breathed seemed fraught with
terrible danger, but she lay quite still—only she
put her other arm over her baby. Then she had
both round it, and she prayed: “ Little baby-
little baby—don t wake ! ” 3
She saw one of the open cracks, quite close to
where she lay, darken with a shadow—for the
moon s rays shone on that side. Then a protest-
growl reached her; and she could fancy she
heard the man turn hastily : she plainly heard the
thud of something striking the dog’s ribs, and the
l°ngj flying strides of the animal as it howled and
ran. Still watching, she saw the shadow darken
every crack along the wall: she knew by the
sounds that the man was trying every position
that might help him to see in ; but how much he
saw she could not tell. She thought of doing
many things that might deceive him into the idea
that she was not alone, but the sound of her voice
would wake baby, and, as though that were the
only danger that threatened her, she dreaded it.
If baby cried she felt as if she, in turn, must be-
tray her weakness, and instinctively cry to her
protector fifteen miles away. So she prayed
J-jittiG baby, don t wake ! don*t cry !

he had his boots off, because of the vibration that
his feet caused as he walked along the verandah,
and gauged the width of the little window
in her room and the resistance of the front
door. Then he went to the other end, and
the uncertainty of what he might be doing
was fearful: she had felt safer, far safer, while
he was close, and she could watch and listen.
But now. Oh God ! it was terrible. She
felt she must watch, and again the great fear
of wakening baby assailed her. And there was

A few miles further down the creek a man
kept throwing an old cap into the water. The
dog would bring it out and lay it on the opposite
side from where the man stood, but would not
allow the man to catch him, though it was only to
wash the blood of the sheep from his mouth and
throat, for the sight of blood made the man
tremble.
But the dog also was guilty.
BARBARA BAYNTON.
Chivalry Upside Down.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“E ve n the creed of the outlawed push is cnivalrv
upside down.”—Lawson.
Goetii Wale °* paradoxical sayings.”
Sing hey for the Push and the Pride of the Push,
for Billy the Crimson Pal;
He jabs a ‘ * trap ” in the dexter eye, he ‘ ‘ stoushes ”
his own best gal;
He lands a rock on a “bleedin’ Chow ” and cries
as he cracks his crown—
“ How’s that, Ho-ki, for the Crimson Pal and
Chivalry upside down ! ”
Sing hey for Sikes of the bull-dog jaw, sing hey
for his bludgeon fell,
For his Nancy done to a direful death in the slime
of a London hell;
Sing hey for the folks whose throats he cut, the
dogs he left to drown,
And the motto quaint of that burglar-saint—
Humanity upside down !
Sing hey for the doctored balance-sheet and the
jovial banking crew,
Hurrah for the gay defaulting band and the banner
of I 0 U;
A cheer for the fellows whose bills you back, the
gallants who do you brown
As they troll a lay in a lightsome way of—
Honesty upside down.
Sing hey for the Sweaters who scrimp the girls in
a stifling city den ;
Here’s luck to the Bagnio’s gentle light and the
joys of the Boozing Ken ;
Sing hey for the rake of sixty years and the wench
who tramps the town—
They all mean well in their simple way—Morality
upside down !
Sing hey for Boniface, Beer, and Bung—success to
the Band of Booze ;
A health to the genial Toper Tom and the shakes
that haunt his shoes :
With a red, red rose on his festive nose he’s gay
as a painted clown,
And his creed he’ll sing till the rafters ring—
“ Sobriety upside down ! ”
Sing hey once more for the garrulous gang who
live by a jabb’ring jaw—
The Quacks of Body, the Quacks of Soul, and the
leech-like brood of Law ;
For a lie is a simple, guileless thing if it lurk in a
learned gown,
And may only be (like a paradox free) —a verity
upside down.
N.Q.
P. LUFTIG.

to give them either through the Feeding-bottle or with a spoon. OLDER CHIL-
DREN may eat them without preparation.

Inebriety may be agreeably avoided by
substituting in the case of those who run to bitter
beer syrup of orange and quinine diluted with
water. Confirmed inebriates are also much bene-
fited—especially the whisky variety—by the sub-
stitution for grog of a teaspoonful of nitro-
muriatic acid and a saltspoonful of quinine to a
tumbler of water.
The residents of a N.Q. township lately
filed a complaint of “ idleness and invisibility ”
against their one local policeman, who was not a
standing terror to depredating aboriginals. Being
asked to make their allegations more definite,
their mouthpiece replied—“ Constable Neverthere
is too fond of nursing the woman and the blan-
kets.” Transferred immediately.

THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, is the largest buyer
of opal in Australia, and opal miners will find it
to their advantage to send their opal direct to
him. The firm has a reputation for straight deal-
ing extending over a period of 40 years, which is
a sufficient guarantee to consignors that they will
be honorably treated. H. Newman is also a
buyer of all other Australian gems.*
“One curse of London life,” says a
Bulletin writer, “is the degrading practice of
tipping. When a naked, half-starved Colombo
’rickshaw coolie begs you to give an anna over
and above his legal fare for making himself a
beast of burden, you haven’t the heart to say no ;
but when a hulking, fat, six-foot gross British
flunkey will haunt and dog your footsteps for a
‘tuppenny ’ tip, it gives one a feeling of horror to
see an alleged white man degrading himself to the
level of a nigger. All the big hotels, while
allegedly professing to forbid it ( vide notices in
their weekly bills), really encourage it. The
managements of these places pay Chinamen’s
wages to their servants, and the servants live on
the tips they get. The other day I was talking
to a well-known American authoress and her
brother in a London hotel. Her little boy of
four years or so was playing with an improvised
battledore and shuttlecock—last article made by
his uncle out of a champagne-cork. Presently we
all went upstairs into the ladies’ sitting-room, the
child leaving the toy behind him. In a few
minutes a great, fat, lumbering flunkey pre-
sented himself at the door holding out a
large salver. ‘ What is it ? ’ says the ’Murkan 1
lady’s brother. ‘ The young gentleman has left
his little toy behind him, sir.’ ‘Well, that’s all !
right. ’Tisn’t of much account, anyway.’ Waiter
bows and withdraws—as far as the door —then
says : ‘ I thought the young gentleman might
’ave missed it, sir ; thought I ’ad better bring hit
hup hat once, sir, yessir ’ —then shuffles his feet
and smiles expectantly. ‘All right,’ said the
’Murkan gentleman carelessly, ‘ much obliged.’
‘Yessir, thank you, sir; thought the young
gentleman better ’ave ’ ‘ Oh, get away, do,’
broke in the lady impatiently ; ‘ but, say, Henry,
just give him two pennies. It’s the custom here.
Maybe he wants to buy a bun .’ ”
Nervous sufferers will find it worth their
while to peruse Herr Rassmussen’s advt. on page
41 of this issue.*

MADAME’S List of UNIQUE BOOKS on Application.
Farewell.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
How long before the weary years
Will give my darling back to me,
Thro’ what dark mists of burning tears
And buried hopes our ways may be ?
I know not, but I fain would tell
All that I mean by this farewell.
I wonder will you ever turn
Your face towards the west, and say,
“ Does he, too, watch the sunset burn
From golden glory into grey,
And dream of what he could not tell,
But I might guess from his farewell ? ”
I wonder will you ever dream
Of those Australian afternoons ;
The whisperings from the hidden stream,
The shadows on the still lagoons.
When Love began to”weave his spell
Ere Duty bid me breathe farewell ?
Or will it be that you forget
The love-light of these summer days—
No memories, save a vagfue regret
That ours should be divergent ways ?
Then, sad as is a passing bell,
O’er broken vows is this farewell.
But if, perchance, in days to be
Stern Fortune’s wheel may turn again
And give me right to whisper free
The story of my love and pain,
Then you may listen when I tell
All that I mean by this farewell.
THANE.

A Much-valued Testimonial from one of
Australia’s Most-Prominent Explorers.
Grand Hotel, Spring-st., Melbourne,
March 7th, 1895.
To Hebe Rassmussen,
Proprietor of the Alfaline Remedies. _
Dear Sir, —It gives me great pleasure to inform
you that I have derived great benefit from your
celebrated Alfaline Remedies during my explor-
ing expeditions throughout Australia, and I con-
sider them invaluable to all travellers in the bush,
—Yours very truly, B. BUTTON,
(Formerly a Councillor, Brighton, England).
Witness : A. F. Bekeb, Manager, Grand Hotel,
Spring-street, Melbourne,
Mr. Button writes later: —“The Pills I had
from you were of great benefit to me and others.
In one case, when three hunters and trappers
were travelling about the ranges near my place on
the Rose River, one was taken very ill; his mates
intended taking him in a waggon to Wangaratta
(about 60 miles) next morning to see a doctor. 1
gave him a few of your Pills. # I am pleased to
inform you that having to pass their camp next
day, I found him up and pegging-out his skins
and looking well. This speaks volumes for your
valuable Remedies.”

A Wonderful Cure.
Herr Rassmussen. August, 1894.
Dear Sir, —In the year of 18711 was eight days
in the water sheep-washing, and contracted rheu-
matism and stiffness in the joints, and have tried
all advertised medicines. When I came to Sydney
I had a great difficulty in walking, and went to
the Turkish baths, which did no good. Passing
your window I saw a testimonial from Mr. E.
W. O’Sullivan, M.P., with the same complaint as
I had, which led me to try your Remedies. On
Thursday before Good Eriday I took the first. of
your Pills ; since then I have used 4 boxes Special
Powerful Alfaline Vitality Pills, 3 boxes Special
Powerful Alfaline Rheumatic Pills, with Liver
and Kidney Pills, and free use of your Alfaline
Eucalyptus Oil, and I am now, with the blessing
of God, as well in health as I was 23 years ago.
I can with great confidence recommend your
Remedies to anyone suffering from the same com-
plaint. May you long be spared under God to
be a blessing to suffering humanity.
I remain, dear sir, yours very gratefully,
JOHN KEAY.
Witness : Edward Hardy, proprietor Town
Hall Coffee Palace, 521 George-street.

CATHERINE HAGERMAN.

purchase and treat, in large or small quantities, all classes
of Gold, Silver, Lead, Copper, and other Ores.

modious Assay Offices and Laboratories are now in
course of erection at the works, which will be equipped

ments samples of ores of all classes will be tested for
the guidance of those not familiar with the value of
same.
